The Denial of Possibility and the Future Time Response
Dialectical exchange on the reality of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical argument against the reality of possibility and the philosophers' refutation by appealing to future time.

Core Idea
Possibility is not a property of the present but a relation to the future.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a classical objection to the concept of possibility (imkan): if a thing exists, it cannot accept non-existence at that moment; if it is non-existent, it cannot accept existence. Thus possibility seems unreal. The philosophers respond by shifting the temporal frame: possibility is not about the current state but about a future time. This lesson walks you through the logic of the objection and the response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's dilemma
The objector argues that any thing is either existent or non-existent. In its present state of existence, it does not accept non-existence; in its present state of non-existence, it does not accept existence. So possibility, which requires the ability to be both, is never realized.
This is a classical dilemma.
2
Understand the philosophers' response
The philosophers argue that possibility is not judged relative to the present time but relative to the future. An existent thing now can cease to exist in the future; a non-existent thing can come into existence. Thus the judgment 'possible' refers to a future state.
The response changes the temporal reference.
3
Reconstruct the full dialectical exchange
Write down the objector's claim (possibility is unreal) and the philosophers' claim (possibility is real). Then state the objector's argument as a dilemma and the philosophers' response as a redefinition of the temporal scope.
This helps clarify the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواب: من أنكر الإمكان أصلاً قال: إن الشيء لا يخلو عن الوجود والعدم. فإن كان موجوداً فهو في حال وجوده لا يقبل العدم، فلا يكون له إمكان الوجود والعدم فلا تحقق للإمكان. وإن كان معدوماً فهو في حال عدمه لا يقبل الوجود فلا يكون له الإمكان. ولما امتنع الخلو عن الوجود والعدم، وثبت أن الإمكان لا يتحقق في واحدة من هاتين الحالتين، ثبت امتناع محقق الإمكان. زبدة القول في الممكن: إما أن يكون سبب وجوده خاصاً أو لا. فإن كان وجب حصوله فيكون واجباً لا ممكناً. وإن لم يكن كان وجوده محالاً. والجواب عن هذا الإشكال قديم: أن الإمان لا يحصل للشيء بالنسبة إلى الزمان الذي هو حاصل فيه، بل لا يتحقق إلا بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل. فالموجود في هذا الوقت يمكن أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً في هذا الوقت؟ بل الحكم عليه في هذا الوقت بأنه يمكن أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً إنما هو بالنسبة إلى الزمان الثاني، فيكون الإمكان حاصلاً له بذلك. لا يقال: الإمان أمر إضافي والإضافات لا توجّه إلا عند المضافين، فلو كان الإمان يتحصل له حال بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل لزم حصول الزمانين معاً، أعني كون المستقبل حاصلاً عند الحاصل. لأنا نقول: هذا يلزم لو كان الإمان أمراً ثابتاً في الخارج، وإنما هو أمر اعتباري. ومذهب الجمهور من الفلاسفة أنهم اعترفوا بالإمكان الاستقبالي.
Answer: One who denies possibility entirely says: The thing cannot be free from existence and non-existence. For if it is existent, then in its state of existence it does not accept non-existence, so it has no possibility of existence and non-existence, thus possibility is not realized. And if it is non-existent, then in its state of non-existence it does not accept existence, so it has no possibility. And since freedom from existence and non-existence is impossible, and it is established that possibility is not realized in either of these two states, the impossibility of the reality of possibility is established. The essence of the statement concerning the possible: Either its specific cause of existence is present or not. If it is present, its occurrence becomes necessary, so it is necessary, not possible. If it is not, its existence is impossible. The answer to this difficulty is ancient: That possibility does not belong to a thing with respect to the time in which it exists; rather it is only realized with respect to future time. So the existent at this time can it be existent or non-existent at this time? Rather, the judgment upon it at this time that it can be existent or non-existent is only with respect to the second time, so possibility is attained for it by that. It may not be said: Possibility is a relational thing, and relations are only directed when the two relata are present. For if possibility were to obtain for it a state with respect to future time, it would necessitate the simultaneous existence of both times, meaning that the future be present at the present. For we say: This would follow if possibility were a thing established in external existence, but it is only a mental consideration. And the majority of the philosophers acknowledged future possibility.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange presenting a classical objection to the reality of possibility (imkan) and the philosophers' response. The objection argues that if a thing exists, it cannot accept non-existence, and if it is non-existent, it cannot accept existence; therefore, at any given moment, possibility is unreal. Additionally, the objector argues that if the possible's specific cause exists, its existence becomes necessary, contradicting possibility; if the cause is absent, its existence is impossible. The response distinguishes between present and future time: possibility is not a property of the thing at its current moment but a relation to the future. The judgment 'possible' means that the thing can be existent or non-existent at a future time, not at the present. A further objection states that if possibility is a relation, both relata (the thing and the future time) must coexist simultaneously, which is impossible. The answer is that possibility is not an external real attribute but a mental consideration (i'tibari); thus the relation need not require simultaneous external existence. The author cites the majority of philosophers who affirm future possibility. The argument is self-contained and essential for understanding modal logic in Islamic philosophy.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الوجوب
إضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' response in this passage, when does the judgment of possibility apply to a thing?
A) At the present moment of the thing's existence
B) At a future time relative to the thing's current state
C) At both the present and future simultaneously
D) Only to non-existent things

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a future-oriented relational concept, not a property of the present state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the objector's dilemma in your own words, then restate the philosophers' response using the concept of future time. Use the example of the world's temporal origination before creation.

7 min
Possibility as Mental Consideration: Resolving the Relational Objection
Resolution of relational objection via mental consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how considering possibility as a mental concept (i'tibari) solves the problem of the required coexistence of relata in a relation.

Core Idea
Possibility is not an external real relation but a mental consideration, so it does not require simultaneous existence of both relata.

In the previous lesson, we saw the philosophers' response that possibility is future-oriented. A further objection arose: if possibility is a relation, both the thing and the future time must coexist; but the future does not exist now. In this passage, the author answers that possibility is not an external attribute but an i'tibari (mental) concept. This lesson examines the logic of that answer and its implications for the ontology of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the relational objection
If possibility is a relation between a thing and a future time, then the relation requires both relata to exist simultaneously. But future time does not exist in the present, so the relation cannot be real.
This objection targets the philosophers' response.
2
Analyze the philosophers' rebuttal
The philosophers reply that this objection would hold only if possibility were a real external attribute. But possibility is a mental consideration (i'tibari), meaning it exists in the mind as a concept, not in external reality. Therefore, the relation does not require simultaneous external existence of both relata.
This is a key distinction in Islamic ontology: mental existence vs. external existence.
3
Apply this to a concrete example
Take the statement 'Zayd can die in the future.' The possibility of Zayd's death is a mental judgment that does not require an external relation. The mind relates Zayd's current state to a future state without needing the future to be present.
Use an example from everyday life or from the kalam context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواب: من أنكر الإمكان أصلاً قال: إن الشيء لا يخلو عن الوجود والعدم. فإن كان موجوداً فهو في حال وجوده لا يقبل العدم، فلا يكون له إمكان الوجود والعدم فلا تحقق للإمكان. وإن كان معدوماً فهو في حال عدمه لا يقبل الوجود فلا يكون له الإمكان. ولما امتنع الخلو عن الوجود والعدم، وثبت أن الإمكان لا يتحقق في واحدة من هاتين الحالتين، ثبت امتناع محقق الإمكان. زبدة القول في الممكن: إما أن يكون سبب وجوده خاصاً أو لا. فإن كان وجب حصوله فيكون واجباً لا ممكناً. وإن لم يكن كان وجوده محالاً. والجواب عن هذا الإشكال قديم: أن الإمان لا يحصل للشيء بالنسبة إلى الزمان الذي هو حاصل فيه، بل لا يتحقق إلا بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل. فالموجود في هذا الوقت يمكن أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً في هذا الوقت؟ بل الحكم عليه في هذا الوقت بأنه يمكن أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً إنما هو بالنسبة إلى الزمان الثاني، فيكون الإمكان حاصلاً له بذلك. لا يقال: الإمان أمر إضافي والإضافات لا توجّه إلا عند المضافين، فلو كان الإمان يتحصل له حال بالنسبة إلى الزمان المستقبل لزم حصول الزمانين معاً، أعني كون المستقبل حاصلاً عند الحاصل. لأنا نقول: هذا يلزم لو كان الإمان أمراً ثابتاً في الخارج، وإنما هو أمر اعتباري. ومذهب الجمهور من الفلاسفة أنهم اعترفوا بالإمكان الاستقبالي.
Answer: One who denies possibility entirely says: The thing cannot be free from existence and non-existence. For if it is existent, then in its state of existence it does not accept non-existence, so it has no possibility of existence and non-existence, thus possibility is not realized. And if it is non-existent, then in its state of non-existence it does not accept existence, so it has no possibility. And since freedom from existence and non-existence is impossible, and it is established that possibility is not realized in either of these two states, the impossibility of the reality of possibility is established. The essence of the statement concerning the possible: Either its specific cause of existence is present or not. If it is present, its occurrence becomes necessary, so it is necessary, not possible. If it is not, its existence is impossible. The answer to this difficulty is ancient: That possibility does not belong to a thing with respect to the time in which it exists; rather it is only realized with respect to future time. So the existent at this time can it be existent or non-existent at this time? Rather, the judgment upon it at this time that it can be existent or non-existent is only with respect to the second time, so possibility is attained for it by that. It may not be said: Possibility is a relational thing, and relations are only directed when the two relata are present. For if possibility were to obtain for it a state with respect to future time, it would necessitate the simultaneous existence of both times, meaning that the future be present at the present. For we say: This would follow if possibility were a thing established in external existence, but it is only a mental consideration. And the majority of the philosophers acknowledged future possibility.

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange presenting a classical objection to the reality of possibility (imkan) and the philosophers' response. The objection argues that if a thing exists, it cannot accept non-existence, and if it is non-existent, it cannot accept existence; therefore, at any given moment, possibility is unreal. The answer is that possibility is not an external real attribute but a mental consideration (i'tibari); thus the relation need not require simultaneous external existence. Enumeration of Conditional Compositions
Enumeration of compound types and form counts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six basic types of compound proposition pairings and calculate the number of possible forms for muttasila (9) and munfasila (6).

Core Idea
The number of conditional forms depends on whether the sides are naturally distinguished or not.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates all possible compositions of conditional propositions. This enumeration is crucial for understanding the structure of compound syllogisms in Islamic logic. The key insight is that the antecedent-consequent distinction in conditionals (muttasila) doubles the number of forms when combined with other proposition types, while disjunctives (munfasila) lack this distinction, resulting in fewer forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of compound propositions
The passage distinguishes between conditionals (muttasila) and disjunctives (munfasila). Conditionals have a natural distinction between antecedent and consequent, while disjunctives do not.
2
List the six possible pairings of proposition types
The author gives six ways: two categoricals, two conditionals, two disjunctives, categorical+conditional, categorical+disjunctive, and conditional+disjunctive.
These are the basic building blocks for compound propositions.
3
Apply the antecedent-consequent distinction to asymmetric pairings
For pairings that involve a conditional and another type, the conditional can be first or second (since the antecedent and consequent are distinguished). So each of the three asymmetric pairings yields two orders.
This doubling applies only for muttasila, not for munfasila.
4
Calculate the total forms for muttasila and munfasila
For muttasila: the three homogeneous pairings (1 each) plus the three asymmetric pairings (2 each) gives 3 + 6 = 9 forms. For munfasila: all six pairings yield only one form each, thus 6 forms.
The asymmetry is due to the nature of the conditional.
5
Verify with the author's explicit statement
The author states: 'فالمتصلة إذن يمكن وقوعها على تسعة أوجه والمنفصلة لا تفي إلا على ستة أوجه' (. Thus the conditional can occur in nine ways, and the disjunctive yields only six ways.)
Confirm that your calculation matches the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول الذي تكون القضية فيه مركبة عن لادي جنبيها فإما أن يكون لازما للنقيضين موجيبين معا أو سالبين معا أو لازم الموجب موجبا ولازم السالب سالبا على العكس. ولما كانت من الأيام وحشية لاجرم بن كناها ومن الأحكام العامة لجمع المنفلات أن المقدم فيها لا يمتز عن الثاني بالطبع كافة المتصلة بل بالوضع في تركيب الشرطيات على واحد من المتصلة المنفصل. أما أن تتألف من حملتين أو متصلتين أو منفصلين أو حملية ومتصلة أو حملية ومنفصلة أو متصلة ومنفصلة. وقد عرفت أن المتصلة تميز فيها المقدم عن الثاني فلا جرم كل واحد من الثلاثة الأخيرة يمكن وقوعه في العمل على وجهين. فالمتصلة إذن يمكن وقوعها على تسعة أوجه والمنفصلة لا تفي إلا على ستة أوجه.
And as for the statement in which the proposition is composed of both sides: it is either a necessary consequent of both contradictories, both affirmatively or both negatively, or a necessary consequent of the affirmative as affirmative and of the negative as negative, in the opposite direction. And since they were among the wild affairs, we therefore [introduced] them. Among the general rules for all disjunctives is that the antecedent in them does not differ from the consequent by nature in all conditionals, but by stipulation in the composition of conditionals in one of the ways of the conditional or disjunctive. It is either composed of two categoricals, or two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conditional, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conditional and a disjunctive. And you have known that in the conditional, the antecedent is distinguished from the consequent, so inevitably each of the last three can occur in practice in two ways. Thus the conditional can occur in nine ways, and the disjunctive yields only six ways.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage enumerates the possible compositions of conditional propositions (shartiyya). The author first describes the types of logical relationship between the two sides of a compound proposition: either each side is a necessary consequent of the other's contradictory (both affirmatively or both negatively), or the affirmative side necessitates the affirmative and the negative necessitates the negative. Then he notes that disjunctives (manfasila) have the unique property that the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) are not distinguished by nature; rather, the conditional nature is by convention. The composition can involve six pairings: two categoricals, two conditionals, two disjunctives, a categorical with a conditional, a categorical with a disjunctive, or a conditional with a disjunctive. Since the last three types involve both a conditional and another type, and because conditionals have a distinguished antecedent and consequent, each of these three can appear in two orders (e.g., categorical as antecedent or consequent). Thus, muttasila (conditionals) have 9 possible forms (6 from homogeneous pairings plus 3x1 from asymmetric pairings with two orders each), while munfasila (disjunctives) have only 6 (since they do not have a fixed antecedent/consequent distinction, so the asymmetric pairings do not double).

Key Terms
قضية مركبة
متصل
منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 9
C) 3
D) 12

Key Takeaway
The number of possible forms for a compound conditional proposition is determined by whether the sides are naturally distinguished (muttasila has 9) or not (munfasila has 6).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examples of Muttasila Compositions
Author provides examples for each of the six types of conditional compositions starting from two categoricals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional propositions based on the types of statements that compose them, using examples from Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
A conditional can be built from categoricals, conditionals, or disjunctives in various combinations.

The author provides concrete examples to illustrate each of the six possible compositions for muttasila propositions that were enumerated earlier. These examples show how to form conditionals from different types of premises, demonstrating the flexibility of conditional logic. Understanding these compositions helps in analyzing legal and theological arguments that employ conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two constituent propositions
Look for the 'if' and 'then' clauses in the Arabic example. Each is a proposition that may itself be categorical, conditional, or disjunctive.
For instance, in 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود', the antecedent is 'الشمس طالعة' and the consequent is 'النهار موجود'.
2
Determine the type of each proposition
Classify each as categorical (a simple declarative), conditional (contains 'if...then'), or disjunctive (contains 'either...or').
In the same example, both are categorical. In 'إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فكلما لم يكن النهار موجودا لم تكن الشمس طالعة', the antecedent and consequent are themselves conditionals.
3
Classify the composition type
Based on the types of the two propositions, assign one of the six types: from two categoricals, two conditionals, two disjunctives, categorical-conditional, categorical-disjunctive, or conditional-disjunctive.
The first example is 'from two categoricals' (من حملتين), the second is 'from two conditionals' (من متصلتين).
4
Verify the logical relationship
Check that the antecedent truly implies the consequent in the example. For the contrapositive example, ensure the equivalence holds.
The second example demonstrates the valid form: (P→Q) → (~Q→~P).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما من حملتين: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود. ومن متصلتين: فإنه إن لزم من لزوم قضية لزم من انتفاء اللازم انتفاء الملزوم فالأجام صحيح - قولنا: إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فكلما لم يكن النهار موجودا لم تكن الشمس طالعة. ومن منفصلتين: لانه متى انفصلت طبيعة إلى تمين انفصل عنها التيهما أيصالا مفتم الأسفل مقيم للأعلى. ومن حملية ومتصلة والمقدم الحملة: إن كان هذا علة لذاك فكلما وجد هذا وجد ذلك عليها إن كان كلما وجد هذا وجدذاك فهول ادم لذاك. ومن حملية ومنفصلة: إن كان هذا عددا فهو كان وج واما فردا عليها ان كان هذا الما سَوادَ لَوَل مَا بَيَاضًا فقولون من متصلة ومنفصلة والمتصلة المقدم: ومعناه ان الذي يلزمه لازم ماو ولابد وان يكون بينه وبين نقيض الازمة لمعاندة فتصيح إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فاقا ان تكون الشمس طالعة واما ان يكون النهارُ موجود. واما المتصلة من حملتين: هذا العدد اما زوج وفرد فكانا كان د وحافلتين بوده. ومن متصلتين: فكل مصلين منا فيقي متح تركب المنقضاة منها كقولك ما ان تكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود ولما ان تكون قد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالها وليس الموجود. ومن منفصلتين: وذكروا في مثاله إما أن تكون هذه الحمى اما صفراوية ولما دعوثة وإما ان تكون هذه الحمى لما بلغمية اوسر دلوعة تالك الشخ وهذه قريبة القوة من متصلة واحدة معمولة من ها الأجور لكن التحقيق أن للعفونية تقسيم إلى الحادة والبَابِدَة وَ كل واحد منهما إلى قسمية من حمليّة وسيلة فكل ما كان علة لغيره.
As for from two categoricals: If the sun is shining, then daytime exists. From two conditionals: for if a proposition's being necessary follows, then from the negation of the necessary the negation of that which necessitates it follows; so the expression is sound: 'If whenever the sun is shining then daytime exists, then whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not shining.' From two disjunctives: because when a nature is divided into two, both parts are separated from it, the lower part is the opposite of the higher. From a categorical and a conditional where the categorical is the antecedent: If this is the cause of that, then whenever this exists that exists; otherwise if whenever this exists that exists, then this is the cause of that. From a categorical and a disjunctive: If this is a number, then it is either even or odd; or if this is black, then it is not white. So we say: from a conditional and a disjunctive where the conditional is the antecedent, and its meaning: that which is necessary is a necessary of something, and it must be between it and the opposite of the necessity a contradiction, so it is sound: 'If whenever the sun is shining daytime exists, then either the sun is shining or daytime does not exist.' And as for the conditional from two categoricals: this number is either even or odd, so they are regarded as two extremes. From two conditionals: every two praying men... a composite of the two negative propositions as in your saying: 'It is not the case that whenever the sun is shining daytime exists, and it is not the case that sometimes when the sun is shining daytime does not exist.' From two disjunctives: they mentioned as an example: either this fever is bilious or it is not bilious, or this fever is phlegmatic or it is not phlegmatic, and this is close in strength to a single conditional composed of these parts; but the reality is that [the division] divides into acute and chronic, and each of these into two divisions from a categorical and a middle term, so everything that is a cause of something else.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples of each compositional type enumerated earlier. The author illustrates each with logical statements. For two categoricals: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists' (a standard conditional). For two conditionals: a hypothetical syllogism in the form 'If P then Q, and if not-Q then not-P' – this is the rule of contraposition (modus tollens). For two disjunctives: the example is obscure but likely involves a disjunction of two disjunctions. For categorical and conditional: 'If this is the cause of that, then whenever this exists that exists' – exploring the relationship between causality and constant conjunction. For categorical and disjunctive: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd' – a classic example of a conditional whose consequent is a disjunction. The example for conditional and disjunctive is: 'If whenever the sun is shining daytime exists, then either the sun is shining or daytime does not exist' – this is a tautology (from a material conditional, the disjunction of the antecedent and negation of the consequent always holds). The author also notes that a conditional composed of two conditionals can lead to a 'composite of two negatives' as in 'It is not the case that P implies Q, and it is not the case that not-P implies Q' (an invalid complex, perhaps). The example of two disjunctives involving fevers is given as close to a single conditional. Overall, the passage demonstrates the wide applicability of conditional logic in scientific and everyday reasoning, though the exact clarity is hampered by OCR errors.

Key Terms
لزوم
انتفاء اللازم

Muttasila compositions are formed by combining two propositions of varying types (categorical, conditional, disjunctive) under implication; classifying them is essential for analyzing logical structure in Islamic texts.

Disjunctive Composition from Conditionals
Author shows how a conditional yields a disjunctive and explains their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a conditional proposition into an equivalent disjunctive proposition and verify the logical relationship between them.

Core Idea
A conditional implies a disjunction of its two possibilities: both true or both false.

In Islamic logic, both conditional (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) propositions appear frequently in arguments. This passage reveals a crucial link: a true conditional can be restated as a disjunction that covers the same ground. Understanding this equivalence allows you to transform arguments between forms, a skill useful in juristic reasoning and theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional
Find a statement of the form 'if A then B' (muttasila). In the example: 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists.'
The conditional may be universal, e.g., 'whenever A then B'.
2
Derive the contrapositive
From the conditional, derive its contrapositive: 'if not-B then not-A'. The passage gives: 'if daytime does not exist, then the sun does not rise.'
The contrapositive is logically equivalent to the original conditional.
3
Form the disjunction
Combine the conditional and its contrapositive into a disjunction: '(A and B) or (not-A and not-B)'. For the sun example: 'Either the sun rises and daytime exists, or the sun does not rise and daytime does not exist.'
This disjunction covers all cases consistently with the conditional.
4
Verify the equivalence
Check that the disjunction conveys the same necessary relationship as the original conditional. The author states this disjunction is 'close to a single conditional'.
The equivalence holds when the conditional expresses a necessary connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولنا إن كان كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، فكلما لم يكن النهار موجودا لم تكن الشمس طالعة، هذا من منفصلتين، لأنه متى انفصلت طبيعة إلى قسمين انفصل عنها اللازم. فكان هذا العلة لذاك. وإن كان هذا عدداً فإنه إما زوج وإما فرد. والتحقيق أن المنفصلة قد تركب من متصلتين، كقولك: إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة فالنها رموجود، وإما أن تكون الشمس غير طالعة ولا نهار موجود. وهذا قريب من متصلة واحدة. فانه حتى وجد وجد المعلول في كونه علة، وإن لا توجد المعلول منافاة، فتح أنه إما أن لا تكون طلوع الشمس النهار، وإما أن تكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجود، كل من علية ومنفصلة.
Our statement: 'If whenever the sun rises then daytime exists, then whenever daytime does not exist the sun does not rise' is from two disjunctives, because when a nature separates into two divisions, its necessary consequent separates from it. So this is a cause for that. And if this is a number, then it is either even or odd. The verification is that the disjunctive may be composed from two conditionals, as you say: 'Either the sun rises so daytime exists, or the sun does not rise and daytime does not exist.' And this is close to a single conditional. For even if the effect exists when the cause exists, and the non-existence of the effect is incompatible, then it opens into either the sun not rising with daytime existing, or whenever the sun rises daytime exists, each of causality and disjunction.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between conditional (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) propositions. The author begins with the classic example: 'If whenever the sun rises then daytime exists, then whenever daytime does not exist the sun does not rise.' This is a conditional proposition, but it is shown to be equivalent to a disjunction of two conditionals: either the sun rises and daytime exists, or the sun does not rise and daytime does not exist. The author argues that when a nature is divided into two mutually exclusive parts, the necessary consequent of that nature becomes separate, implying that the conditional implies a disjunctive. For instance, a number is either even or odd, and this corresponds to a disjunctive. The verification (tahqeeq) is that a disjunctive can be composed from two conditionals, as in the sun/daytime example. The passage further clarifies that if A is a cause of B, then the existence of A implies existence of B, and the non-existence of B implies non-existence of A, which yields a disjunction: either not-A or (if A then B). This demonstrates the logical equivalence between conditionals and disjunctives, a key concept in the theory of propositions in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
متصلة
منفصلة
شرطية

C) The conditional 'if sun rises then daytime exists' is equivalent to the disjunctive 'either sun rises or daytime exists'.
D) The conditional 'if sun rises then daytime exists' is equivalent to the disjunctive 'either sun does not rise or daytime does not exist'.

A conditional proposition can be transformed into a disjunctive proposition that captures the same necessary relationship between the two states.

Three Types of Inference in Logic
Author classifies reasoning into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish syllogism, induction, and analogy based on the direction of inference (universal/particular) and identify the logical structure of each.

Core Idea
Arguments move either from universal to particular, particular to universal, or particular to particular.

In this passage, al-Razi provides a foundational classification of all inferences. Understanding this classification is crucial because it determines the certainty of an argument: syllogism yields certainty, induction gives probability unless complete, and analogy is derivative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Find in the Arabic text the three categories: القياس (syllogism), الاستقراء (induction), التمثيل (analogy). Note that each is introduced with a distinct definition.
The passage starts with 'الاستدلالُ ما أن يكونَ بالكُلِّي على الجُزئيِّ وهو القياسُ'.
2
Map the direction of inference
For each type, determine whether the reasoning goes from universal to particular, particular to universal, or from one particular to another.
Syllogism: universal → particular; Induction: particular → universal; Analogy: particular → particular via a shared universal.
3
Understand analogy as a compound
The author states that analogy is 'composed of the first two sections'. This means it implicitly uses induction to extract a universal from particulars, then syllogism to apply it to a new particular.
For example, observing several animals that move their lower jaw (induction) leads to the rule 'all animals move their lower jaw', which is then applied to a new animal (syllogism).
4
Handle inference from universal to universal
The passage explains that if two universals are related by inclusion, the inference is a syllogism; if they share a higher universal, it is analogy; otherwise, no inference is possible.
This shows that the three types cover all possible cases of valid inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلالُ ما أن يكونَ بالكُلِّي على الجُزئيِّ وهو القياسُ، كقولِنا: كلُّ جِسْمٍ مُؤَلَّفٌ وكلُّ مُؤَلَّفٍ مُحْدَثٌ، فقد تَعَرَّفْنا شَوْقَ الحَدَثِ للجِسْمِ من ثُبوتِهِ للمُؤَلَّفِ الذي هو مُنْدَرِجٌ فيه. والثاني: الاستقراءُ، فأنتَ إذا قلتَ: كلُّ حيوانٍ يُحَرِّكُ فَكَّهُ الأسفلَ عندَ المَضْغِ، واستدلَلْتَ عليه بتَصَفُّحِ الحيواناتِ الجُزئيَّةِ، فقد استدلَلْتَ بتلك الجُزئيَّاتِ على الكُلِّيِّ. والثالثُ: التمثيلُ، وإنَّما يَتِمُّ عندَ انْدِراجِهِما في الكُلِّيِّ، وهو بالحقيقةِ مَرْكَبٌ من القِسْمَيْنِ الأوَّلَيْنِ. وأمَّا الاستدلالُ بالكُلِّيِّ على الكُلِّيِّ فهو داخِلٌ فيما ذَكَرْناهُ، لأنَّ أحَدَ الكُلِّيَّيْنِ إن كان داخِلًا في الآخَرِ كان جُزْءًا منه بالقياسِ إليه وهو القياسُ، وإن لم يكن داخِلًا فيه فإمَّا أن يَنْدَرِجَا في كلٍّ واحدٍ وهو التمثيلُ، أو لا يكونُ كذلك وحينئذٍ لا يمكنُ الاستدلالُ بأحَدِهِما على الآخَرِ.
Inference is either from the universal to the particular, which is the syllogism, as in our saying: 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing is originated.' For we have known the attribution of origination to the body from its being established for the composed thing, which is included in it. The second: induction, for when you say: 'Every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing,' and you deduce that by examining particular animals, you have inferred from those particulars to the universal. The third: analogy, and it is only completed when both are included under the universal, and it is actually composed of the first two sections. As for inference from universal to universal, it is included in what we mentioned, because if one of the two universals is included in the other, it is a part relative to it—that is the syllogism. If it is not included in it, then either they are both included in a single universal—that is analogy—or they are not, and then it is impossible to infer one from the other.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage classifies the three main types of argument used in logic. The author begins by defining the first type: the syllogism (qiyās), which is reasoning from a universal premise to a particular conclusion. The example given is the classic syllogism: 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing is originated, therefore every body is originated.' Here, the middle term 'composed' connects the major term 'originated' to the minor term 'body'. The second type is induction (istiqrā'), which goes in the opposite direction: from particular instances to a universal rule. The example: by observing that each particular animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, one concludes that all animals do so. The third type is analogy (tamthīl), which infers from one particular to another based on a shared universal. The author notes that analogy is essentially a combination of the first two: one implicitly uses induction to arrive at the universal and then syllogism to apply it to the new particular. The passage also clarifies that inference from universal to universal is a subcase of syllogism when one universal is included in the other, or of analogy when both fall under a higher universal; otherwise, no inference is possible. This classification is foundational for understanding the logic of demonstration.

Key Terms
القياس
الاستقراء
التمثيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's threefold classification of arguments covers all possible directions of inference between universals and particulars.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Syllogism: Inference from Universal to Particular
Author defines syllogism with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a categorical syllogism, extract its minor, major, and middle terms, and verify its validity using the example from al-Razi.

Core Idea
A syllogism moves from a universal rule to a specific instance through a middle term.

Al-Razi's example of a syllogism is a textbook case: 'Every body is composed; every composed thing is originated; therefore every body is originated.' This is a perfect first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara). Let's dissect its logical anatomy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is the statement that is proved: 'Every body is originated' (كل جسم محدث). It is the proposition that follows from the premises.
In the Arabic, the conclusion is implicitly stated: the author says 'we have known the attribution of origination to the body'.
2
Find the two premises
The premises are: 'Every body is composed' and 'Every composed thing is originated.' They are the starting points from which the conclusion is deduced.
These are explicit in the text: 'كل جسم مؤلف وكل مؤلف محدث'.
3
Label the terms
Minor term (subject of conclusion): 'body' (جسم). Major term (predicate of conclusion): 'originated' (محدث). Middle term (connects the two): 'composed' (مؤلف).
The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
4
Determine figure and validity
This syllogism belongs to the first figure (middle term is subject of major premise and predicate of minor premise). Mood: AAA (all universal affirmatives). Such a syllogism is always valid.
The first figure is the only one that yields certain conclusions directly, without conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلالُ ما أن يكونَ بالكُلِّي على الجُزئيِّ وهو القياسُ، كقولِنا: كلُّ جِسْمٍ مُؤَلَّفٌ وكلُّ مُؤَلَّفٍ مُحْدَثٌ، فقد تَعَرَّفْنا شَوْقَ الحَدَثِ للجِسْمِ من ثُبوتِهِ للمُؤَلَّفِ الذي هو مُنْدَرِجٌ فيه. والثاني: الاستقراءُ، فأنتَ إذا قلتَ: كلُّ حيوانٍ يُحَرِّكُ فَكَّهُ الأسفلَ عندَ المَضْغِ، واستدلَلْتَ عليه بتَصَفُّحِ الحيواناتِ الجُزئيَّةِ، فقد استدلَلْتَ بتلك الجُزئيَّاتِ على الكُلِّيِّ. والثالثُ: التمثيلُ، وإنَّما يَتِمُّ عندَ انْدِراجِهِما في الكُلِّيِّ، وهو بالحقيقةِ مَرْكَبٌ من القِسْمَيْنِ الأوَّلَيْنِ. وأمَّا الاستدلالُ بالكُلِّيِّ على الكُلِّيِّ فهو داخِلٌ فيما ذَكَرْناهُ، لأنَّ أحَدَ الكُلِّيَّيْنِ إن كان داخِلًا في الآخَرِ كان جُزْءًا منه بالقياسِ إليه وهو القياسُ، وإن لم يكن داخِلًا فيه فإمَّا أن يَنْدَرِجَا في كلٍّ واحدٍ وهو التمثيلُ، أو لا يكونُ كذلك وحينئذٍ لا يمكنُ الاستدلالُ بأحَدِهِما على الآخَرِ.
Inference is either from the universal to the particular, which is the syllogism, as in our saying: 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing is originated.' For we have known the attribution of origination to the body from its being established for the composed thing, which is included in it. The second: induction, for when you say: 'Every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing,' and you deduce that by examining particular animals, you have inferred from those particulars to the universal. The third: analogy, and it is only completed when both are included under the universal, and it is actually composed of the first two sections. As for inference from universal to universal, it is included in what we mentioned, because if one of the two universals is included in the other, it is a part relative to it—that is the syllogism. If it is not included in it, then either they are both included in a single universal—that is analogy—or they are not, and then it is impossible to infer one from the other.

This passage classifies the three main types of argument used in logic. Induction and Analogy: Istiqra and Tamthil
Author defines induction and analogy, noting analogy is composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish induction from analogy, and explain how analogy combines induction with syllogism as described by al-Razi.

Core Idea
Induction gathers particulars into a universal; analogy moves from one particular to another via that universal.

After discussing syllogism, al-Razi defines induction as reasoning from particular instances to a universal rule, and analogy as reasoning from one particular to another based on a shared universal. He makes an important observation: analogy is actually a compound of induction and syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of induction
Find in the Arabic: 'والثاني: الاستقراء' and note that it infers a universal from examining many particulars, e.g., 'every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing' after observing many animals.
Induction is probabilistic unless it is complete (covering all particulars).
2
Identify the definition of analogy
Find 'والثالث: التمثيل' and note that it infers from one particular to another because they share a universal. The author says it is completed only when both particulars are 'included under a universal'.
Analogy is used extensively in Islamic jurisprudence (qiyas fiqhi).
3
Understand the compound nature of analogy
The author states that analogy is actually 'composed of the first two sections'. This means: first, induction is used to establish a universal rule from particulars; then, syllogism applies that universal to a new particular.
For example: observing that wine is forbidden and intoxicates (particulars) leads to the universal 'all intoxicants are forbidden'; then from that universal and the fact that nabidh is an intoxicant, we deduce 'nabidh is forbidden' via syllogism.
4
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider the analogical argument: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates. Nabidh also intoxicates, so nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the implicit induction (the universal 'all intoxicants are forbidden' derived from wine) and the syllogism (applying that universal to nabidh).
This shows how tamthil in logic corresponds to qiyas in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلالُ ما أن يكونَ بالكُلِّي على الجُزئيِّ وهو القياسُ، كقولِنا: كلُّ جِسْمٍ مُؤَلَّفٌ وكلُّ مُؤَلَّفٍ مُحْدَثٌ، فقد تَعَرَّفْنا شَوْقَ الحَدَثِ للجِسْمِ من ثُبوتِهِ للمُؤَلَّفِ الذي هو مُنْدَرِجٌ فيه. والثاني: الاستقراءُ، فأنتَ إذا قلتَ: كلُّ حيوانٍ يُحَرِّكُ فَكَّهُ الأسفلَ عندَ المَضْغِ، واستدلَلْتَ عليه بتَصَفُّحِ الحيواناتِ الجُزئيَّةِ، فقد استدلَلْتَ بتلك الجُزئيَّاتِ على الكُلِّيِّ. والثالثُ: التمثيلُ، وإنَّما يَتِمُّ عندَ انْدِراجِهِما في الكُلِّيِّ، وهو بالحقيقةِ مَرْكَبٌ من القِسْمَيْنِ الأوَّلَيْنِ. وأمَّا الاستدلالُ بالكُلِّيِّ على الكُلِّيِّ فهو داخِلٌ فيما ذَكَرْناهُ، لأنَّ أحَدَ الكُلِّيَّيْنِ إن كان داخِلًا في الآخَرِ كان جُزْءًا منه بالقياسِ إليه وهو القياسُ، وإن لم يكن داخِلًا فيه فإمَّا أن يَنْدَرِجَا في كلٍّ واحدٍ وهو التمثيلُ، أو لا يكونُ كذلك وحينئذٍ لا يمكنُ الاستدلالُ بأحَدِهِما على الآخَرِ.
Inference is either from the universal to the particular, which is the syllogism, as in our saying: 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing is originated.' For we have known the attribution of origination to the body from its being established for the composed thing, which is included in it. The second: induction, for when you say: 'Every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing,' and you deduce that by examining particular animals, you have inferred from those particulars to the universal. The third: analogy, and it is only completed when both are included under the universal, and it is actually composed of the first two sections. As for inference from universal to universal, it is included in what we mentioned, because if one of the two universals is included in the other, it is a part relative to it—that is the syllogism. If it is not included in it, then either they are both included in a single universal—that is analogy—or they are not, and then it is impossible to infer one from the other.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage classifies the three main types of argument used in logic. The author begins by defining the first type: the syllogism (qiyās), which is reasoning from a universal premise to a particular conclusion. The example given is the classic syllogism: 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing is originated, therefore every body is originated.' Here, the middle term 'composed' connects the major term 'originated' to the minor term 'body'. The second type is induction (istiqrā'), which goes in the opposite direction: from particular instances to a universal rule. The example: by observing that each particular animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, one concludes that all animals do so. The third type is analogy (tamthīl), which infers from one particular to another based on a shared universal. The author notes that analogy is essentially a combination of the first two: one implicitly uses induction to arrive at the universal and then syllogism to apply it to the new particular. The passage also clarifies that inference from universal to universal is a subcase of syllogism when one universal is included in the other, or of analogy when both fall under a higher universal; otherwise, no inference is possible. This classification is foundational for understanding the logic of demonstration.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
التمثيل
جزئي
كلي

Induction and intuition
Induction and deduction

Key Takeaway
Analogy is a compound inference: it uses induction to extract a universal rule and then syllogism to apply it to a new particular.

The Interaction of Premises in Producing the Conclusion
Author critiques the view that the conclusion proceeds from each premise individually

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical argument about whether the conclusion arises from individual premises or the whole set, and identify the fallacy in thinking each premise alone suffices.

Core Idea
The conclusion emerges from the ordered combination of premises, not from any single premise alone.

In this lesson, we examine a crucial dialectical exchange in logic: how do premises produce the conclusion? The author critiques the view that each premise individually suffices, showing that a mere sum of premises cannot yield a new cognition without a specific order and combination. This foreshadows the concept of the syllogism as a unit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The objector asks whether the conclusion comes from each premise individually or from the whole set. Write down these two options.
The objector thinks both are problematic, setting up the dialectic.
2
Understand the refutation of individual premises
If each premise individually produced the conclusion, then each premise would itself be a conclusion—a contradiction. Why? Because a premise is supposed to lead to a distinct conclusion, not be identical to it.
This shows the absurdity of the first option.
3
Understand the refutation of the whole set
If the whole set produces the conclusion, either a change occurs in each premise when combined (leading to infinite regress) or no change occurs (so no new conclusion). Think about why change leads to regress: if a premise changes when combined, the change itself would need a cause, and so on.
This shows the absurdity of the second option.
4
Synthesize the author's view
The author argues that the conclusion arises from the whole as an ordered combination, not from any component alone. This is the nature of thought (fikr).
The ordered arrangement is essential for the emergence of a new cognition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الذي يلزم من تسليمه تسليم غيره ليس القول الثاني فإن من تلفظ بالمقدمات لا يلزمه التلفظ بالنتيجة بل الأفكار النفسانية والفكر ليس إلا مجموع علوم أو ظنون مرتبة ترتيبا خاصا يلزم من حصولها في الذهن حصول علم أو ظن آخر. ثم قائل أن يشكك فيقول: المقتضي لحصول النتيجة هل هو مجموع تلك العلوم أو كل واحد منها؟ والأول باطل، والثاني باطل. أما الأول فلأن حصول العلم في الذهن يمتنع أن يكون علة لشيء. وأما الثاني فلأنه لو كان كل واحد مستقلا باقتضاء النتيجة لكان كل واحد منها نتيجة، وإن لم يكن مستقلا كان الكلام فيه كالكلام في الأول. فإن كان المقتضي هو المجموع فإما أن يحصل عند اجتماعها تغير ما في كل واحد منها أو لا. فإن حصل تغير فيلزم التسلسل. وإن لم يحصل تغير فلكل واحد منها حكمه عند الانفراد فلا يحصل النتيجة. هذا خلف.
The principle whose admission necessitates the admission of another is not the second statement, for the one who utters the premises is not necessitated to utter the conclusion; rather, the psychological thoughts, and thought is nothing but a collection of sciences or opinions arranged in a specific order, from whose occurrence in the mind the occurrence of another science or opinion necessarily follows. Then an objector may raise a doubt, saying: Is the determinant for the occurrence of the conclusion that whole collection of sciences or each one of them? The first is false, and the second is false. As for the first, because the occurrence of knowledge in the mind cannot be a cause for something else. As for the second, because if each one were independent in necessitating the conclusion, then each one would itself be a conclusion; and if it were not independent, then the discussion about it is the same as the discussion about the first. And if the determinant is the whole, then either when they come together some change occurs in each one of them or not. If a change occurs, then an infinite regress follows. And if no change occurs, then each one has its own judgment when isolated, so the conclusion does not occur. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage engages in a dialectical critique of a mistaken view about how premises produce the conclusion in a syllogism. The author first clarifies that thought (al-fikr) is not merely uttering premises but a psychological composite of cognitions (sciences or opinions) arranged in a specific order, from which another cognition necessarily arises. Then a hypothetical objector (qa'il) raises a challenge: does the conclusion arise from the sum total of the premises or from each premise individually? The author shows both options lead to absurdity. If each premise individually produced the conclusion, then each premise would itself be a conclusion—a contradiction. If the sum total produces it, then either each premise changes upon combination (leading to infinite regress) or nothing changes, in which case the premises remain as they were individually and cannot produce a new conclusion. This refutation shows that the conclusion does not arise mechanically from single premises but from their ordered combination as a whole, which is a key insight into the nature of inference in Islamic logic.

المقتضي

C) Because the conclusion is already known beforehand.
D) Because the second premise is always uncertain.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a syllogism arises from the ordered combination of premises as a whole, not from any single premise individually.

Distinguishing Istithna'i and Iqtirani Syllogisms
Author divides qiyas into istithna'i and iqtirani

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given syllogism is istithna'i or iqtirani based on whether the conclusion is explicitly mentioned in the premises.

Core Idea
In istithna'i the conclusion is stated; in iqtirani it is inferred.

This lesson introduces the primary division of syllogisms in Islamic logic. The key distinction is whether the conclusion appears explicitly in the premises. Understanding this will help you recognize the structure of arguments used in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
The passage defines istithna'i as that in which the conclusion is mentioned (مذكورة) actually, and iqtirani as that in which it is not. Find this definition in the Arabic.
The definition is given by a dichotomy: either the conclusion is present (istithna'i) or not (iqtirani).
2
Examine the istithna'i example
The example: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is not a human; therefore it is not an animal.' Observe that the conclusion 'it is not an animal' is part of the first premise (the consequent of the conditional).
Note that the argument form is denying the antecedent, which is a valid istithna'i form in Islamic logic.
3
Examine the iqtirani example
The example: 'Every body is composite; every composite is temporally originated; therefore every body is temporally originated.' The conclusion is not stated in either premise; it is derived.
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure (Barbara).
4
Apply the definition
Given any syllogism, check if the conclusion is already contained in one of the premises (as part of a conditional or as a component) or not. That determines the type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس إما أن تكون النتيجة مذكورة فيه بالفعل، وهو الاستثنائي، وإلا فلا يكون، وهو الاقتراني. مثال الأول: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان، لكنه ليس إنساناً، فليس بحيوان. مثال الثاني: كل جسم مؤلف، وكل مؤلف محدث، فكل جسم محدث. والاقتراني ينقسم بحسب ما يتركب عنه إلى ما يتركب من الحملات أو المتصلات أو المنفصلات أو الحملات والمتصلات أو الحملات والمنفصلات أو المتصلات والمنفصلات. وأما بحسب الركن فإلى أشكال أربعة، لأن كل قضية فلها جزءان: محكوم به ومحكوم عليه. ثم تعني الشكل الثاني.
Qiyas is either that in which the conclusion is actually mentioned, which is istithna'i, or otherwise it is not, which is iqtirani. Example of the first: If this is a human, then it is an animal, but it is not a human, so it is not an animal. Example of the second: Every body is composite, and every composite is temporally originated, so every body is temporally originated. Iqtirani is divided according to what it is composed of into composed of categorical propositions, conditionals, disjunctives, categoricals and conditionals, categoricals and disjunctives, or conditionals and disjunctives. And according to the extreme term into four figures, because every proposition has two parts: subject and predicate. Then the second figure is meant.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage classifies the different types of qiyas (syllogism). The author begins by distinguishing two main kinds: istithna'i (conditional syllogism) and iqtirani (categorical syllogism). In istithna'i, the conclusion is explicitly stated in the premises; in iqtirani, it is not. The example given for istithna'i is: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is not a human; therefore it is not an animal.' Here the conclusion 'it is not an animal' appears explicitly in the first premise (the consequent). For iqtirani, the example is: 'Every body is composite; every composite is temporally originated; therefore every body is temporally originated.' The conclusion is not stated in either premise. Then the author further divides iqtirani based on the type of propositions that compose it: categorical (حمليات), conditional (متصلات), disjunctive (منفصلات), or combinations thereof. Finally, iqtirani is divided according to the 'extreme terms' (الركن) into four figures. The basis for this is that every proposition has two parts: subject and predicate. The arrangement of these parts in the premises yields different figures. The passage ends by indicating that the next topic is the second figure (الشكل الثاني), implying that a detailed discussion of the figures follows.

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
قياس اقتراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Istithna'i has two premises; iqtirani has three.
D) Istithna'i is used only in Kalam; iqtirani in Fiqh.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is istithna'i if its conclusion appears in the premises; otherwise it is iqtirani.

Subdivisions of Iqtirani by Matter and Form
Author subdivides iqtirani by matter and form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify iqtirani syllogisms based on the types of propositions (categorical, conditional, disjunctive) and by the four figures.

Core Idea
Iqtirani syllogisms vary in composition and structure; understanding these divisions is key to mastering categorical logic.

After distinguishing iqtirani from istithna'i, the author further divides iqtirani by what kinds of propositions compose it and by the arrangement of its terms (figures). This lesson introduces these subdivisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of division by matter
The passage says iqtirani is divided according to what it is composed of (بحسب ما يتركب عنه) into six types: categoricals only, conditionals only, disjunctives only, mixture of categoricals and conditionals, etc. Recognize each type.
2
Recognize the basis of division by form
The passage says iqtirani is divided into four figures according to the extreme term (الركن). Understand that 'extreme term' likely refers to the position of the middle term.
The four figures are standard, though some logicians recognize only three.
3
Relate to the parts of a proposition
Every proposition has subject and predicate. In a categorical syllogism, these form the terms. The arrangement of the middle term gives the figure.
4
Connect to future lessons
The passage ends with 'then the second figure is meant', indicating that detailed discussion of figures will follow. Prepare to learn the conditions of each figure.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Four Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Author defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of a categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term and reduce any syllogism to the first figure using conversion.

Core Idea
The first figure alone yields a certain conclusion directly; all other figures must be reduced to it.

The categorical syllogism is the backbone of logical reasoning in Islamic sciences. Its validity depends on the arrangement of the middle term, which gives rise to four distinct figures. Understanding these figures allows you to check whether a syllogism is valid at a glance and, when needed, transform any valid syllogism into the natural first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In the given syllogism, find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). It links the minor and major terms.
If there is no term appearing exactly twice, the argument is not a simple categorical syllogism.
2
Locate the middle in each premise
For the minor premise (the one that usually contains the subject of the conclusion), check whether the middle term is the subject (mawdu') or the predicate (mahmul). Do the same for the major premise (the one that usually contains the predicate of the conclusion).
The minor premise is often the first premise, but not always; context matters.
3
Match the pattern to one of the four figures
Compare the positions: 
- First figure: middle is predicate in minor, subject in major.
- Second figure: middle is predicate in both.
- Third figure: middle is subject in both.
- Fourth figure: middle is subject in minor, predicate in major.
The first figure is natural because the mind moves from subject to predicate.
4
Test reduction to the first figure
If the syllogism is in the second figure, convert the major premise; if third, convert the minor; if fourth, convert both. After conversion, the syllogism should become a first-figure syllogism, which directly yields the conclusion.
Conversion here refers to exchanging subject and predicate while preserving truth (with appropriate quantifier changes, e.g., 'A is B' becomes 'Some B is A' for universal affirmative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاقتراني ينقسم: أما بحسب ما تركبت منه فإلى ما تركب من الحملات أو المتصلات أو المنفصلات أو الحملات والمتصلات أو الحملات والمنفصلات أو المتصلات والمنفصلات. وأما بحسب الركن الأعلى فإلى أشكال أربعة. وذلك لأن كل قضية لها جزآن: محكوم به ومحكوم عليه. ثم الأوسط إما أن يكون محمولا في الصغرى موضوعا في الكبرى أو بالعكس أو محمولا فيهما أو موضوعا فيهما. فالشكل الأول هو الذي يكون الأوسط فيه محمولا في الصغرى موضوعا في الكبرى، وذلك للترتيب الطبيعي لأن الذهن ينقل من الموضوع إلى المحمول. فإذا كان إنتاجه تاما، وإن عكس كبراه صار الأوسط محمولا في المقدمتين، وهو الشكل الثاني، ولذلك يتد الثاني إلى الأول بعكس الكبرى. وإن عكس صغراه صار الأوسط موضوعا في المقدمتين، وهو الشكل الثالث، ولذلك يتد الثالث إلى الأول بعكس الصغرى. وإن عكست المقدمتان معا صار الأوسط موضوعا في الصغرى محمولا في الكبرى، وهو الشكل الرابع، وهو في غاية البعد عن الطبع لتغير كلتا المقدمتين عن النظر الطبيعي ووقوع الطرفين في الوسط والوسط في الطرفين. وقد اشتركت الأشكال الأربعة في أنه لا قياس من سالبتين ولا عن صغرى سالبة.
The categorical syllogism is divided: according to what it is composed of, into that which is composed of categoricals, conditionals, disjunctives, or categoricals and conditionals, or categoricals and disjunctives, or conditionals and disjunctives. And according to the higher pillar, into four figures. That is because every proposition has two parts: that which is judged of and that which is judged. Then the middle term is either a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major, or the opposite, or a predicate in both, or a subject in both. The first figure is that in which the middle is a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major, and that is according to the natural order because the mind transfers from the subject to the predicate. So if its production is complete, and if its major is converted, the middle becomes a predicate in both premises, and that is the second figure, and therefore the second is reduced to the first by conversion of the major. And if its minor is converted, the middle becomes a subject in both premises, and that is the third figure, and therefore the third is reduced to the first by conversion of the minor. And if both premises are converted together, the middle becomes a subject in the minor and a predicate in the major, and that is the fourth figure, and it is extremely far from nature because both premises are changed from the natural view and the occurrence of the two extremes in the middle and the middle in the extremes. And the four figures share that there is no syllogism from two negatives nor from a negative minor.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism (al-qiyas al-iqtirani). The author first classifies the categorical syllogism in two ways: by the type of premises (categoricals, conditionals, etc.) and by the structure of the middle term, which yields the four figures. The key is the position of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) relative to the minor term (al-asghar) and major term (al-akbar) in the premises. In the first figure, the middle is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major. This is the natural figure because the mind moves from subject to predicate; it directly produces the conclusion. The second figure arises when the middle is predicate in both premises; it reduces to the first by converting the major premise. The third figure has the middle as subject in both premises; it reduces by converting the minor. The fourth figure reverses the natural order, with the middle as subject in the minor and predicate in the major; it is unnatural and rarely used. Finally, the author notes a common rule: no syllogism can have two negative premises or a negative minor premise. For example, in the first figure: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' If we say 'No intoxicant is permissible; but some nabidh is not intoxicant'—that would be invalid. The discussion underscores that logical validity depends on the arrangement of terms and the quality of premises.

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني (al-qiyas al-iqtirani): The categorical syllogism consisting of two premises and a conclusion, where all propositions are categorical or a mix. [Note: In al-Abhari's Isaghuji, iqtirani strictly means categorical; here it includes mixed premises.]
الشكل (al-shakl): Figure: configuration of the middle term in the two premises. [Note: Stable.]
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat): Middle term appearing in both premises but not in the conclusion. [Note: Stable.]
الأصغر (al-asghar): Subject of the conclusion, also subject of minor premise in first figure. [Note: Stable.]
الأكبر (al-akbar): Predicate of the conclusion, also predicate of major premise in first figure. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the categorical syllogism 'All intoxicants are forbidden; all nabidh is intoxicant; therefore all nabidh is forbidden', to which figure does it belong?
A) First figure
B) Second figure
C) Third figure
D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The position of the middle term determines the figure of a categorical syllogism; only the first figure is naturally conclusive; all others must be reduced to it via conversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the argument: 'All sins require repentance; lying is a sin; therefore lying requires repentance.' Identify the figure of this syllogism. Then, using the conversion rules taught in this lesson, show how a second-figure syllogism like 'No forbidden act is permissible; some nabidh is permissible; therefore some nabidh is not forbidden' can be reduced to the first figure.

Conditions for the First Figure
Author states conditions for first figure's production.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why the first figure requires an affirmative minor and a universal major, and test whether a given premise pair meets these conditions.

Core Idea
The first figure's power rests on two conditions: affirmative minor, universal major.

Before we can use the first figure, we must know what makes it work. The author gives two essential conditions: the minor must be affirmative and the major must be universal. This lesson unpacks why these conditions are necessary for a valid conclusion to follow.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of the middle term
The middle term connects the minor and major terms. The judgment on the minor is passed through the middle term. If the middle term is not fully captured (e.g., only some of it is spoken about), the judgment may not transfer to all of the minor.
Example: if major is 'Every B is A', but minor is 'Some C is B', we cannot conclude 'Every C is A'.
2
Why major must be universal
The major premise must be universal because the judgment on the minor depends on the middle term being fully known. If the major is particular, the middle term is not limited enough to guarantee the judgment extends to all of the minor.
Common misunderstanding: a particular major would leave part of the middle term unspecified.
3
Why minor must be affirmative
The minor must be affirmative because a negative minor would be problematic. The author argues that a negative minor is in the power of affirmation, likely meaning that a negative minor can be reinterpreted, but it breaks the flow of the figure.
Check: if minor is negative, the conclusion could be negative but the figure's structure fails.
4
Apply the conditions to a candidate premise pair
Given a pair of propositions, check if minor is affirmative and major is universal. If not, the pair is disqualified from producing a valid first-figure conclusion.
Example: 'No wine is permissible' (universal negative) as minor fails the affirmative condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط الشكل الأول للإنتاج كون الصغرى موجبة، والكبرى كلية. وكون الكبرى كلية لأن الحكم على الأصغر إنما هو بالحكم على الأوسط فلا يتعدى الحكم إلا إذا كان الأوسط محصوراً. وكون الصغرى موجبة لأنها لو كانت سالبة ممكنة انعقد منها لإمكان كونها في قوة الموجبة. ثم نقول: قد عرفت أن القضية إما محصلة أو مهملة أو مخصوصة والمصورات أربع. فلو كانت الصغرى موجبة كلية، نظم إليها أربع كليات وكذا البواقي، فحصل ستة عشر. لكن الصغرى السالبة الكلية والجزئية لا تنتج، فسقط ثمانية. والكبرى الجزئية مستحيلة، فسقط أربعة أخرى، وبقي المنتجة أربعة. فمن موجبتين كليتين تنتج موجبة كلية: كل ج ب، وكل ب أ، ينتج كل ج أ. ومن كلية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى تنتج سالبة كلية: كل ج ب، ولا شيء من ب أ، ينتج لا شيء من ج أ. ومن موجبة كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى تنتج موجبة جزئية: بعض ج ب، وكل ب أ، ينتج بعض ج أ. ومن موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى تنتج سالبة جزئية: بعض ج ب، ولا شيء من ب أ، ينتج ليس كل ج أ. وهذه هي الضروب المنتجة في الشكل الأول. والمهملات والمخصوصات فهي في قوة الجزئيات، فالقياس ينعقد بها لكنه بخلاف القاعدة.
The condition for the production of the first figure is that the minor premise is affirmative and the major premise is universal. The major premise must be universal because the judgment on the minor is only through the judgment on the middle term, so the judgment does not extend unless the middle term is limited. The minor premise must be affirmative because if it were negative, it might be possible, due to its being in the power of affirmation. Then we say: You have known that the proposition is either determinate, or indeterminate, or particular, and the quantified ones are four. So if the minor is an affirmative universal, four universals are paired with it, and similarly for the rest, so sixteen [combinations] result. But the negative universal and negative particular minors do not produce, so eight drop out. And the particular major is impossible, so another four drop out, leaving four productive. So from two affirmative universals, a universal affirmative results: every J is B, and every B is A, therefore every J is A. From a universal minor and a universal negative major, a universal negative results: every J is B, and no B is A, therefore no J is A. From an affirmative universal minor and an affirmative particular major, a particular affirmative results: some J is B, and every B is A, therefore some J is A. From an affirmative particular minor and a universal negative major, a particular negative results: some J is B, and no B is A, therefore not every J is A. These are the productive moods of the first figure. And the indeterminate and particular propositions are in the power of particulars, so a syllogism is formed from them, but it is contrary to the rule.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for the first figure to produce a conclusion and then enumerates its four valid moods. The author begins by stating two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. The major must be universal because the conclusion's judgment passes from the middle term to the minor; if the middle term is not fully encompassed (e.g., only some of it is spoken of), the judgment may not transfer to all of the minor. For example, if we say 'Every A is B, and some B is C', we cannot say 'Every A is C'. The minor must be affirmative because, according to the author, a negative possibility is still in the power of affirmation. Next, he calculates the number of possible premise pairs. There are four quantified propositions: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O). For the first figure, if we consider the minor as a free variable, we have 4 x 4 = 16 combinations. But the minor cannot be negative (E or O) because that violates the condition; so 8 drop. Also the major cannot be particular (I or O) because it must be universal; so another 4 drop, leaving 4. These four are the traditional moods: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. He gives them in a form with variable terms: ج for minor, ب for middle, أ for major. The first mood (Barbara) gives a universal affirmative. The second (Celarent) gives universal negative. The third (Darii) gives particular affirmative. The fourth (Ferio) gives particular negative. Finally, he notes that indefinite and particularized propositions (muhmala and mukhususa) are treated like particulars, so a syllogism can be formed from them but it does not follow the strict rule (perhaps meaning it is not formally valid in the same way).

جزئية
صغرى
كبرى

Key Takeaway
The first figure demands an affirmative minor and a universal major; without these, no valid conclusion can be drawn.

The Four Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author enumerates the four valid moods of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify, construct, and label the four valid moods of the first figure (AAA, EAE, AII, EIO) and verify their validity.

Core Idea
Only four of sixteen possible premise combinations produce conclusions in the first figure.

Now that we know the conditions, the author enumerates exactly which premise pairs work. He calculates 16 possible combinations, then eliminates those that violate the conditions, leaving four. These four moods are the backbone of categorical logic.

Guided Path
1
List the four quantified proposition types
Recall the four types: Universal Affirmative (A), Universal Negative (E), Particular Affirmative (I), Particular Negative (O).
These are the standard forms used in syllogistic logic.
2
Calculate the possible premise pairs
If minor can be any of A/E/I/O and major any of A/E/I/O, there are 4x4 = 16 combinations.
But conditions remove many: minor cannot be negative (E or O), major cannot be particular (I or O).
3
Apply the conditions to filter
Eliminate minors that are E or O (8 combos). Eliminate majors that are I or O (4 from the remaining). The surviving combos are: AA, EA, AI, EO? Wait, check: minor must be A or I? Actually condition only says minor must be affirmative, so minor can be A or I (affirmatives). Major must be universal, so major can be A or E. So possible pairs: minor A & major A, minor A & major E, minor I & major A, minor I & major E. That gives four.
These correspond to AAA, EAE, AII, EIO moods.
4
Examine each mood with an Islamic example
For each of the four, construct a concrete example from fiqh or kalam and verify the conclusion follows necessarily.
Example for AAA: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is intoxicant. Therefore every nabidh is forbidden.'
5
Recognize the variable notation
The author uses ج for minor, ب for middle, أ for major. Learn to translate this notation into concrete terms.
In the source: 'كل ج ب' means 'Every J is B' (minor premise).

The Condition for Productive First-Figure Syllogisms
Author states the condition for productivity in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a first-figure syllogism to be productive: the minor premise must be affirmative and the minor term must fall under the middle term.

Core Idea
Productivity requires the minor premise to be affirmative so the judgment does not exceed the genus of the middle.

The first figure of categorical syllogisms is the most powerful, but only certain premise pairs yield a necessary conclusion. The author lays down a fundamental condition: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the minor term must be encompassed by the middle term. This lesson explains why this condition is necessary for the conclusion to follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions stated
In the Arabic passage, find the phrase 'شرطه لإنتاج كون الصغرى موجبة والأصغر يندرج تحت الأوسط.' This is the condition. Write it down.
2
Understand why the minor must be affirmative
If the minor is negative, e.g., 'No J is B', then even if every B is A, the subject J could be entirely outside B, so no link is established. The condition ensures the minor term is inside the middle term's scope.
3
Check the condition on an example
Take a valid first-figure syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (major), Every nabidh is an intoxicant (minor).' The minor is affirmative. Now try flipping the minor to negative: 'No nabidh is an intoxicant.' Would the conclusion follow? No. Confirm the condition's necessity.
4
Test your understanding
Given a syllogism, check the two conditions: (1) minor affirmative, (2) minor term falls under middle (middle term must be predicate of minor premise in first figure). If either fails, no conclusion is produced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول في الأقيسة البسيطة من المحمولات والكلام على المطلقات الشكل الأول من الضروب الأربعة وشرطه لإنتاج كون الصغرى موجبة والأصغر يندرج تحت الأوسط فلا يتعدى الحكم جنس الأوسط. واعلم أن القضية إما محصورة مهملة أو مخصوصة والضروب أربعة. فإذا كانت الصغرى موجبة كلية نظم إليها أربع كبرى وكذا البواقي فحصل ستة عشر. لكن الصغرى السالبة الكلية والأجزئية لا تنتج فسقطت ثمانية. والكبرى الجزئية لا تنتج فسقطت أربع أخرى. فبقي المنتجة أربعة. فمن موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة كلية: كل ج ب وكل ب أ فكل ج أ. ومن موجبة كلية وسالبة كلية ينتج سالبة كلية: كل ج ب ولا شيء من ب أ فلا شيء من ج أ. ومن موجبتين صغراهما جزئية ينتج جزئية موجبة: بعض ج ب وكل ب أ فبعض ج أ. ومن موجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية ينتج جزئية سالبة: بعض ج ب ولا شيء من ب أ فليس كل ج أ.
The first art: simple syllogisms from categorical propositions and discussion of absolutes. The first figure has four moods. Its condition for production is that the minor premise is affirmative and that the minor term falls under the middle term so that the judgment does not extend beyond the genus of the middle term. Know that a proposition is either quantified or particular, and the moods are four. If the minor is universal affirmative, four majors combine with it, and likewise the rest, resulting in sixteen. But the universal negative minor and particular negative minor do not produce, so eight drop. The particular major does not produce, so four more drop. Thus four remain productive. From two universal affirmatives yields a universal affirmative: Every J is B and every B is A, so every J is A. From universal affirmative and universal negative yields universal negative: Every J is B and no B is A, so no J is A. From two affirmatives with particular minor yields particular affirmative: Some J is B and every B is A, so some J is A. From particular affirmative and universal negative yields particular negative: Some J is B and no B is A, so not every J is A.
