Commentary
This passage enumerates the four valid moods of the first figure in categorical syllogisms. The author begins by stating the condition for any first figure syllogism to be productive: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the minor term must be subsumed under the middle term. Then, assuming propositions are either universal or particular, and considering the four types (affirmative/negative × universal/particular), he combines each minor type with the four major types, yielding sixteen possibilities. He eliminates the eight combinations where the minor is negative (since the condition requires affirmative), and the four where the major is particular (as the first figure requires the major premise to be universal, a rule mentioned implicitly). The remaining four combinations are the classical valid moods: Barbara (AAA-1), Celarent (EAE-1), Darii (AII-1), and Ferio (EIO-1). The author provides the standard notation with terms ج (minor), ب (middle), أ (major). This is a fundamental matn passage that should be memorized.

Key Terms
شكل أول (shakl awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. [Note: No significant shift—this definition is stable across the tradition.]
صغرى (sughra): The minor premise of a syllogism, containing the minor term and the middle term. [Note: Stable term.]
كبرى (kubra): The major premise of a syllogism, containing the major term and the middle term. [Note: Stable.]
نتاج (intaj): The production of a necessary conclusion from the premises; a syllogism is 'intaji' (productive) when its premises entail the conclusion. [Note: Stable.]

Why Only Four Moods? The Combinatorial Elimination
Author explains why only four moods survive elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recreate the elimination process that reduces sixteen premise-combinations to four valid moods in the first figure.

Core Idea
Out of sixteen possible premise-pairs, only four survive: eight are eliminated by the affirmative minor condition, and four more by the universal major requirement.

The author walks through a systematic elimination: start with all possible combinations of minor and major premises, then apply the constraints. This lesson unpacks that reasoning so you can derive the four moods yourself, without memorizing them.

Guided Path
1
List the four proposition types
Propositions are either universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), or particular negative (O). So there are four types. The minor can be any of these, and the major any of these.
2
Compute the total combinations
Since each premise can be one of four types, the total is 4 × 4 = 16 possible pairs of minor and major.
3
Apply the condition: minor must be affirmative
If the minor is negative (E or O), the syllogism is unproductive. So eliminate all pairs where the minor is E or O. That removes 2 (minor types) × 4 (major types) = 8 pairs. Only pairs with minor A or I remain (2 × 4 = 8).
4
Apply the condition: major must be universal
The first figure also requires the major premise to be universal (otherwise the conclusion may not follow). So from the remaining 8, eliminate those where the major is particular (I or O). That removes (2 minor types) × 2 particular majors = 4 pairs. The remaining 4 are the valid moods: AA, EA, AI, EI (with minor affirmative, major universal).
5
Label the four valid moods
The four surviving combos correspond to: A+A (universal affirmative conclusion), E+A (universal negative), A+I (particular affirmative), E+I (particular negative). These are Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول في الأقيسة البسيطة من المحمولات والكلام على المطلقات الشكل الأول من الضروب الأربعة وشرطه لإنتاج كون الصغرى موجبة والأصغر يندرج تحت الأوسط فلا يتعدى الحكم جنس الأوسط. واعلم أن القضية إما محصورة مهملة أو مخصوصة والضروب أربعة. فإذا كانت الصغرى موجبة كلية نظم إليها أربع كبرى وكذا البواقي فحصل ستة عشر. لكن الصغرى السالبة الكلية والأجزئية لا تنتج فسقطت ثمانية. والكبرى الجزئية لا تنتج فسقطت أربع أخرى. فبقي المنتجة أربعة. فمن موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة كلية: كل ج ب وكل ب أ فكل ج أ. ومن موجبة كلية وسالبة كلية ينتج سالبة كلية: كل ج ب ولا شيء من ب أ فلا شيء من ج أ. ومن موجبتين صغراهما جزئية ينتج جزئية موجبة: بعض ج ب وكل ب أ فبعض ج أ. ومن موجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية ينتج جزئية سالبة: بعض ج ب ولا شيء من ب أ فليس كل ج أ.
The first art: simple syllogisms from categorical propositions and discussion of absolutes. The first figure has four moods. Its condition for production is that the minor premise is affirmative and that the minor term falls under the middle term so that the judgment does not extend beyond the genus of the middle term. Know that a proposition is either quantified or particular, and the moods are four. If the minor is universal affirmative, four majors combine with it, and likewise the rest, resulting in sixteen. But the universal negative minor and particular negative minor do not produce, so eight drop. The particular major does not produce, so four more drop. Thus four remain productive. From two universal affirmatives yields a universal affirmative: Every J is B and every B is A, so every J is A. From universal affirmative and universal negative yields universal negative: Every J is B and no B is A, so no J is A. From two affirmatives with particular minor yields particular affirmative: Some J is B and every B is A, so some J is A. From particular affirmative and universal negative yields particular negative: Some J is B and no B is A, so not every J is A.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four valid moods of the first figure in categorical syllogisms. The author begins by stating the condition for any first figure syllogism to be productive: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the minor term must be subsumed under the middle term. Then, assuming propositions are either universal or particular, and considering the four types (affirmative/negative × universal/particular), he combines each minor type with the four major types, yielding sixteen possibilities. He eliminates the eight combinations where the minor is negative (since the condition requires affirmative), and the four where the major is particular (as the first figure requires the major premise to be universal, a rule mentioned implicitly). The remaining four combinations are the classical valid moods: Barbara (AAA-1), Celarent (EAE-1), Darii (AII-1), and Ferio (EIO-1). The author provides the standard notation with terms ج (minor), ب (middle), أ (major). This is a fundamental matn passage that should be memorized.

Key Terms
شكل أول (shakl awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise. [Note: No significant shift—this definition is stable across the tradition.]
صغرى (sughra): The minor premise of a syllogism, containing the minor term and the middle term. [Note: Stable term.]
كبرى (kubra): The major premise of a syllogism, containing the major term and the middle term. [Note: Stable.]
نتاج (intaj): The production of a necessary conclusion from the premises; a syllogism is 'intaji' (productive) when its premises entail the conclusion. [Note: Stable.]

The first figure's four productive moods are the only survivors after imposing the conditions of affirmative minor and universal major.

The Four Valid Moods of the First Figure
Author lists the four productive moods with their patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recite each of the four valid moods of the first figure using Arabic variables (أ، ب، ج) and identify the conclusion type.

Core Idea
Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio — these four moods cover all valid first-figure deductions.

Having established that only four combinations are viable, the author now presents each mood with its distinct conclusion pattern. This lesson provides the notations and examples so you can use them in your own reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the four moods in Arabic
The passage lists four moods: 1) من موجبتين كليتين → موجبة كلية. 2) من موجبة كلية وسالبة كلية → سالبة كلية. 3) من موجبتين صغراهما جزئية → جزئية موجبة. 4) من موجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية → سالبة جزئية. Write them down.
The mood names are not in the text, but the patterns are.
2
Learn the standard Latin names
These correspond to: AAA-1 (Barbara), EAE-1 (Celarent), AII-1 (Darii), EIO-1 (Ferio). Associating the Latin names helps in modern discussions.
3
Understand the variable notation
The author uses ج (ج) for the minor term, ب (ب) for the middle term, and أ (أ) for the major term. For example: 'كل ج ب و كل ب أ فكل ج أ' means 'Every J is B, every B is A, so every J is A.'
Recognize that the order is minor premise first, then major premise, then conclusion.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the Barbara mood: Every intoxicant (ب) is forbidden (أ). Every nabidh (ج) is an intoxicant (ب). Therefore every nabidh (ج) is forbidden (أ). Write this in Arabic: كل مسكر حرام، كل نبيذ مسكر، فكل نبيذ حرام. That matches the pattern.
Ensure the middle term (مسكر) is subject in the major and predicate in the minor.
5
Test all four moods
For each mood, construct one valid syllogism using Islamic terms (e.g., from fiqh, aqida, Arabic grammar). Write in the ج ب أ format.

Four Relations of Major to Middle in First Figure
Author lists four relations of major to middle in first figure moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the major term is more general, equal, more specific, or partially overlapping with the middle term in a first-figure syllogism and assess the validity implications.

Core Idea
In the first figure, the major can relate to the middle in four ways—all valid.

The first figure of categorical syllogisms is the most perfect, but its validity also depends on the conceptual relation between the major and middle terms. The author expands on the four productive moods by showing that the major may be more general, equal, more specific, or partially overlapping with the middle. Understanding these relations helps you analyze real arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four productive moods
List the four valid moods of the first figure: Barbara (AAA-1), Celarent (EAE-1), Darii (AII-1), Ferio (EIO-1). Each has a different quantifier combination.
These are the only moods that yield a valid conclusion directly.
2
Identify the four relations between major and middle
The author states that the major (al-akbar) can be: (1) more general (a'amm) than the middle, (2) equal (musāwī) to the middle, (3) more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the middle, or (4) partially overlapping (min wajh). These refer to the extension of the terms.
These relations affect the distribution of terms but do not invalidate the syllogism.
3
Analyze each mood's possibilities
For each mood, the author claims that the four relations can occur. For example, in the third mood (Darii), if the minor premise is true universally, the major may be more general than the middle from one aspect (like 'human' and 'white').
The passage's examples are corrupt but the principle is clear.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take a first-figure syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Some nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore some nabidh is haram.' Here, the major 'haram' is more general than the middle 'intoxicant' because there are other haram things. Identify the relation and confirm validity.
This example fits Darii and shows a 'more general' relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا حَت وَهُوَ أَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْأَضْرَابِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ يَتَّسِعُ وُجُوهًا مُخْتَلِفَةً لِوُجُوهِ اعْتِبَارِ حُدُودِهَا فِي الْعُلُومِ وَالْخُصُوصِ. أَمَّا الْأَوَّلُ فَلِأَنَّ الْأَكْبَرَ قَدْ يَكُونُ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الْأَوْسَطِ وَقَدْ يَكُونُ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ وَقَدْ يَكُونُ أَخَصَّ مِنْهُ وَقَدْ يَكُونُ مِنْ وَجْهٍ. وَمِثَالُ الْمَسْلُوبِ عَنْ كُلِّ الْأَوْسَطِ وَعَنْ كُلِّ الْأَصْغَرِ الْبَحْرُ بِالْبَيْنِ إِلَى الْإِنْسَانِ الْأَوْسَطِ. وَقَدْ يَكُونُ شَاوِيًا لَهُ وَالْأَوْسَطُ قِسْمٌ مِنْ أُمُورٍ. وَقَدْ يَكُونُ مِنْ وَجْهٍ. فَكُلُّ ضَرْبٍ يَقَعُ عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَوْجُهٍ. وَأَمَّا الثَّالِثُ فَالصُّغْرَى الْجُزْئِيَّةُ إِنْ صَدَقَتْ كُلِّيَّةً كَانَ الْأَمْرُ وَالْأَكْبَرُ أَعَمَّ مِنَ الْأَوْسَطِ مِنْ وَجْهٍ كَالْإِنْسَانِ وَالْأَبْيَضِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ كَاللَّوْنِ وَالْأَبْيَضِ. وَالْكُبْرَى عَلَى وَجْهَيْنِ. فَهَذَا الضَّرْبُ أَيْضًا عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَوْجُهٍ. وَأَمَّا الرَّابِعُ فَالصُّغْرَى الْجُزْئِيَّةُ مُحْتَمِلَةٌ لِلْوَجْهَيْنِ. فَالْأَكْبَرُ الْمَسْلُوبُ عَنْ كُلِّ الْأَوْسَطِ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَسْلُوبًا عَنْ كُلِّ الْأَصْغَرِ فِي الْجَلْيَةِ وَمُخَالَطِي. وَوُقُوعُ هَذَا الضَّرْبِ أَيْضًا عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَوْجُهٍ.
Then here is a limit: that each of these four moods extends to different aspects due to the considerations of their boundaries in the sciences and particularities. As for the first, because the major may be more general than the middle, or equal to it, or more specific than it, or related in some aspect. An example of what is negated from all middle and from all minor: the ocean by the bay to the human middle. And it may be equal to it, and the middle is a division of things. And it may be from one aspect. So each mood occurs in four aspects. As for the third, the particular minor—if it is true universally, then the matter and the major are more general than the middle in one aspect, like 'human' and 'white'; and if not, like 'color' and 'white'. And the major is in two aspects. So this mood also has four aspects. As for the fourth, the particular minor is probable for the two aspects. The major negated from all middle must be negated from all minor in clarity and mixture. This mood also occurs in four aspects.

Commentary
This passage expands on the four productive moods of the first figure by considering the logical relationship between the major term and the middle term. For each of the four moods, the author notes that the major term can relate to the middle in four ways: (1) the major is more general than the middle, (2) they are equal in extension, (3) the major is more specific, or (4) they overlap partially (min wajh). He then provides an example (though corrupt) and explains that each of these possibilities yields valid syllogisms as long as the middle term condition is satisfied. The author then applies this to the particular moods: for Darii (third mood), he discusses the cases where the minor premise is true universally (which may affect the relation), and for Ferio (fourth mood), he emphasizes that the major's negation of all middle must carry to the minor. The passage shows the subtlety of the first figure's validity conditions, moving beyond mere mood to consider the ontological relations of terms. This is a characteristic expansion of the logical doctrine found in advanced texts like al-Razi's Mulakhkhas.

Three Interpretations of the Middle Term in the First Mood
Author refutes two interpretations of the middle term in the first mood of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute two incorrect interpretations of the middle term in the first mood of the first figure, and apply the correct interpretation to syllogisms from Kalam.

Core Idea
The middle term cannot be the subject's essence nor a mere predicate—it must be a distinct linking term.

The first mood of the first figure is the most powerful syllogistic form, but its validity depends on the correct role of the middle term. In this passage, the author dissects three possible meanings of the middle term, rejecting two as logically unsound. Understanding this refinement is crucial for constructing valid demonstrations in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three interpretations
The author lists three possibilities for what the middle term (ج) represents: (1) the very essence of the subject, (2) a predicate of the subject, or (3) a third distinct meaning. Write down each interpretation in your own words.
The third interpretation is not fully explained due to OCR corruption.
2
Analyze the refutation of the first interpretation
The author argues that if the middle term is the subject's essence, then the syllogism becomes circular—proving the subject by itself. This defeats the purpose of demonstration, which is to establish a predicate for a subject. Consider why this would invalidate the syllogism.
Example: 'Every B is A and every A is B' would be trivial.
3
Analyze the refutation of the second interpretation
If the middle term is merely a predicate of the subject, transitivity of predication fails. The author explains that the middle term has a distinct reality from the minor, so 'minor is described by middle' and 'middle is described by major' does not imply 'minor is described by major'. Test this with an example from the text.
Example: 'Man is rational' and 'Rational is a genus' does not make 'Man a genus'.
4
Examine the third interpretation and the example
The author hints at a third meaning, then illustrates with a problematic example: 'Every man is rational and every rational is man' leads to the false conclusion 'Man is a genus'. This shows that mere mutual predication does not yield a valid syllogism. Reflect on why the third interpretation must preserve the distinctness of the middle term.
The correct view is that the middle term must be a common term that is truly universal and not identical to either extreme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الضرب الأول من هذا الشكل الذي سواحل هذه الأقلية وجلاءها شكوكاً. فإذاً قلنا: كروب. فإما أن يراد به أن الجيم هو الذات نفسه، أو موصوف به، أو معنى ثالث. والأول باطل: أما أولاً فلأننا نعلم أن حقيقة الجم ليست حقيقة الموصوف بمعنى تقاد المفهوم في قولنا: كل جو مؤلف. وأنا كانت أفلاس الغرض من هذه الأقلية الاستدلال على إثبات الموضوع بالمحمول، وإثبات الذات نفسه عن معقول، فضلاً عن أن يكون خطاباً بالحجة. والثاني أيضاً باطل، لأنه يكون معنى القياس حينئذ أن الأصغر موصوف بالأوسط والأوسط موصوف بالأكبر، وذلك لا يقتضي كون الأصغر موصوفاً بالأكبر، لأن حقيقة الأوسط مخالفة لحقيقة الأصغر، فلا يلزم من إثبات الأوسط للأكبر إثبات الأصغر بعينه. نعم يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر من ذا الموصوف للأكبر، لكن لا يجب أن يكون الموصوف بالموصوف بالشيء موصوفاً به. وإن أردت معنى ثالثاً فالقوة النظر فيه. ولكن لمن امتح أن المعطوف بالموصوف بال[؟] موصوف بال[؟]، ولكن القياس الأقل أنا نفتح هذه الفتحة مع هذه المقدمة، فهذا على خلاف إجماع المنطقين، فإنهم اتفقوا على أن قولنا: كلب كل وجب بالذات أن كل جوان مع من إضمار مقدمة أخرى ولكن يسلمنا ذلك. لكن قولنا: الإنسان جوان، والجوان جنس، صادقة، والتتيجة وهي أن الإنسان جنس كاذبة. فقولنا: كل إنسان ناطق وكل ناطق إنسان، مقدمتان حقان، ولكن متى [؟] ناطق، وذلك باطل لأنه يقتضي إثبات الذات بنفسه. والجواب عن الأول: اتحامل هذا القياس يجع الحرف.
Know that the first mood of this figure, which overcomes the difficulties of these premises and clarifies them, has doubts. So we say: 'Krub' (?). It may be intended that the 'Jim' (middle term) is the essence itself, or a predicate of it, or a third meaning. The first is false: first because we know that the reality of 'Jim' is not the reality of the predicate in the sense of the correspondence of the concept in our statement 'Every J is composed'. And the purpose of these premises is reasoning to establish the subject with the predicate, and establishing the essence itself from the intelligible, let alone being a rhetorical argument. The second is also false, because the meaning of the syllogism would then be that the minor is described by the middle and the middle is described by the major, and that does not necessitate the minor being described by the major, because the reality of the middle is different from the reality of the minor, so establishing the middle for the major does not necessitate establishing the minor itself. Yes, it necessitates that the minor is among that which is described by the major, but it is not necessary that what is described by what is described by a thing is itself described by it. And if you intend a third meaning, then force must be considered in it. But for one who tests [it], that which is attached to the described is described by that which [is described], but the lesser syllogism is that we open this gap with this premise, and this is contrary to the consensus of logicians, for they agree that our statement 'Every B is C and every C is A' [yields] 'Every B is A'? Yes, but we concede that. But our statement 'Man is C, and C is a genus' is true, and the conclusion that 'Man is a genus' is false. For our statement 'Every man is rational and every rational is man' are two true premises, but when [we say] 'rational' [it is false], because it necessitates establishing the essence by itself. The answer to the first: the distortion of this syllogism... makes the letter.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of a doubt regarding the middle term in the first mood of the first figure of syllogism. The author presents three possible interpretations of what the middle term (represented by the variable 'ج') represents in such a syllogism. The first interpretation is that the middle term is the very essence of the subject (i.e., identical to it). The author refutes this because if the middle term were identical to the essence, then the syllogism would be proving the subject through itself, which is not the purpose of demonstration, and the premises would be circular. The second interpretation is that the middle term is a predicate of the subject, meaning the minor is described by the middle and the middle is described by the major. The author refutes this because mere predication does not guarantee transitivity: the fact that the minor has the middle as a predicate and the middle has the major as a predicate does not imply that the minor has the major as a predicate, since the realities are distinct. The author then suggests a third meaning, but the text becomes corrupt. The passage then uses an example: 'Every man is rational and every rational is man' leads to the false conclusion that 'Man is a genus', because 'rational' is not a genus. This illustrates that the middle term cannot be merely a predicate. The answer to the first objection is mentioned but the OCR cuts off.

Key Terms
الجيم
الضرب الأول
الذات

B) Because it would make the syllogism circular, proving the subject by itself.
C) Because the middle term must be a universal, not a particular.
D) Because logicians have agreed that the middle term is always a predicate.

Key Takeaway
In the first mood of the first figure, the middle term must be a distinct intermediary, not the subject's essence nor a mere predicate, to ensure a valid demonstration.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Identity Objection to the First Figure
Author presents and refutes an objection to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that the first figure syllogism requires the middle term to be identical to the major term.

Core Idea
The first figure works by mediation, not identity.

A common criticism of the first figure is that it seems to assume that the middle term is essentially the same as the major term. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi refutes this by clarifying the role of the middle term as a bridge, not an identity. In this lesson, you will dissect the objection and understand why the syllogism is valid without any hidden premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The critic claims that in the first figure syllogism (e.g., Every B is A, every C is B, so every C is A), the minor term (C) must be identical to the major term (A), or must be described by A in a way that requires identity, or there is some third meaning. Find this in the passage.
2
Recognize the two invalid interpretations
The author shows that if the minor term were identical to the major, the syllogism would be trivial. If it were merely described by the major, that does not guarantee the conclusion. Understand why each fails.
3
Understand the correct function of the middle term
The middle term (B) is not identical to either extreme. Instead, it serves as a subject in one premise and a predicate in the other, mediating the attribution. The major premise is not about B itself but about everything that has the attribute B.
4
See how the answer resolves the objection
The author explains that the syllogism reduces to a single essence (the major) with two attributes: one known, one unknown. The major premise establishes that whatever has the first attribute has the second, so from the known attribution to the minor, the unknown follows. This bypasses any need for identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الضرب الأول من هذا الشكل الذي سوى هذه الأقيسة [؟] ولجلائها شكوكا. فلذا قلنا: كروب [؟] فإما أن يراد به أن الجيم هو الأكبر نفسه أو موصوف به أو معنى ثالث. والأول باطل. أما أولا فلأنا نعلم أن حقيقة الجيم ليست حقيقة الأكبر بمعنى تقاد [؟] المفهوم في قولنا كل جيم مؤلف [؟] وإن كانت إفلات [؟] الغرض من هذه الأقيسة الاستدلال على اتصاف الموضوع بالمحمول، واتصاف الأكبر نفسه عن معقول [؟] فضلا عن أن يكون خطابا بالحجة. والثاني أيضا باطل لأنه يكون معنى القياس حينئذ أن الأصغر موصوف بالأوسط والأوسط موصوف بالأكبر وذلك لا يقتضي اتصاف الأصغر بالأكبر لأن حقيقة الأوسط مخالفة لحقيقة الأصغر فلا يلزم من اتصاف الأوسط باتصاف الأصغر بعينه، نعم يلزم منه أن يكون الأصغر من حيث هو موصوف بالأكبر لكن لا يجب أن يكون الموصوف بالموصوف بالشيء موصوفا به. وإن أردت معنى ثالثا فالقوة النظر فيه ولكن لمن امتح [؟] أن المعطوف [؟] بالموصوف بالشيء موصوف بالثاني [؟] ولكن القياس الأقلي [؟] أنا نفتح هذه الفتحة مع هذه المقدمة فهذا على خلاف إجماع المنطقيين فاهم [فإنهم] ينفقوا [يتفقون] على أن قولنا كل ب كل ج [؟] بالذات أن كل ج ب [؟] من غير إضمار مقدمة أخرى. ويسلمنا ذلك لكن قولنا الإنسان ج [؟]، الجيم [؟] جنس صادقة والتجة [؟] وهي أن الإنسان جنس كاذبة ولينا [؟] فقولنا كل إنسان ناطق وكل ناطق إنسان مقدمتان حقان ولكن مما [من] عل [؟] ناطق وذلك باطل لأنه يقتضي اتصاف الشيء بنفسه وذا باطل. فالجواب عن الأول: اتحامل [؟] هذا القياس يجع [يجعل] الحرف إلى حرف واحد وهو الأكبر الذات الواحدة إذا كانت لها صفتان وثبوت إحداهما لها معلوم وثبوت الأخرى لها مجهول لكن ثبوتها لكل ما تنت [ثبت] له الصفة الأولى معلوم من تعرف [؟] من هذين الأمرين المعلوم ذلك المعلوم المجهول. خلاف قولنا كل جيم مؤلف [؟] فإن صفة التأليف حاصلة له وقولنا في الكبرى وكل مؤلف محدث، لا نريد به أن الحدوث ثابت للمؤلفة [؟] بل كل ما ثبت له المواقية [التأليف] ثبت له الحدوثية، فالحاصل: الكبرى تطلب مرادها إثبات الأكبر للأوسط بل إثباته لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط. والإشكال المذكور لازم من النقد [؟] من الأول لمن [؟] من الثاني، وهو الجواب عن الشكل الثاني.
Know that the first type of this figure, which includes these syllogisms, [is] for its elucidation doubts. So we said: 'Krub?' [Maybe 'qiyas'?]. Either it is intended that the minor term (الجيم) is the major term itself, or is predicated of it, or a third meaning. The first is invalid. As for the first [reason], we know that the essence of the minor is not the essence of the major in the sense of the concept in our statement 'Every minor is composed', even if the purpose of these syllogisms is to deduce the attribution of the subject to the predicate, and the attribution of the major itself? much less that it be a rhetorical argument. The second is also invalid, because the meaning of the syllogism then would be that the minor is described by the middle and the middle is described by the major, and that does not necessitate the minor being described by the major, because the essence of the middle differs from the essence of the minor, so it does not follow from the attribution of the middle the very attribution of the minor. Yes, it follows from it that the minor, insofar as it is described by the major, but it is not necessary that the thing described by what is described by something is described by that thing. And if you intend a third meaning, then consider it, but for one who tests [?] that the thing described by a thing is described by the second [?], but the syllogism of the few [?] that we open this opening with this premise, that is contrary to the consensus of logicians, for they agree that our statement 'Every B is every J' [?] essentially that every J is B without implying another premise. And we concede that, but our statement 'Man is J' [??], 'J is a genus' is true, and the conclusion that man is a genus is false, and our statement 'Every man is rational and every rational is man' are two true premises, but from them 'rational' [??] follows, and that is invalid because it requires the self-attribution of a thing, which is false. The answer to the first: this syllogism reduces to a single letter, which is the major, the one essence: when it has two attributes, the affirmation of one of them for it is known, and the affirmation of the other for it is unknown, but its affirmation for everything to which the first attribute is attributed is known, so from these two known things the unknown becomes known. Unlike our statement 'Every composed thing is in time', for the attribute of composition belongs to it, and our statement in the major 'Everything composed is originated' does not mean that origination is attributed to composition, but rather that everything to which composition is attributed, origination is attributed. So the result: the major does not intend to affirm the major for the middle, but to affirm it for everything to which the middle is affirmed. And the mentioned objection follows from the first [figure]? from the second? And that is the answer about the second figure.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the first figure syllogism and provides an answer. The author, Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, begins by presenting a doubt about the first figure: some might think that in the syllogism, the minor term (الجيم) must be identical to the major term, or at least be described by it, or there is a third meaning. He refutes the first possibility by noting that the essence of the minor is not the same as the essence of the major; otherwise, the syllogism would be trivial. The second possibility is also invalid because if the syllogism says 'the minor is described by the middle and the middle by the major,' it does not logically follow that the minor is described by the major, since the middle term has its own essence different from the minor. However, it does follow that the minor is described by the major in a certain sense, but not necessarily that the thing described by something described by a thing is itself described by that thing. The author then mentions that some might propose a third meaning, but it is problematic because it would require an additional premise, contrary to the consensus of logicians. The answer given is that the syllogism effectively reduces to a single term, the major, when the major term has two attributes: one known to belong to it, the other unknown. The syllogism works by establishing that whatever has the first attribute has the second, so from the known attribution of the first to the subject, the unknown second follows. For example, if we say 'Every composed thing is originated,' we do not mean origination belongs to composition itself, but to everything that has composition. This resolves the objection and also serves as an answer for the second figure.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
الجيم
الأكبر
الأوسط

Key Takeaway
The first figure is valid because the middle term mediates the relation between subject and predicate without requiring identity or an extra premise.

The Syllogism

Second Figure: Definition and Conditions
defines the second figure and its two conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure of a categorical syllogism and verify whether its premises satisfy the two conditions for validity.

Core Idea
The second figure only yields negative conclusions because the middle term is the predicate in both premises, requiring an opposition in quality.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism is defined by the position of the middle term. Unlike the first figure, where the middle term is subject in one and predicate in the other, here it is the predicate in both premises. This unique arrangement imposes specific conditions for a valid inference. In this lesson, we will explore these conditions and see how they ensure a sound argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. Locate the term that repeats in both premises and check its syntactic role.
If the middle term is the subject in both premises, it is the third figure.
2
Examine the quality of the premises
Determine whether each premise is affirmative or negative. The first condition requires that the two premises differ in quality—one must be affirmative and the other negative.
If both are affirmative or both negative, the syllogism is invalid.
3
Apply the first condition
Check if the premises differ in quality. If they do not, no conclusion follows. If they do, proceed to the second condition.
4
Consider the second condition
The second condition concerns the appropriate choice of the middle term. It must relate the extremes properly to produce a negative conclusion. Due to textual corruption, the exact condition is unclear, but generally the middle term must be such that the attributed contrariety leads to a valid inference.
In classical logic, the second figure only yields negative conclusions.
5
Derive the conclusion
If both conditions are met, the conclusion will be negative (either universal or particular). Verify that the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.
Common valid moods in the second figure: Camestres (AEE), Cesare (EAE), Festino (EIO), Baroco (AOO).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And its reality [lies] in collective [aspect?] to inference by the contrariety of one [thing] to the contrariety of things, therefore it only produces negative [conclusions]? And it has two conditions for production: one is that its two premises differ in quality, for if they both share [the same quality] it does not produce. The second is that the cause... [corrupted example]... and the agreement of contraries shares that each one is contrary to the other, so they share in genus, place, time, etc., and the agreement of the compatible [things]... [further corrupted].

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of the syllogism. In this figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. The author states that due to the nature of contrary attributes, the second figure only yields negative conclusions. He then lists two necessary conditions for a valid syllogism in this figure. First, the two premises must differ in quality—one affirmative and one negative; if both are affirmative or both are negative, no conclusion follows. Second, the cause or middle term must be appropriate. The example given seems to involve 'man' and 'rational' but is too corrupted to parse. The author also discusses the concept of contrariety and agreement between terms, noting that contraries can share a genus, place, or time, and that compatible terms also share something. However, the text is too damaged to extract a coherent teaching on these points.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Second Figure of Syllogism: Definition and Conditions
Defines the second figure and its two conditions for productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical syllogism belongs to the second figure and verify its validity by checking the two conditions.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the middle term is always the predicate.

The second figure of categorical syllogism is a powerful tool for proving that two things are incompatible. Unlike the first figure, where the middle term connects subject and predicate differently, here the middle term serves as the predicate in both premises. The logicians have identified two strict conditions that a second-figure syllogism must meet to produce a valid conclusion. Let us unpack them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In a categorical syllogism, find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the second figure, this middle term must be the predicate (the second part) of both the major and minor premises.
For example, in 'Every believer is just; No non-believer is just', the middle term is 'just', which is predicated of both 'believer' and 'non-believer'.
2
Check the quality of the premises
The two premises must differ in quality: one must be affirmative and the other negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, no conclusion follows.
Example of failure: 'Every human is an animal; Every horse is an animal' – both affirmative, so we cannot conclude anything about human and horse.
3
Check the quantity of the major premise
The major premise (the one containing the major term, which is the predicate of the conclusion) must be universal. If it is particular, the conclusion may not follow.
Example of failure: 'Some animal is white; Every human is an animal' – major premise is particular, so we cannot conclude that 'Some human is white' fails to exclude other possibilities.
4
Test a syllogism for validity
Apply both conditions to a given example. If the syllogism has a middle term that is predicate in both premises, premises differ in quality, and the major premise is universal, then it is a valid second-figure syllogism.
Practice with: 'No eternal being is temporally originated; The world is temporally originated; Therefore the world is not eternal.' Verify each condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السكاك الثاني وهو الذي الأوسط فيه محمولة المقدمتين وحقيقة بالجمعية الى الاستدلال بتعاند الله از مر على تعاند الملومات ولذالك لا ينتج الا الله وله في الأنتاج شرطان احدهما اختلاف مقدمتيه بالكيف فات المشتركات ن : لازم واحد سلنا كان إم إجَابًا قد تكون معانك وقد تكون متوا ٢٠١٠ اشتراك المتعائلات والمتوافقات السلب فلان كُل شكيله نبع الانسان مثال المرافقين الانسان ولانا طبق فان سبب ایجی مثلا گران مشتر کے سینما کے زیر الاولي ولا شي من الفرير المجر وافق الاشي توافق فلابد وان بيشتر كان سبب ما عدا هما عنها و امان الانجاب المتعاندات مشاركة كون كل واحد منها معاندا الآخر فقد تشارك ية الجنس و المحل والزبان وغيرها وكون المتوافقات تكنة ظام و ادل كان الاشتراكة اللوازم مشتركاين المتعائدات والمتعليقات لم يصح ماضر عن المون ومر و الكون في الزمان المجز انتاجه بالانجاب واللي كان ما
The second figure is that in which the middle term is predicated in both premises. Its reality according to the [logicians] is to infer from the mutual contradiction of the two extremes upon the contradiction of the two conclusions. Therefore it only produces [a conclusion] with [conditions]. For production it has two conditions: one is the difference of its two premises in quality; if they are both affirmative or both negative, it does not produce. [...] The second condition is that the major premise be universal; for if it were particular, the major term which is predicated of some of the middle term might be [predicated of] something else [...]
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of the categorical syllogism and its conditions for validity. The author begins by stating that in the second figure, the middle term (al-awsat) is the predicate in both the major and minor premises. For example, if we take the terms 'human', 'animal', and 'substance', the middle term 'animal' is predicated of 'human' in the minor and of 'substance' in the major. The purpose of this figure is to prove the mutual contradiction of the extremes: if one extreme is affirmed of the middle and the other denied, then the conclusion denies one extreme of the other. The author then gives the two essential conditions. First, the two premises must differ in quality: one must be affirmative and the other negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, no conclusion follows. For example, 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every horse is an animal' do not produce a conclusion about human and horse because both are affirmative. Second, the major premise must be universal. If the major is particular, e.g., 'Some animal is white' and 'Every human is an animal', we cannot conclude that every human is white or not, because the major term 'white' is only affirmed of some animals, not all. Thus, a valid second figure syllogism requires a universal major premise and one negative premise.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني
الأوسط
اختلاف الكيف
كبرى كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid second-figure syllogism?
A) Every human is an animal. Every horse is an animal. Therefore no human is a horse.
B) Every believer is just. No non-believer is just. Therefore no non-believer is a believer.
C) Some animal is white. Every human is an animal. Therefore some human is white.
D) No intoxicant is allowed. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore no nabidh is allowed.

Key Takeaway
A valid second-figure syllogism requires the middle term to be predicate in both premises, the premises to differ in quality, and the major premise to be universal.

Modal Restrictions in the Second Figure
Lists prohibited modal propositions and allows temporal exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modal propositions are prohibited in the second figure and explain the temporal exception that allows their use.

Core Idea
The second figure demands fixed incompatibility; modals without it are barred unless time difference ensures it.

Al-Razi's discussion of modal restrictions in the second figure highlights a critical principle: the second figure requires a permanent incompatibility between the two extremes. Propositions that are contingent, temporary, or non-necessary cannot guarantee this, so they are excluded. However, if the two attributes are temporally incompatible—like sleeping and waking—then a valid syllogism can be formed. In this lesson, we will examine the list of prohibited modals and understand the reasoning behind the temporal exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited modals
Look at the list: particular possible, non-necessary existence, non-permanent existence, temporal, spread. These all lack necessity or permanence.
2
Understand the reason for prohibition
The second figure relies on the fact that the two extremes cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time. If the proposition is modal, it may not guarantee this incompatibility.
3
Recognize the temporal exception
The passage states that if the difference is in time, the syllogism is possible. Temporal difference ensures that the two attributes cannot occur simultaneously (e.g., Zayd cannot be asleep and awake at the same moment).
4
Apply to an example
Consider: No sleeping person is awake. Every reciting Quran person is awake. Therefore, no reciting Quran person is asleep. This is a valid second figure (Camestres) using temporal predicates.
This example uses temporally incompatible attributes: sleeping and reciting Quran cannot coexist at the same time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا نعقد القياس في هذا الشكل من المكنات الخاصة والوجودي اللاضرورى والوجود اللاداء والوقتي والمنتشر ولما كان المكن العام و المطلق العام محمد ان يكون كذلك لا هم لم نعقد القياس فاذن لا يعقد الفناس هذا الشكر من هذه القضايا السد را بسطا ولا مختلطا بعضا مع بعض واما اذا كان الاخلاف و التوائم فانه سيتح لان احد الطرفين اد الرحة والاعلام الطرف الآخر وجبت المباينة لا محالة
We do not form the syllogism in this figure from particular possible propositions, non-necessary existence, non-permanent existence, temporal, and spread. And since the general possible and absolute general cannot be so, we do not form the syllogism. So the syllogism is not formed in this figure from these propositions, either simply or mixed with each other. But if the difference is in time, then it is possible, because one of the two extremes is in one time and the other in another time, so mutual exclusion is necessary.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage discusses modal restrictions on the second figure. The author explains that certain types of modal propositions cannot be used in second figure syllogisms. These include: particular possible (al-mumkin al-khāṣṣ), non-necessary existence (al-wujūdī lā-ḍarūrī), non-permanent existence (al-wujūdī lā-da'īm), temporal (al-waqtī), and spread (al-muntashir). The reason is that these propositions do not guarantee a fixed relationship necessary for the syllogism to produce a certain conclusion. The author then says that the general possible (al-mumkin al-ʿāmm) and the absolute general (al-muṭlaq al-ʿāmm) also cannot be used in this figure. However, if the difference between the extremes is temporal—that is, one attribute occurs at one time and the other at another time—then the syllogism can be valid because the two attributes are mutually exclusive in time. The author's point is that the second figure relies on the incompatibility of the two terms, and temporal difference can establish that incompatibility. For example, 'Everything that is asleep is not awake' and 'Everything that is awake is not asleep' would be a valid second figure if the major is universal. The key is that the two predicates (sleeping and waking) are temporally incompatible. Thus, while many modal propositions are excluded, temporal difference provides a basis for production.

Key Terms
المكن الخاص (al-mumkin al-khāṣṣ): A proposition about what is possible in a specific time or instance (particular possibility). [Note: This term may be used differently in modal logic contexts; here it refers to a type of contingent proposition.]
الوجودي اللاضروري (al-wujūdī lā-ḍarūrī): Existence that is not necessary; a contingent affirmative proposition.
الوقتي (al-waqtī): A proposition restricted to a specific time, e.g., 'Zayd is sleeping at noon'.
المباعدة الزمانية (al-mubā'ada al-zamāniyya): Temporal incompatibility: two attributes that cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The second figure bars modals that lack necessity, but temporal difference provides a basis for valid syllogisms.

Conditions and Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates the four productive moods of the second figure after applying the conditions of universal major and quality difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid second-figure categorical syllogism and list its four productive moods.

Core Idea
In the second figure, only four premise combinations satisfy the twin conditions of universal major and opposite quality.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism has the middle term as the predicate in both premises. Without restrictions, there would be sixteen possible premise pairs. But the logicians impose two conditions: the major premise must be universal, and the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This lesson explains why these conditions are necessary and shows how they reduce the possibilities to exactly four productive moods.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of the second figure
In a second-figure syllogism, the middle term is the predicate of both the major and minor premises. For example, if M is the middle term, then the major premise relates the predicate P to M, and the minor relates the subject S to M.
2
Apply the condition of universal major
The major premise must be a universal proposition: either 'All P are M' or 'No P are M'. This eliminates all combinations where the major is particular.
3
Apply the condition of quality difference
One premise must be affirmative and the other negative. This eliminates combinations where both are affirmative or both negative.
4
Identify the four surviving moods
After both filters, only four premise pairs remain. In all four, both premises are universal, and the conclusion is either universal or particular depending on which premise is negative.
The standard moods are Cesare, Camestres, Festino, and Baroco.
5
Test validity using conversion or counterexample
To confirm a second-figure mood is valid, you can convert the major premise and reduce the argument to the first figure, or use the method of counterexample to show that the opposite would lead to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك فقوك اشتراط كلية الكبرى أسقط من الستة عشر ثانية والعناد الاعلاف في الكيف أسقط ارتعة اخرى فتقت القروب المنحة ادلعة فان كليف و الكبرى سالبة نيت سالبة كليه كلب ولا من اب فالله من جو ایان اما بعكس الكبری نے بصیر عانى الأول أو بالتخلف إن كذب لاش من قول صدق بعض حل ولا نے منات فبعض الدرب وكان كل حب هذا الخلفي من كليتن والصغرى سالبة في كلتة سالبة لان من جواب وكلاب فلاش من جول بيان بعكس الصدى ثم جعلها كثرى ثم على التجة وبالخلف المذكور
Thus the condition of the universality of the major cancelled [some] of the sixteen [combinations] and the opposition in quality cancelled four others, so the productive pairs remained four: for if [the premises] are both universal and the major is negative, it yields a universal negative; and if [both universal] and the minor is negative, it yields a universal negative; and if [both universal] and the major is affirmative, it yields a particular; and if [both universal] and the minor is affirmative, it yields a particular. The proof of their productiveness is by conversion of the major to the first [figure] or by counterexample. And if [the universal affirmative major] is false, some particulars are false; and if the particular is false, some universal is false. And every [mood] in this [group] is from two universal [premises] and the minor is negative, in both [cases] it is negative, because if [the minor] is universal affirmative and [the major] is universal negative, no conclusion follows. The proof is by conversion of the minor then making it major then [deriving] the conclusion, and by the mentioned counterexample.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage discusses the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author states that two conditions must be met for a syllogism in this figure to be productive: first, a difference in quality between the premises (one affirmative, one negative), and second, the major premise must be universal. When these conditions are applied to the sixteen possible combinations of quality and quantity, eight are eliminated: four by the universality condition and four by the quality difference. This leaves four productive moods. The author then lists these four moods: both premises universal with major negative yields a universal negative conclusion; both universal with minor negative also yields a universal negative; both universal with major affirmative yields a particular affirmative; both universal with minor affirmative yields a particular negative? Actually the text says 'من كليتن والصغرى سالبة في كلتة سالبة' which indicates both negatives? It is unclear. The general method to prove these moods is by conversion of the major premise to the first figure, or by the method of counterexample (reductio ad impossibile). The author also hints at converting the minor premise to reduce to the fourth figure, but notes that this is problematic because the minor is negative and cannot be simply converted.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thani): The second figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
كلية الكبرى (kulliyyat al-kubra): The condition that the major premise must be a universal proposition (all or none).
العناد في الكيف (al-‘inad fi al-kayf): Opposition in quality, meaning one premise is affirmative and the other negative.
القرائن المنتجة (al-qara'in al-muntija): The productive moods or combinations of premises that yield a valid conclusion.
عكس الكبرى (‘aks al-kubra): Conversion of the major premise, used to reduce a second figure syllogism to the first figure. [Note: In later texts, conversion is also applied to other premises, but here specifically for the major.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A valid second-figure syllogism always has a universal major premise and premises of opposite quality, resulting in exactly four productive moods.

The Fourth Figure and Its Reduction to the Second Figure
Author discusses the fourth figure and its reduction to the second via conversion of minor and swapping premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a fourth-figure categorical syllogism to the second figure by converting the minor premise and swapping premises, and identify the logical issue with al-Ghazali's reduction claim.

Core Idea
The fourth figure reduces to the second, but the conversion method actually proves the fourth, not the second.

This lesson explores the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms, a figure often considered a variant of the first but here related to the second. The author, building on al-Ghazali, explains a reduction method: convert the minor premise and swap premises to obtain the second figure. However, he immediately raises a critical objection: this conversion actually leads back to the fourth figure, not the second. We will unpack these steps and the critique, showing how logical precision is essential in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth figure structure
Recall that in the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate in the major premise and the subject in the minor. For example: All B are A, All C are B implies Some A are C (if the premises are particular or universal).
The fourth figure is often omitted in later Islamic logic but is discussed here.
2
Understand the reduction method
According to al-Ghazali, to reduce the fourth to the second, convert the minor premise (swap subject and predicate) and then swap the two premises. This yields a second-figure syllogism (middle term is predicate in both premises). Verify with an example.
The conversion of the minor must be valid only for certain types (e.g., universal negative).
3
Apply the reduction to a concrete example
Consider a fourth-figure syllogism from Kalam: 'Some contingent things are temporally originated. Every temporally originated thing has a cause. Therefore, some things that have a cause are contingent.' Convert the minor: 'Some temporally originated things are contingent.' Then swap: major becomes minor, minor becomes major. The result: 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause. Some temporally originated things are contingent. Therefore, some things that have a cause are contingent?' Actually this is not a valid second figure. This shows the issue.
The reduction must preserve validity; here the swapped premises do not yield a standard second-figure mood.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author points out that after converting the minor, the resulting syllogism is actually a fourth-figure syllogism again, not a second. This undermines al-Ghazali's claim that the reduction proves the second figure. The conversion itself relies on the properties of the fourth figure.
This critique shows the importance of careful figure analysis in logical theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رضى الله عنه الغزالي: إن مها شرفات عكس الصغرى ما أوده إلى الشكل الرابع. وهذا الشكل الرابع بيّن جعل الكبرى صغرى ثم بعكس النتيجة كما عرفت، فقد عاد الرابع نقض ما على الثاني. وإذا تحقق هذا فأقول: إن قول من قال بأنه بيّن الثاني بعكس الصغرى فيه نظر، فإنه إذا عكس الصغرى ماد هذا الضرب وهذه الضروب مما يبين بالرابع.
He said, may Allah be pleased with him, al-Ghazali: Indeed, the honours of conversion of the minor have brought it to the fourth figure. And this fourth figure is clarified by making the major a minor then converting the conclusion as you know, so the fourth returns to critique of the second. And if this is verified, I say: The statement that it is clarified by conversion of the minor in the second is questionable, because if one converts the minor, this mood and these moods are clarified by the fourth.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between the fourth and second figures of the categorical syllogism. The author references al-Ghazali's view that converting the minor premise leads to the fourth figure. He explains that the fourth figure can be reduced to the second by making the original major premise the minor and then converting the conclusion. However, the author then raises a critical point: the claim that the second figure is proven by conversion of the minor is problematic, because when you convert the minor, those moods are actually proven by the fourth figure, not the second. This shows a careful analysis of the reduction methods between figures.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع
عكس الصغرى

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure reduces to the second by converting the minor and swapping premises, but this reduction is contested because the conversion itself produces a fourth-figure syllogism.

The Third Figure: Definition, Conditions, and Valid Moods
Author defines the third figure, states its conditions, and enumerates its six valid moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure in a categorical syllogism and verify its validity by checking the two required conditions.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises, requiring at least one universal and an affirmative minor.

Now that you have studied the first and second figures, the third figure completes the picture. In this figure, the middle term serves as the subject in both premises, which imposes strict conditions to ensure a valid conclusion. This lesson will walk you through the definition, the two necessary conditions, and the six valid moods that survive them. The passage from al-Mulakhkhas is our guide, even though its examples are partially corrupted.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. Look at any categorical syllogism and identify the term that appears as the subject in both premises. That is the middle term.
If the middle term is not the subject in both, it is not the third figure.
2
Check the first condition (universality)
At least one premise must be universal. If both are particular, the minor and major terms might be in different parts of the middle, and no conclusion follows.
The author warns: without universality, 'composition is not obtained'.
3
Check the second condition (minor affirmative)
The minor premise must be affirmative. If it is negative, the minor term could be completely outside the middle, making a valid conclusion impossible.
The passage gives a garbled example of why negation fails.
4
Enumerate the valid moods
The two conditions reduce the possible moods from 64 to 6. The passage lists six moods. One example: from two universal affirmatives (Darapti): 'Every ج is ب, every ح is ج, so some ح is ب'. Learn to recognize these six moods.
The six moods are standard in the tradition.
5
Verify the proof method
For each mood, the logicians provide a proof by converting one premise to reduce to the first or fourth figure, or by reductio ad absurdum. This shows that the moods are reducible to the evident first figure.
The passage mentions 'by converting the minor until it becomes first or fourth'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعرف الشكل الثالث الذي الاوسط فيه موضوع في الطرفين ولا نتح إلا البحرين وشرط انتاجه إن كون احد المقدمتين كلية والا احتمل ان يكون البعض الذي فيه الاصغر مغاير الذي فيه الاكبر فلا يحصل الالفة وان تكون الصغرى موجبة لان الاصغر قد يخرج عن الاوسط وامتنع ان يكون خارجا عن الاكبر ماننا له كقولنا: كل ناص ل بواد وكل بياض من ق للبصرة الحق ههنا السلب وتحمل ان يكون الخلافية كما اذا قلنا: كل حب وكل يا ضلون واعى هنا الا تحاب لان سلب العام عن بعض الخاص غير محال. واعلم ان كون الصغرى موجبا اسقط ثمانية واختبارات لايكونا جويتين اسقط مين آخرين فبقيت المنتجة ست من توجين كليتين ستة موجبة كلية كل ج وكل ح فبعض حج. بيانه: أنا بعكس الصغرى في الصيد ثالث الاول لو بالمختلف من كليتن والكبرى سالبة لفتح حربية سالبة كل ج ولا من ح فليس كل حج بلسانه. وبيانها بعكس الصغرى حتى نصرة ربع الاول او بالخلف من موجتين والصغرى جرية الفتح حربية موجبة بعض ح وكل ح بعضرت ابيانه بالطريقين المنكرين من حبتين والكرى حربية التي جنبية موجبة كل ح وبعض الضعف ترب انه يعكس الذي وجعلها صغرى ثم فكر المنحة أو بالخلف من موجة جونية صغوى وكلية سالبة كبرى تفتح جنه كي سحنة سالبة بعض حوت
Know the third figure, in which the middle term is subject in both extremes, and there is no production except from two premises. Its production condition is that one of the two premises be universal, otherwise it is possible that the part containing the minor is different from the part containing the major, so composition is not obtained; and that the minor be affirmative, because the minor may be outside the middle and is prevented from being outside the major, as in our saying: 'Every ناص is a بواد and every whiteness is من ق للبصرة'—here negation applies—and it may be opposite as when we say: 'Every حب and every يا ضلون'—here negation is not necessary because negating the universal of some particular is not impossible. Know that making the minor affirmative drops eight [moods], and if they are not both affirmative, it drops another two, so the productive remain six: from two universal affirmatives are six [moods]: 'Every ج is ب, every ح is ج, so some ح is ب'. Its proof: we convert the minor in [؟] third first, or by the different from two universal [premises] and the major is negative, so it yields a negative [conclusion] 'Every ج is not من ح, so not every ح is ج'—its proof by converting the minor until it becomes first or fourth, or by reduction [per impossibile]. From two affirmative [premises] and the minor is particular, [the mood] is 'Some ح is ج, every ح is ج, so some [؟]'—its proof by the two mentioned methods...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism and sets out its conditions for validity. The author explains that in the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. Two conditions are necessary: first, at least one premise must be universal; otherwise, the minor and major terms might not be linked through the middle. Second, the minor premise must be affirmative; otherwise, the minor term might be excluded from the middle. He gives garbled examples to illustrate. Then he claims that requiring the minor to be affirmative eliminates eight possible moods, and requiring not both negative eliminates another two, leaving six valid moods. He then lists these six moods: for example, from two universal affirmatives, we get a particular affirmative conclusion (e.g., 'Every ج is ب, every ح is ج, so some ح is ب'). He briefly mentions methods of proof for each mood, such as converting the minor premise to reduce to the first or fourth figure, or using reductio ad absurdum. The passage is a standard exposition of the third figure's moods.

الوسط
ضروب منتجة

Key Takeaway
The third figure requires the middle term to be the subject in both premises, with at least one universal premise and an affirmative minor premise, yielding six valid moods.

The Third Figure: Conditions and Valid Moods
Definition of third figure with conditions and valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid third-figure categorical syllogisms by applying the two necessary conditions and list the six productive mood combinations.

Core Idea
Third figure needs a universal premise and an affirmative minor to guarantee term connection.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism places the middle term as subject in both premises. Unlike the first figure, it does not always yield a conclusion automatically. Al-Razi explains why a universal premise and an affirmative minor are required, then enumerates the six valid combinations that survive these restrictions. Understanding these conditions helps you recognize when a third-figure argument is sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's figure
Check whether the middle term is the subject in both premises. If so, it is the third figure.
The third figure is defined by the position of the middle term, not by the order of premises.
2
Verify the minor premise is affirmative
The author stresses that the minor premise (containing the subject of the conclusion) must be affirmative. A negative minor would risk the minor term not being included under the middle.
If the minor is negative, the conclusion cannot be drawn in most cases.
3
Verify at least one premise is universal
If both premises are particular, the middle term might not cover a common part, destroying the connection (ulfa).
One universal is sufficient; both can be universal.
4
List the six productive mood combinations
After applying the two conditions, the remaining valid pairs are: (1) two universal affirmatives, (2) two particular affirmatives, (3) universal affirmative + particular affirmative, (4) universal affirmative + universal negative, (5) universal negative + universal affirmative, (6) universal affirmative + particular negative (giving a particular negative conclusion).
These correspond to the well-known moods AAA, AII, IAI, AEO, OAO, EAO? Actually check: In third figure, valid moods are AAA, AII, IAI, EAO, OAO, EIO? The author's list includes a universal affirmative + particular negative giving a particular negative conclusion – that would be AOO (invalid in first figure but valid in third? In third figure, AOO is not valid; the valid moods are AAI, EAO, IAI, AII, OAO, EIO. The author's list seems non-standard. However, the source is corrupt, so we stick to the author's list as given. For pedagogical consistency, we might note the standard list elsewhere. But here we follow the source exactly.

فاعرف الشكل الثالث الذي الأوسط فيه موضوع في الطرفين. وشرط انتاجه أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كلية، وإلا احتمل أن يكون البعض الذي فيه الأصغر مغايرا للذي فيه الأكبر فلا يحصل الإلفة. وأن تكون الصغرى موجبة، لأن الأصغر المائل عن الأوسط إن محتمل أن يكون خارجا عن الأكبر مانعا له، كقولنا: [؟] وكل بياض من [؟] للحق هنا السلب. وتحتمل أن تكون الخلافية كما إذا قلنا: [؟] الكبرى وكل [؟] هنا الاحتياج لأن سلب العام عن بعض الخاص غير لازم. وأما إذا جعلنا التالية كلية كان اللازم سلب الخاص عن بعض العام وقوع [؟]. واعلم أن كون الصغرى موجبة أسقط ثمانية احتمالات، وعدم كونها موجيتين أسقط ثمانية آخرين، فبقيت المنتجة ست: من موجبتين كليتين، وموجبتين جزئيتين، وكلية موجبة وجزئية موجبة، ومن كليتين: كلية موجبة وسالبة كلية، وكلية سالبة وموجبة كلية، ومن كلية موجبة وجزئية سالبة فتح سالبة جزئية. بيان ذلك بعكس الصغرى إلى الأولى، أو بالخلف.
Know the third figure, in which the middle term is subject in both extremes. Its production condition is that one of the two premises be universal, otherwise it is possible that the part containing the minor term is different from that containing the major, so no connection occurs. And that the minor premise be affirmative, because the minor term inclined away from the middle may possibly be outside the major, preventing it, as in our statement: [؟] and every whiteness of [؟] for the truth here is negation. And it may be different as when we say: [؟] the major, and every [؟] here is need because negating the universal from some particular is not necessary. But when we make the consequent universal, the necessary implication is negating the particular from some universal, occurring [؟]. Know that making the minor affirmative eliminates eight possibilities, and not making them both affirmative eliminates another eight, so six productive ones remain: from two universal affirmatives, two particular affirmatives, a universal affirmative and a particular affirmative, from two universals: universal affirmative and universal negative, universal negative and universal affirmative, and from a universal affirmative and a particular negative yielding a particular negative. Its demonstration is by converting the minor to the first figure, or by reductio.

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-thalith) where the middle term (al-awsat) serves as the subject in both premises. The author then states the two necessary conditions for a valid conclusion: first, at least one premise must be universal; second, the minor premise must be affirmative. He explains why these conditions are needed: without universality, the terms might not share a common part; without an affirmative minor, the minor term might not fall under the middle. The author then enumerates the possible premise combinations: starting from all eight combinations of quantity and quality, the two conditions eliminate certain ones, leaving six valid moods. These are listed as pairs: (1) two universal affirmatives, (2) two particular affirmatives, (3) universal affirmative and particular affirmative, (4) universal affirmative and universal negative, (5) universal negative and universal affirmative, and (6) universal affirmative and particular negative giving a particular negative conclusion. The author notes that these can be reduced to the first figure either by conversion of the minor premise or by reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). The explanation uses examples from logic, though the OCR obscures the exact terms.

The Fourth Figure: Definition, Conditions, and Reduction
Definition and conditions of the fourth figure, with reduction method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of a categorical syllogism, state its validity conditions, and reduce it to the first figure.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires one universal affirmative when the other premise is particular negative.

This lesson introduces the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi'), a valid syllogistic form less common than the first three. You will learn its defining structure—the middle term is the subject in the minor premise and the predicate in the major—and the specific conditions that govern its validity. We also explore how to reduce such syllogisms to the first figure for easier evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
In the fourth figure, the middle term (al-awsat) is the subject of the minor preface and the predicate of the major preface. Check the given examples to confirm this pattern.
For instance, in 'Every human is animal, not every sentient being is human', the middle term 'human' is the subject of the minor (human is animal) and the predicate of the major (not every sentient being is human).
2
Apply the condition for particular negative premises
If one premise is a particular negative (saliba juz'iyya), the other must be a universal affirmative (mujiba kulliyya). Two negatives or two particulars produce no conclusion.
The author states this explicitly: 'if we make one of the two premises a particular negative, then the other must be a universal affirmative, because there is no syllogism from two negatives nor from two particulars.'
3
Analyze the two cases presented
The passage discusses two arrangements: when the particular negative is the minor preface, and when it is the major. Work through the examples: first, 'Not every animal is human, every human is animal' (particular negative minor); second, 'Every human is animal, not every sentient being is human' (particular negative major). Notice how the terms are compatible in each case.
Compatibility means the terms are not mutually exclusive; e.g., human is a subset of animal, so the negative particular does not prevent a valid syllogism.
4
Reduce to the first figure
Reduction is done by swapping the prefaces: make the minor the major and vice versa. The resulting first figure must have the middle term as subject in the major and predicate in the minor. However, the particular negative premise cannot serve as the major of the first figure because the first figure requires the major to be universal.
The author says: 'the particular premise cannot be the major of the first (figure).' This means after swapping, if the particular negative becomes the major, the reduction fails; further conversion of the premise may be needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الرابع هو الذي الأوسط فيه موضوع في الصغرى محمول على الكبرى. لا بد من تقديم مقدمات خمس. فأما شروط انتاجه: أنه لا عن استعمال التالية اللازمة فيه. أما إذا جعلنا إحدى المقدمتين سالبة جزئية فلابد وأن تكون الأخرى موجبة كلية، لأنه لا قياس عن سالبتين ولا عن جزئيتين. وأما إن نجعل السالبة الجزئية صغرى أو كبرى: الأول لأن المحمول قد يكون أعم من الموضوع، فإذا سلب عن بعض ذلك المحمول وعمم على كل شيء آخر عن المعتمد أن يكون ذلك المسلوب وذلك الموضوع متوافقين، كقولنا: ليس كل حيوان إنسانا، وكل إنسان حيوان. والثاني لأن اتصلات الشيء إذا حصل على شيء ثم سلب عن بعض شيء آخر فقد يكون ذلك الشيء وذلك الآخر متوافقين، كقولنا: كل إنسان حيوان، وليس كل حساس إنسانا. واما إذا كانتا متباينتين، كقولنا: كل إنسان حيوان، وليس كل حيوان إنسانا. وإنما يكون الرد إلى الأولى بجعل الصغرى كبرى، والتالية الجزئية لا تصلح كبرى الأول.
The fourth figure is the one in which the middle term is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major. It is necessary to present five premises. As for its production conditions: it is not about using the necessary consequence in it. If we make one of the two premises a particular negative, then the other must be a universal affirmative, because there is no syllogism from two negatives nor from two particulars. And if we make the particular negative the minor or the major: the first is because the predicate may be more general than the subject, so if it is negated from some of that predicate and universalized over every other thing, it is necessary that the negated thing and the subject be compatible, as in our statement: 'Not every animal is a human, and every human is an animal.' The second is because the connections of a thing, if they obtain for something, then are negated from some other thing, may be that thing and the other are compatible, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and not every sentient being is a human.' And if they are mutually exclusive, as in our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and not every animal is a human.' Reduction to the first figure is by making the minor the major, and the particular premise cannot be the major of the first (figure).
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-rabi'), where the middle term (al-awsat) is the subject in the minor premise and the predicate in the major. The author states that 'five premises' must be presented (likely a reference to the five universals or a specific classification; the OCR is unclear). The condition for a valid conclusion is that if one premise is a particular negative, then the other must be a universal affirmative, since two negatives or two particular premises cannot yield a conclusion. The author then discusses two cases: when the particular negative is the minor premise and when it is the major. In the first case, the predicate (major term) may be more general than the subject, and negating it from some of that predicate while affirming universally of another requires compatibility. The example: 'Not every animal is a human, and every human is an animal' illustrates that animal is more general, and the negative particular premise does not prevent a valid conclusion because the terms are compatible (humans are a subset of animals). In the second case, the particular negative is the major premise; the connections are such that if one thing is affirmed of something, then negated from another, compatibility is needed. Example: 'Every human is an animal, and not every sentient being is a human' (sentient being is more general). If terms are mutually exclusive, like 'Every human is an animal, and not every animal is a human', still valid. Finally, the author explains that reduction to the first figure is done by swapping the premises (making the minor the major), but the particular negative premise cannot serve as the major of the first figure because of its particularity.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع (al-shakl al-rabi'): The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle is subject in minor and predicate in major. [Note: Stable; some post-Avicennan logicians consider it a variant of the first figure.]
السالبة الجزئية (al-saliba al-juz'iyya): A particular negative proposition (e.g., 'Not every A is B'). [Note: No significant shift.]
رد إلى الأول (radd ila al-awwal): Reduction to the first figure by converting or swapping premises. [Note: Standard technique across the tradition.]

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure has a unique structure where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major; its validity requires that when one premise is particular negative, the other must be universal affirmative, and reduction to the first figure involves swapping premises, with the particular negative unable to serve as the major.
