Valid Premise Pairs in the First Figure
Author enumerates valid premise pairs in first figure and reduction methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise combinations are valid for a first-figure syllogism and explain why others fail.

Core Idea
In the first figure, a negative particular must pair with a universal affirmative; two negatives or two particulars never produce a conclusion.

The first figure is the most perfect syllogistic form in Islamic logic. However, not every pair of premises yields a valid conclusion. The author specifies that if one premise is a negative particular (saliba juz'iyya), the other must be a universal affirmative (mujiba kulliyya). Furthermore, two negatives or two particulars never generate a syllogism. Let us examine these conditions step by step using examples from Islamic knowledge.

Guided Path
1
1. Recall the structure of the first figure
In the first figure, the middle term is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise. For example: 'Every a is b' (minor) and 'Every b is c' (major) yields 'Every a is c'.
2
2. Identify the rule about negative particular premises
If one premise is a negative particular (e.g., 'Not every S is P'), then the other must be a universal affirmative. Two negatives or two particulars are forbidden because they cannot connect the terms properly.
3
3. Apply the rule to given premise pairs
Take the author's example: 'Not every animal is rational' (negative particular) and 'Every human is an animal' (universal affirmative). According to the rule, this pair is allowed. Now test a forbidden pair: 'Some animal is not rational' and 'Some human is an animal' – two particulars yield no conclusion.
The author also warns about incompatible terms that break the syllogism.
4
4. Summarise the valid moods of the first figure
The known valid moods are Barbara (AAA), Celarent (EAE), Darii (AII), and Ferio (EIO). In Islamic logic, these correspond to specific proposition combinations. The conditions here ensure that only these moods are accepted.
The passage presumably aims to reduce all valid moods to these conditions.

Validity Conditions and Moods of the Third Figure
Author enumerates valid premise combinations for the third figure and illustrates with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid premise pairs for the third figure of the categorical syllogism and explain why other combinations are invalid.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises, and only certain premise combinations yield a valid conclusion.

In this lesson, we delve into the specific conditions that determine which premise pairs are valid in the third syllogistic figure. The author of the Mulakhkhas enumerates which combinations of universal and particular, affirmative and negative premises produce a sound syllogism, and which fall into fallacy. By understanding these rules, you will be able to quickly evaluate any third-figure syllogism you encounter in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term (hadd al-awsat) is the subject of both the minor and major premises. This imposes constraints on which quantifier/quality combinations can yield a conclusion.
Standard third figure: M is subject in both premises.
2
Apply the general rules of categorical syllogisms
No syllogism can have two negative premises, nor two particular premises. The third figure also has a special rule: the conclusion must be particular.
These rules are derived from the nature of categorical propositions.
3
Enumerate allowed premise pairs as per the author
If the minor is universal affirmative (A), the major can be A, E, or I. If the minor is universal negative (E), only major A is valid. If the minor is particular affirmative (I), only major E is valid. These correspond to moods Darapti, Felapton, Disamis, Datisi, Bocardo, Ferison.
The author's list is incomplete but covers the key combinations.
4
Test with examples from the text
Reconstruct valid third-figure syllogisms using Islamic examples, e.g., 'Every human is rational, every human is animal, therefore some animal is rational' (Darapti).
The text's examples are corrupt; we use standard ones for clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان كذلك سقطت من الثة عشر ثمانية واما المحموت اللث فنقول إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة كلية امكن جعل الكبرى موجبة كلبة وسالبة كلية وموجة جنية واما اذا كانت سالبة كلية الكن جعد الكبرى موجة كلية فاما السالبة الكلية فلا لانه لا قياس عن سالبين ولا موجة جنية لانه لا قياسَ عَنصُوَى سالبة كلية وكرى جزية واما اذا كانت موجة جونية امكن جعل الكبرى سالبة كلية اما الموجة الجبة فلا لانه لاقتباس من جزين و الموجبة الجلسة فلا امسال فلانه اذا جعل بعض الاوسط موضو عالي وحمل كله علا سے آخرفة اذكر الشيئان قد يكونان متباينين كقولك بعض اللون سعاد وَكُلِّ بَاضٍ لَون والحَقِّ ان من السواد بياض وقد يكونان متوافقين كقولك بعض الحيوان إنسَانَ وَكَل ناطق حيوان و الحق علا ن ناطق وانا انا
And when that is the case, eight of the thirteen drop out. As for the third [figure], we say: if the minor is universal affirmative, it is possible to make the major universal affirmative, universal negative, or particular affirmative. But if it is universal negative, then you can only make the major universal affirmative; as for universal negative, no, because there is no syllogism from two negatives; and not particular affirmative, because there is no syllogism from a universal negative and a particular major. If the minor is particular affirmative, it is possible to make the major universal negative; as for the affirmative particular, no, because there is no syllogism from two particulars; and the universal affirmative? Commentary
This passage enumerates the valid moods of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first notes that out of the thirteen theoretical moods, eight are invalid, leaving five valid ones (though the text is corrupt; standard logic books list six valid moods for the third figure: Darapti, Felapton, Disamis, Datisi, Bocardo, Ferison). The author then explains the possible combinations of premises: if the minor premise is universal affirmative (A), the major can be A, E, or I. If the minor is universal negative (E), the major can only be A (because two negatives are invalid, and E+I yields no conclusion). If the minor is particular affirmative (I), the major can only be E (because I+I is invalid, and I+A is not discussed but would yield I in some accounts; the text seems to reject it). The author provides two examples: one where the middle term ‘black’ is predicated of ‘color’ (exclusive relation?), and one where ‘human’ is predicated of ‘animal’ under ‘rational’. The examples are garbled but illustrate that the extremes may be mutually exclusive or overlapping. This passage is a matn-like enumeration of valid premise pairs; the student must memorize which combinations are allowed and why.

Key Terms
شكل ثالث
صغرى موجبة كلية
كبرى سالبة كلية

Key Takeaway
The third figure's valid premise pairs are determined by the position of the middle term and the general rules of categorical logic; the conclusion is always particular.

Conditions of the Second Figure and Its Conclusions
Author enumerates three conditions for the second figure and their variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of the second figure of categorical syllogism and determine which types of conclusions it can validly produce.

Core Idea
The second figure requires same-quality premises and a particular major; it yields only particular conclusions.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi lays out the conditions specific to the second figure of the categorical syllogism. Understanding these conditions is crucial because they determine what conclusions can be drawn. We will examine why a universal affirmative conclusion is impossible in this figure, and why only particular conclusions are valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for the second figure
The author states two conditions: (1) the two premises must agree in quality (both affirmative or both negative), and (2) the major premise must be particular (juz'iyya). Locate these in the Arabic text.
2
Note the contrasting condition of the third figure
The author mentions that the third figure has only one condition: it is permissible for the minor premise to be negative. This contrast helps highlight the distinct constraints of each figure.
3
List the conclusions the second figure can produce
According to the author, the second figure can produce particular affirmative and particular negative conclusions, but it cannot produce a universal affirmative conclusion. Confirm this in the translation.
4
Analyze why a universal affirmative conclusion is impossible
The author explains that a universal affirmative conclusion requires two universal premises. Since the second figure requires the major premise to be particular, this condition cannot be met. Additionally, the minor term would need to be more specific than the middle term, which is not possible under these constraints.
This is the logical reasoning embedded in the passage.

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author states that the second figure requires two conditions: (1) the two premises must agree in quality (both affirmative or both negative), and (2) the major premise must be particular (juz'iyya). The third figure has only one condition: it is permissible for the minor premise to be negative. The second figure can produce particular affirmative and particular negative conclusions, but it cannot produce a universal affirmative conclusion. This is because a universal affirmative conclusion requires that the minor term be more specific than the middle term, which would then require a universal major premise—but the second figure's major is particular, so universality is impossible. The author then poses a rhetorical question to emphasise the logical constraint.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thāni): The second figure of categorical syllogism where the middle term is predicate in both premises. [Note: This definition is standard across the tradition; no significant shift.]
الكيف (al-kayf): The quality of a proposition (affirmative or negative).
الكلية الموجبة (al-kulliyya al-mūjiba): A universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every A is B').

Author explains three reduction methods with an example of reductio ad absurdum.

Reducing the second figure is like translating a foreign language—conversion and contradiction are your tools.

You already know that the first figure is the only one that directly yields a certain conclusion. But many real arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology come in the second figure. Commentary
This passage explains the methods of reducing the second figure to the first figure. The author mentions three main methods: (1) By converting the minor premise (عكس الصغرى) to get a second figure syllogism that can be reduced; (2) By converting the major premise (عكس الكبرى) to get a third figure; (3) By using proof by contradiction (الخلف). The author then gives an example: Suppose the conclusion is 'No B is C' and the premises are 'Every B is A' and 'No A is C'. To prove this by reductio, we assume the contradictory of the conclusion, i.e., 'Some B is C'. From this with the first premise ('Every B is A') we get 'Some A is C' by first figure, but this contradicts the second premise ('No A is C'), so the assumption is false and the original conclusion is true. This shows the logic of reduction using contradiction.

Why Only Necessary Universal Premises Yield Certainty
Author critiques unqualified universals and introduces mixed moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author considers unqualified universal premises insufficient for certain knowledge and identify the shift to mixed modal syllogisms.

Core Idea
Non-necessary universal premises cannot produce certainty; only necessary ones are epistemically useful.

The author has just established that the human mind can only assert universal propositions when they are necessary. Now he draws the epistemological consequence: unqualified (non-modal) universal premises are not beneficial for the sciences. After this critique, he signals the upcoming discussion of mixed modal syllogisms, where premises carry different modalities. This lesson unpacks why necessity is the gold standard for certain knowledge in logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Find the statement that the mind cannot assert a universal proposition except the necessary ones. This is the core epistemological principle.
The Arabic phrase 'لا يتمكن من الجزم بالقضية الكلية إلا الضروريات' explicitly restricts certain judgment to necessary propositions.
2
Understand the reason
Why are unqualified universals insufficient? Because they lack modal force—they might be true in some cases but not all necessarily, so they don't yield certainty.
The author says 'تخلف العلم'—the knowledge that occurs in unqualified propositions is not beneficial.
3
Recognize the consequence
Since unqualified premises don't produce certain knowledge, they are useless for the demonstrative sciences ('ulum).
This is a strong critique: non-necessary universals cannot serve as foundations for proof.
4
Note the transition
The author announces the next topic: mixed modal syllogisms, where premises have different modalities (e.g., necessary and possible).
The phrase 'القسم الثاني في الخلطات في الأشكال الأربعة' marks the shift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قد عرفت ما قررناه من قبل أن العقل لا يتمكن من الجزم بالقضية الكلية إلا الضروريات فيكون تخلف العلم في هذه المطلقات غير منتفع به في العلوم أصلا. و لما الكلام في سائر الموجهات البسيطة فيندرج في القسم الذي يأتي بعد ذلك إن شاء الله تعالى. القسم الثاني في الخلطات في الأشكال الأربعة.
And you have known what we established before: that the mind cannot assert a universal proposition except the necessary ones. So the knowledge that occurs in these unqualified [propositions] is not beneficial for the sciences at all. And since the discussion of the remaining simple modal propositions is included in the section that comes after that, God willing. The second section: on the mixed moods in the four figures.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique and a transition. The author reminds the reader of a previously established point: the human mind cannot confidently judge any universal proposition unless it is a necessary truth (i.e., a proposition whose truth is necessary, such as 'Every human is rational' or 'Every body is extended'). Consequently, the knowledge gained from syllogisms that use unqualified (non-modal) universal premises is not beneficial for the demonstrative sciences ('ulum), because such premises do not yield certainty. After this critique, the author announces that the remaining discussion of simple modal propositions (such as possible, impossible, etc.) will be covered in the next section. He then explicitly begins the second section of the book, which deals with mixed modal syllogisms (al-khalatat)—i.e., syllogisms whose premises have different modalities (e.g., one necessary, one possible). Thus, this passage serves as a bridge between the treatment of unqualified categorical syllogisms and the more complex modal logic that follows.

Mixed Syllogisms in the First Figure: Three Cases
Author classifies mixed modal syllogisms in the first figure into three cases based on the modality of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a first-figure mixed modal syllogism by classifying the major premise as necessary, perpetual, or merely possible given a possible minor premise.

Core Idea
In mixed modal syllogisms, the conclusion's modality follows the major, but conditional necessity can block the inference.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas classifies mixed modal syllogisms in the first figure. When the minor premise is possible (mumkina), the conclusion's modality depends on the major premise. We'll examine three cases and an important objection that reveals the subtlety of modal logic in Islamic Kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise's modality
The passage states the minor premise is possible (mumkina). Confirm what 'possible' means: contingent, neither necessary nor impossible.
Possible here is 'specific possibility' (mumkina khassa).
2
Classify the major premise into one of three types
The three categories: necessary in itself (daruriyya), perpetual (da'ima), or merely possible (muhtamala). Understand the difference: necessary holds always by essence, perpetual holds at all times but not necessarily, possible holds not always.
3
Determine the conclusion's modality based on the major
For necessary major, conclusion is necessary; for perpetual, perpetual; otherwise, particularly possible. This is the rule given by the author.
The conclusion's modality mirrors the major's modality.
4
Analyze the objection
The objection: 'Every sperm is possibly a human' (minor) and 'Every human is necessarily an animal' (major) leads to 'Every sperm is necessarily an animal' – which is false. So the rule seems to fail.
Identify the premises and conclusion in the objection.
5
Evaluate the author's response
The author says the major premise is not absolutely necessary but conditional (mashruta): it holds for humans only as long as they are human. A sperm is not yet human, so the necessary conclusion does not follow. This shows the importance of subject-term scope in modal logic.
Conditional necessity depends on the subject retaining its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبل الخوض في التحديد لابد من تقديم مقدمات ثلاث: ما إذا كانت الصغرى ممكنة فالكبرى إما أن تكون ضرورية لذاتها أو لا ضرورية ولا دائمة أو محتملة. القسم الأول فالنتيجة فيه ضرورية. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن تكون الكبرى دائمة فالنتيجة دائمة. وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن لا تكون ضرورية ولا دائمة فالنتيجة ممكنة خاصة. ولا يقال هذا باطل بمثل قولنا بالامكان كل نطفة إنسان وبالضرورة كل إنسان حيوان فلا يلزم بالضرورة كل نطفة حيوان. لأنا نقول إن الكبرى ليست ضرورية مطلقة بل مشروطة. والجواب ضعيف لأن النطفة على انقلابها يمكن أن تخلع صفات الإنسان...
Before delving into the specification, it is necessary to present three premises: when the minor premise is possible, the major premise is either necessary in itself, or not necessary and not perpetual, or probable. The first case: the conclusion is necessary. As for the second case: when the major is perpetual, the conclusion is perpetual. As for the third case: when it is neither necessary nor perpetual, the conclusion is particularly possible. And one should not object that this is invalid by such as our statement: 'Every sperm is possibly a human, and every human is necessarily an animal' – for it does not follow that 'Every sperm is necessarily an animal'. For we say that the major premise is not absolutely necessary but conditional. And the answer is weak because the sperm, in its transformation, can shed the attributes of a human...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage deals with mixed modal syllogisms in the first figure. The author first introduces three preliminary cases concerning the modality of the minor and major premises. When the minor is possible (mumkina), the major can be necessary (daruriyya), or not necessary and not perpetual (da'ima), or probable (muhtamala). The conclusion follows the modality of the major: if the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if it is perpetual, the conclusion is perpetual; otherwise, the conclusion is particularly possible. The author then anticipates an objection: consider the minor 'Every sperm is possibly a human' and the major 'Every human is necessarily an animal' – the conclusion 'Every sperm is necessarily an animal' seems false. The author responds that the major is not absolutely necessary but conditional (mashruta) – meaning it holds only as long as the subject exists under certain conditions. The objection is then claimed to be weak because the sperm, in its potential to change, may shed the attributes of humanity. This highlights the subtlety of modal logic and the importance of distinguishing absolute from conditional necessity.

ممكنة

Three Divisions of Modal Syllogisms
Classification of modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a modal syllogism into one of three divisions based on the modality of its major premise and determine whether the conclusion's necessity is absolute or conditional.

Core Idea
The modality of the conclusion follows the major premise, but necessity is conditional, not absolute.

In this passage from the Mulakhkhas, the author classifies modal syllogisms into three kinds depending on the modality of the major premise. This classification is essential for understanding how modal conclusions follow from modal premises. The key insight is that even when the conclusion is necessary, that necessity is not absolute but conditional upon the subject's existence and possession of the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality of the major premise
Look at the major premise (the premise containing the major term and middle term). Determine if it is expressed as necessary (daruriyya), perpetual (da'ima), or something else (possible, existential, indefinite).
The major premise's modality is the decisive factor.
2
Apply the classification rule
If the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary (but conditional). If the major is perpetual, the conclusion is perpetual. Otherwise, the conclusion is specifically possible (mumkina khassa).
This rule is based on the author's argument that the major's modality transfers to the conclusion.
3
Understand why necessity is conditional
The major says 'whenever the middle holds, the major holds necessarily.' The minor says the middle is possible for the minor. So the necessity of the conclusion only holds when that possibility is actualized. Hence it is conditional (mashruta), not absolute.
Absolute necessity would hold regardless of any condition.
4
Distinguish conditional from absolute necessity
Conditional necessity depends on the subject existing and having the middle term. Absolute necessity would be unconditional. The author emphasizes this to avoid misunderstanding.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic, especially in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول فالمنتجة فيه ضرورية لأن الكبرى دلت على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر بالضرورة في جملتها وقبل وجوده وبعده، والصغرى دلت على المكان [المكان] شوق الأوسط للأصغر، فبتقدير وقوع ذلك المكان يكون ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر ضروريا. وكل ما يكون ضروريا عند فرض وقوع شيء ممكن الوقوع كان ضروريا سواء وقع ذلك الممكن أو لم يقع، لأنه من المستحيل أن يصير ضروريا عند وقوع ذلك الممكن مع أنه قبل ذلك ما كان ضروريا. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن تكون الكبرى دائمة فالنسبة [النتيجة] دائمة بعين تلك الحجة. وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن لا تكون ضرورية ولا دائمة فالنسبة [النتيجة] ممكنة خاصة، وكانت الكبرى ممكنة خاصة أو وجودية لا دائرة [لا دائمة] أو منتشرة، لأن الكبرى دلت على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر بالجهة المعتبرة في الكبرى، والصغرى دلت على المكان [الإمكان] شوق الأوسط للأصغر. وقد بينا أن الكبرى ليست ضرورية مطلقة بل مشروطة.
The first division: the conclusion in it is necessary, because the major premise indicates that everything to which the middle term is established, no matter how, the major is established for it necessarily in its totality and before its existence and after it. And the minor premise indicates the possibility of the middle term for the minor. So by supposing that possibility occurs, the establishment of the major for the minor becomes necessary. And everything that is necessary when the occurrence of a possible thing is supposed is necessary whether that possible occurs or not, because it is impossible that it becomes necessary at the occurrence of that possible while before that it was not necessary. As for the second division: that the major is perpetual, then the conclusion is perpetual by the same argument. As for the third division: that it is not necessary nor perpetual, then the conclusion is specifically possible, and the major is specifically possible or existential, not perpetual, or indefinite, because the major indicates that everything to which the middle is established, no matter how, the major is established for it according to the modality considered in the major, and the minor indicates the possibility of the middle for the minor. And we have shown that the major is not absolutely necessary but conditional.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage classifies modal syllogisms based on the modality of the major premise. The author states three divisions: first, when the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary. The proof is that the major asserts that whenever the middle holds for something, the major holds necessarily, regardless of time. The minor asserts that the middle is possible for the minor. So if that possibility is actualized, the major becomes necessary for the minor. But since the necessity is conditional on a possible event, it remains necessary even if the event does not occur, because a conditional necessity is not contingent on actual occurrence. This is a key point: the necessity here is 'conditional necessity' (darura mashruta), not absolute. The second division: if the major is perpetual (da'ima), the conclusion is perpetual by the same reasoning. The third: if the major is neither necessary nor perpetual, the conclusion is specifically possible (mumkina khassa). The author explains that the major's modality determines the conclusion's modality, and the minor only indicates the possibility of the middle term. The author reminds that the major necessity is not absolute but conditional, which addresses a potential misunderstanding: the necessity is not unconditioned but restricted to the condition that the subject exists and is characterized by the middle term. This classification is foundational for Islamic logic's treatment of modal syllogisms, where the modality of premises governs the modality of conclusions.

Key Terms
ضرورية
دائمة
ممكنة خاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Possible (mumkina)
B) Necessary (daruriyya mutlaqa)
C) Conditional necessary (daruriyya mashruta)
D) Perpetual (da'ima)

Key Takeaway
In modal syllogisms, the modality of the conclusion follows the major premise, but necessity is always conditional on the minor's possibility, not absolute.

The Fourth Division of Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author classifies the fourth division of mixed modal syllogisms and states its conclusion type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of major premises in the fourth division of mixed modal syllogisms and determine the modality of the conclusion.

Core Idea
The major premise 'loaded' with necessity and possibility determines the conclusion's modality.

In post-Avicennan logic, modal syllogisms are classified based on the modality of premises. The fourth division deals with major premises that are not purely necessary or purely possible, but 'loaded' with both. This lesson will help you parse the four specific types of such major premises and understand how the conclusion's modality is derived.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'mixed modal' in context
The phrase 'الكبرى محمّلة للضرورة واللاضرورة' means the major premise is loaded with both necessity and non-necessity; it is not fixed to one modality.
This is a specific post-Avicennan concept; earlier logicians did not use this division.
2
Identify the four types of major premises
The author lists: (1) al-mumkinah al-ʿāmmah (general possible), (2) al-muṭlaqah al-ʿāmmah (general absolute), (3) al-ʿurfiyyah al-ʿāmmah (general conventional), (4) al-mashrūṭah al-ʿāmmah (general conditional). Memorize these terms and their meanings.
Each type has a specific condition on the persistence of the predicate.
3
Determine the default conclusion modality
The text states: 'كانت النتيجة كلية عامة' (the conclusion is universal absolute). This is the default when the major premise is one of these types.
Universal absolute means the predicate applies to all individuals of the subject without modal qualifier.
4
Apply the condition for necessity
If the predicate (المحمول) is true in itself as necessary (ضرورية), then the conclusion is necessary; otherwise it is particular possible (مكنة خاصة).
This condition overrides the default absolute conclusion.
5
Handle the conditional absolute case
For al-mashrūṭah al-ʿāmmah, if it is not true in its conditional sense (i.e., the condition fails), then its truth becomes absolute possibility (الإمكان). This affects the conclusion accordingly.
The passage is corrupt here; this is the best reconstruction.

القسم الرابع وهو ما إذا كانت الكبرى محمّلة للضرورة واللاضرورة، وهي في أربع قضايا: المكنة العامة، والمطلقة العامة، والعرفية العامة، والمشروطة العامة. كانت النتيجة كلية عامة، لأن المحمول [؟] إن صدقت في نفسه ضرورية كانت النتيجة ضرورية، وإلا فمكنة خاصة. والمشروطة العامة إن لم تصدق خرجت صدقي العام [؟] هو الإمكان. والكبرى العرفية الخاصة والمشروطة الخاصة [؟] تكون صغرى [؟]
The fourth division: when the major premise is loaded with necessity and non-necessity, and it consists of four types: absolute possible, absolute absolute, conventional absolute, and conditional absolute. The conclusion is universal absolute, because the predicate [؟] if it is true in itself as necessary then the conclusion is necessary, otherwise it is particular possible. As for the conditional absolute, if it is not true, its truth is absolute [؟] which is possibility. As for the major conventional particular and conditional particular [؟] it becomes minor [؟]

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth division of mixed modal syllogisms in the context of the Mulakhkhas. The author classifies the cases where the major premise is mixed (muḥammalah) between necessity and non-necessity. He enumerates four types of such major premises: (1) al-mumkinah al-ʿāmmah (general possible), (2) al-muṭlaqah al-ʿāmmah (general absolute), (3) al-ʿurfiyyah al-ʿāmmah (general conventional), and (4) al-mashrūṭah al-ʿāmmah (general conditional). The conclusion in all these cases is said to be a universal absolute (kulliyyah ʿāmmah). The reasoning given is that the predicate (al-maḥmūl) if it is true in itself as necessary, then the conclusion is necessary; otherwise it is a particular possible. For the conditional absolute type, if it is not true in its conditional sense, its truth is the absolute (al-ʿāmm) which is possibility. The passage then mentions that the specific conventional and specific conditional major premises become minor [premises?], but the text is corrupt and unclear. The author is organizing the possible combinations of modal premises in a syllogism, particularly when the major premise has mixed modal force. This is essential for determining the modality of the conclusion in such mixed syllogisms.

Modal Conditions in First-Figure Syllogisms: The Durable Major Premise
Author explains condition for first figure syllogism when major premise is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a necessary (durable) major premise in a first-figure categorical syllogism leads to a contradiction and explain how to resolve it by reinterpreting the subject.

Core Idea
Necessity in major premise must match the permanence of the minor premise's description, not existence.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas explores a subtle condition for valid categorical syllogisms in the first figure when the major premise is necessary (durable). The author shows that a seemingly valid syllogism can break down if the necessity of the major term is tied to the description of the middle term rather than its existence. We will parse the condition and learn how to avoid contradictory premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism components
Extract the major, middle, and minor terms from the author's description. The major term (al-akbar) is the predicate of the conclusion, the middle term (al-awsat) connects the premises, and the minor term (al-asghar) is the subject of the conclusion.
2
Understand the temporal condition
Note that 'dawam' (permanence) applies to the description of the middle term, not its existence. The major premise asserts that the major term holds necessarily as long as the middle term's description persists.
3
Detect the contradiction
If the major premise requires permanence of the major for any subject described by the middle term, but the minor premise only asserts that the minor term has the middle term without guaranteeing permanence of that description, then the premises become contradictory: the major implies that nothing described by the middle term is permanently described by the major, while the minor assumes the opposite.
4
Apply the resolution
The author resolves the contradiction by taking the subject of the major premise absolutely (without the condition of permanence). This allows the major premise to be true without conflicting with the minor premise, and the syllogism can yield a valid conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: القياس لا ينعقد منه؛ لأن الأكبر دائم بدوام وصف الأوسط لا بدوام وجوده، والأوسط يكون حكمه بدوام وصف الأصغر لا بدوام وجوده، موجب أن يكون الأكبر دانا بدوام وصف الأصغر لا بدوام وجوده. ثم [؟] القسم الثاني: فالقياس الصادق المقدمات لا ينعقد منه؛ لأنك إذا شرطت في الكبرى دوام الأكبر بدوام وصف الأوسط لم تأخذ العرفية الخاصة، بل أخذت ضرورة الأكبر بدوام وصف الأوسط كما في المشروطة الخاصة. ثم حكمت الصغرى بأن الأصغر إلى بعض موصوف بالأوسط، ولما فسلم من دوام الأوسط للأصغر دوام الأكبر، لكن ذلك باطل؛ لأنك حكمت في الكبرى أنه لا شيء من الموصوفات بالأوسط موصوف بالأكبر. فلم تتئت [؟] من المقدمتين معاندة. لكن إذا أخذنا موضوع هذه الكبرى مطلقا بل بشرط اللادوام اندفعت المعاندة، مثل أن نقول: كل موصوف بالأوسط على قسمين، منه ما لا انصافه به حائم [؟].
The first division: The syllogism is not formed from it; because the major term is permanent with the permanence of the description of the middle term, not with the permanence of its existence, and the middle term's judgment is with the permanence of the description of the minor term, not with the permanence of its existence, necessitating that the major term is always [present] with the permanence of the description of the minor term, not with the permanence of its existence. Then the second division: The syllogism with true premises is not formed from it; because if you stipulate in the major premise the permanence of the major term with the permanence of the description of the middle term, you have not taken the particular conditional, but rather you have taken the necessity of the major term with the permanence of the description of the middle term, as in the particular conditional. Then you judged the minor premise that the minor term is in some way described by the middle term, and since it is not free from the permanence of the middle term for the minor term, [there is] permanence of the major term, but that is false; because you judged in the major premise that none of the things described by the middle term are described by the major term. So the two premises become contradictory. But if we take the subject of this major premise absolutely rather than with the condition of non-permanence, the contradiction is removed, such as when we say: Everything described by the middle term is of two kinds, including what its description is not permanent.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage addresses the conditions under which a first-figure syllogism with modal premises yields a valid conclusion. The author distinguishes two divisions. In the first division, the syllogism is not formed (i.e., does not produce a conclusion) when the major premise is necessary (durable) because the necessity of the major term depends on the permanence of the description of the middle term, not on the existence of the middle term itself. In the second division, the syllogism with true premises also fails if the major premise is taken as a universal conditional (e.g., 'Every A is B necessarily'). The contradiction arises because the minor premise affirms that the minor term has the middle term, but not permanently, while the major requires permanence. However, if the major is interpreted with an absolute subject (without the condition of permanence), the conflict disappears. For example, we can say that everything described by the middle term may or may not be permanent. This analysis is crucial for understanding modal syllogisms in the Islamic logical tradition, where temporal conditions affect validity.

Key Terms
الأكبر
الأوسط
الأصغر
دوام
المشروطة الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The validity of a first-figure categorical syllogism with a necessary major premise depends on the permanence of the middle term's description, not its existence.

Conclusion Modality in Syllogisms with Non-Durative Minor
Author explains how conclusion modality is determined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the conclusion of a categorical syllogism follows the modality of the major or the minor premise when the minor is non-durative.

Core Idea
The conclusion typically follows the major's modality, unless the major is 'urfiyya, then it follows the minor's.

In classical Islamic logic, the modality of the conclusion in a categorical syllogism is not always determined by the major premise. When the minor premise is non-durative (i.e., it does not assert that the predicate holds at all times), special rules apply. This lesson unpacks these rules from the Mulakhkhas of al-Razi, using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises' types
Determine if the minor premise is durative or non-durative, and note the type of the major premise (e.g., general absolute, general 'urfiyya, necessary, etc.).
2
Apply the default rule
If the major is not a general 'urfiyya, the conclusion shares the major's modality (e.g., if both are general absolute, the conclusion is general absolute).
3
Apply the exception
If the major is a general 'urfiyya, the conclusion takes the modality of the minor premise, because the 'urfiyya major only asserts the predicate as long as the middle term holds.
4
Verify with examples
Check the passage's examples: with general absolute minor and general absolute major, the conclusion is general absolute; with general absolute minor and general 'urfiyya major, the conclusion follows the minor's modality.

Because the predicate of the minor premise is not restricted to duration, and its subject is the inheritance of the subject of the major premise, with a witness [؟] and one with duration of description [؟] and it is the minor, and from it not like that, like the predicates of the major [؟]. And the third division: when the minor is opposed to duration and generality, that is propositions of general possibility, general absolute, special possibility, general 'urfiyya, general condition, and necessary existential [؟]. Most likely, the syllogism is not valid if the minor is either durative or non-durative [؟]. If we assume duration, the syllogism is not valid [؟]. ... The middle term, that is except the two possibilities, and it is eleven types, and the major premise is a proposition in which the duration of the predicate with the duration of the subject's description is not considered, and that is except the [؟] and the conditioned, and it is wide in scope, and the conclusion follows the major, and that is one hundred battles, one [؟] for the meaning of the major in all of them, so that what has the middle term established for it, however it is established, the major is established for it. ... If you know these premises, let us return to the expression of the mentioned aspect in the general absolute minor with the general absolute major, then there is no doubt in the product with the mentioned figures, and with the [؟] the conclusion is the major for the mentioned inclusion. ... And with the general 'urfiyya, the conclusion is like the minor because the meaning of the 'urfiyya is that everything that has the middle term established for it, however it is established, the major is established for it in every time the middle term is established for it, but the minor has the middle term established for it without specification of that establishment, so the major is established for it in every time.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion of the conditions for the validity of a categorical syllogism when the minor premise is not restrictive with respect to duration (i.e., it is absolute or possibilistic). The author first describes the case where the minor is non-durative and the major is durative, noting that the syllogism is valid if the minor is the subject of the major's predicate, but not vice versa. Then he presents the third division: when the minor premise is not durative and not general, listing the types of propositions that can serve as the minor: general possibility, general absolute, special possibility, general 'urfiyya, general conditional, and necessary existential. The author argues that for the syllogism to be valid, the minor must be either durative or non-durative, but if it is durative the syllogism may not be valid when the major is of certain types. The conclusion follows the major premise in its modality. For example, with a general absolute minor and a general absolute major, the conclusion is general absolute. But if the major is a general 'urfiyya (customary conditional), the conclusion follows the minor's modality because the 'urfiyya major requires the predicate to hold as long as the middle term holds, but the minor assures the middle term holds only without specifying duration. This analysis applies Islamic logical examples, such as 'Zayd is moving' (minor) and 'Everything moving is changeable' (major), but the author notes that the major must be considered as absolute, not necessary or conditional, to avoid confusion.

Key Terms

الكبرى
المطلقة العامة
العرفية العامة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the major premise is a general 'urfiyya, what does the conclusion follow?
A) The major premise
B) The minor premise
C) The middle term
D) The figure of the syllogism

Key Takeaway
In a categorical syllogism with a non-durative minor, the conclusion's modality generally follows the major, but if the major is 'urfiyya, it follows the minor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the minor premise 'The world is temporally originated' (general absolute) and the major premise 'Everything temporally originated is contingent' (general absolute). What is the modality of the conclusion, and why?
?

Types of Non-Durative Minor Premises
Author lists six types of non-durative minor premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six types of propositions that can serve as non-durative minor premises in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A non-durative minor can be general absolute, general possibility, special possibility, general 'urfiyya, general conditional, or necessary existential.

In the Mulakhkhas, al-Razi enumerates the specific types of propositions that are eligible as minor premises when the minor is not durative. This lesson catalogs these types and their characteristics, helping you recognize them in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
List the six types
From the passage: general possibility, general absolute, special possibility, general 'urfiyya, general conditional, and necessary existential.
2
Define each type
General absolute: A is B without any temporal restriction. General possibility: A can be B. Special possibility: A can be B at some unspecified time. General 'urfiyya: A is B as long as A is described by some quality C. General conditional: If A is B, then A is C. Necessary existential: A necessarily exists.
Exact definitions may vary; use the author's intended meanings.
3
Contrast with durative premises
Durative premises assert that the predicate holds for all times the subject exists; non-durative premises lack that assertion.
4
Apply to example
Given a candidate minor premise like 'The world is temporally originated', classify it as general absolute.

General Absolute vs. General 'Urfiyya
Author contrasts general absolute and general 'urfiyya in the context of conclusion modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a general absolute proposition and a general 'urfiyya proposition and explain how each affects the conclusion's modality in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A general absolute asserts the predicate without temporal condition; a general 'urfiyya asserts it conditionally on the subject's description.

Two key types of premises in the Mulakhkhas are the general absolute and the general 'urfiyya. While both are non-durative, they differ in their logical import, especially when they appear as the major premise. This lesson clarifies the distinction and its impact on the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Define general absolute
A proposition of the form 'Every A is B' without any temporal or conditional restriction. Example: 'Everything moving is changeable' (absolute).
2
Define general 'urfiyya
A proposition of the form 'Every A is B as long as A is described by some quality C' (the middle term). Example: 'Everything moving is changeable as long as it moves' (urfiyya).
3
Contrast their behavior in syllogisms
When general absolute is major, the conclusion takes its modality (absolute). When general 'urfiyya is major, the conclusion follows the minor's modality.
This is the key rule from the passage.
4
Apply to an example
Take minor: 'Zayd is moving' (absolute). Major 1: 'Everything moving is changeable' (absolute) → conclusion: 'Zayd is changeable' (absolute). Major 2: 'Everything moving is changeable as long as it moves' (urfiyya) → conclusion: 'Zayd is changeable' but with the same modality as minor (absolute), not necessarily permanent.

The author argues that for the syllogism to be valid, the minor must be either durative or non-durative, but if it is durative the syllogism may not be valid when the major is of certain types. The conclusion follows the major premise in its modality. For example, with a general absolute minor and a general absolute major, the conclusion is general absolute. But if the major is a general 'urfiyya (customary conditional), the conclusion follows the minor's modality because the 'urfiyya major requires the predicate to hold as long as the middle term holds, but the minor assures the middle term holds only without specifying duration. This analysis applies Islamic logical examples, such as 'Zayd is moving' (minor) and 'Everything moving is changeable' (major), but the author notes that the major must be considered as absolute, not necessary or conditional, to avoid confusion.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة
العرفية العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The absolute has no temporal condition, while the 'urfiyya is conditional on the subject's description
C) The 'urfiyya is stronger because it asserts permanence

Key Takeaway
The general absolute asserts unconditionally; the general 'urfiyya asserts conditionally on the subject's description, and this difference affects the conclusion's modality in a syllogism.

Conclusion Modality in First Figure: Absolute Major
Rule and refutation for absolute major in first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a first-figure syllogism when the minor is universal absolute and the major is also absolute or similarly unmodalized, and refute the common objection.

Core Idea
In first figure, when the major premise is absolute, the conclusion follows the major in modality.

When both premises in a first-figure categorical syllogism are universal absolute, the conclusion is also absolute. Some opponents mistakenly argue that the conclusion should follow the 'weaker' minor premise, but their counterexample rests on a misidentified modal type. This lesson clarifies the correct rule and the error behind the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism structure
Note that this is a first-figure categorical syllogism: the minor premise is universal absolute (e.g., 'Zayd is moving'), the major premise is also universal absolute (e.g., 'Everything moving is changing'). The middle term is 'moving'.
The modality 'universal absolute' means no temporal or necessity qualification – a simple universal affirmative.
2
Apply the rule for conclusion modality
Al-Razi states: 'al-natījah ka al-kubrā' – the conclusion takes the modality of the major premise. Since both are absolute, the conclusion is absolute.
This rule holds for all absolute-like modalities listed: absolute necessary, existential non-necessary, non-permanent absolute, temporal, widespread, general and specific possible.
3
Examine the opponents' objection
The opponents give an example: 'Zayd is moving' (absolute) and 'Everything moving is changing' (necessary). They claim the conclusion 'Zayd is changing' is not necessary, so the conclusion should follow the minor (absolute).
Their example seems to show a necessary major, not absolute, so it is not a counterexample to the rule.
4
Refute the objection using modal distinction
Al-Razi responds: the major in their example is 'conditional necessary' (ḍarūrah mashrūṭah), not absolute necessity. Our discussion concerns only absolute necessity (ḍarūrah muṭlaqah). Therefore, their objection does not apply.
This highlights the importance of distinguishing modal types in syllogistic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلنرجع إلى التعبية الوجه المذكور في الصغرى المطلقة العامة مع الكبرى المطلقة العامة فالإنتاج مع الأشكال المذكور ومع الصدقية فالنتيجة كالكبرى للاندراج المذكور وخالف قوم سطبون لأوسطهم وقالوا إنها الصغرى لا نا نقول بالإطلاق زيد متحرك وبالضرورة كل متحرك متغير ولا يلزم أن يقال بالضرورة زيد متغير. وجواب أن هذه الكبرى ضرورية مشروطة لا مطلقة وكلامنا في الضرورة المطلقة والمانع اللذاعة والوجودية اللاضرورية واللادوام المطلقة والوقتية والمنتشرة والممكنة العامة والخاصية فالنتيجة كالكبرى للاندراج المذكور. وإما مع العرفية العامة فالنتيجة كالصغرى لان معنى الكبرى أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط كيف كان ثبت له الأكبر في كل زمان ثبوت الأوسط له لكن الأصغر ثبت له الأوسط من غير بيان كيفية ذلك الثبوت فيثبت له الأكبر في كل زمان ثبوت الأوسط له ثم في احتمال أن يكون ثبوته له دائما أو آناما، وليس بدوام ذات الأصغر وإن لا يكون كذلك والمشترك هو الإطلاق العام.
Let us return to the arrangement of the aforementioned mode: with the minor universal absolute and the major universal absolute. Then the production [of conclusion] occurs according to the mentioned figures and truthfulness, so the conclusion is like the major due to the mentioned subsumption. And some people opposed, claiming that it is like the minor, saying, 'We say absolutely: Zayd is moving, and necessarily: everything moving is changing, but it does not necessarily follow that Zayd is changing.' The answer is that this major is conditional necessary, not absolute. Our discussion concerns absolute necessity and the preventing factor? and the existential non-necessary and the non-permanent absolute and the temporal and the widespread and the general and special possible. So the conclusion is like the major for the mentioned subsumption. And with the general conditional? the conclusion is like the minor, because the meaning of the major is: everything to which the middle term is established, however it is established, the major term is established for it in every time of the middle term's establishment to it. But the minor is established to have the middle term without specifying the modality of that establishment, so the major term is established for it in every time of the middle term's establishment to it. Then there is a possibility that its establishment to it is perpetual or momentary, not by the permanence of the essence of the minor, and that it is not so. And the common factor is the universal absolute.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage examines the conclusion of a first-figure syllogism when the minor premise is universal absolute (i.e., a universal affirmative proposition without modal specification) and the major premise varies in modality. First, when the major is also universal absolute, the conclusion follows the major in modality—it is absolute. Some opponents incorrectly thought the conclusion should follow the minor, giving an example: 'Zayd is moving' (absolute) and 'Everything moving is changing' (necessary) but it does not follow that 'Zayd is changing' necessarily. The author refutes this by noting that the major in their example is actually a conditional necessary, not an absolute necessary, so it is not the case under discussion. For the absolute necessary, the conclusion remains absolute. Second, when the major is 'general conditional' (perhaps meaning 'conditional universal'), the conclusion follows the minor, i.e., it is absolute. The reason is that the major asserts that whenever the middle term holds for something, the major term holds for it, but without specifying the duration of that holding for the minor. The minor only establishes the middle term without specifying modality; thus the major term holds at every moment the middle term holds, but this could be perpetual or momentary. The common factor in all these cases is the universal absolute proposition (al-itlaq al-'amm). This analysis is crucial because it shows how modal qualifications affect the validity and modality of syllogistic conclusions in the first figure.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة
الضرورة المطلقة
المشروطة العامة

C) Necessary, because humility is essential
D) Conditional, because prayer is conditional

Key Takeaway
In first-figure categorical syllogisms, when the minor is universal absolute, the modality of the conclusion matches the modality of the major premise, as long as the major is also absolute or an absolute-like modality.

Special Case: Conditional Major in First Figure
Exception: conditional major leads to conclusion like minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the conclusion follows the minor rather than the major in a first-figure syllogism, specifically when the major is a general conditional (mashrūṭah ʿāmmah).

Core Idea
When the major is general conditional, the conclusion follows the minor's modality (absolute).

Al-Razi introduces a special case where the major premise is a 'general conditional' (al-mashrūṭah al-ʿāmmah). In this scenario, the conclusion takes the modality of the minor premise (absolute), not the major. This is because the conditional major only guarantees the major term whenever the middle term holds, but does not specify the duration of that holding for the minor term. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this exception.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'general conditional' major
Al-Razi explains: the general conditional major means that for everything that has the middle term (however it has it), the major term holds in every time that the middle term holds. This is a conditional statement: if the middle term is present, the major term is present.
This differs from absolute, which makes no condition; the conditional restricts the major's application to times when the middle holds.
2
Analyze the minor premise
The minor premise is universal absolute: it asserts that the minor term has the middle term, but does not specify whether that possession is perpetual or temporary.
The minor only says 'the minor has the middle', not 'the minor always has the middle'.
3
Determine the conclusion's modality
Since the major only applies when the middle holds, and the minor does not specify the duration of the middle's holding, the conclusion can only assert the major term at times when the minor holds the middle. But because the minor is absolute, the conclusion is also absolute – it does not acquire the conditional modality of the major.
Thus, the conclusion follows the minor's modality (absolute), not the major's conditional.
4
Contrast with previous rule
Recall that when the major is absolute, the conclusion followed the major. Here, the major is conditional, so the rule changes: the conclusion follows the minor. This shows the sensitivity of syllogistic rules to modal nuances.
Al-Razi says: 'the conclusion is like the minor' (النتيجة كالصغرى).

Common Feature in Modal Syllogisms with General and Specific Conditional Premises
Author contrasts the common feature (absolute) with specific cases where conclusion is suspended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute absolute (al-iṭlāq al-ʿāmm) as the common modal feature in a syllogism with a conditional major premise and determine whether the conclusion is absolute or suspended based on the type of conditional.

Core Idea
The common modal feature of conditional syllogisms is the absolute absolute, but specific conditionals lead to suspension (tawaqquf).

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi examines modal syllogisms where the major premise is a conditional proposition. He introduces a crucial distinction between general conditionals (الشرطية العامة) and specific conditionals (الشرطية الخاصة), and identifies the common modal feature—the absolute absolute (الإطلاق العام)—that runs through both. However, he warns that with a specific conditional, the conclusion's modality is suspended (توقف), meaning no definite conclusion can be drawn without further analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise type
First, determine whether the major premise is a general conditional (المشروطة العامة) or a specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة). Al-Razi contrasts these two types in the passage.
General conditional yields a conclusion similar to the minor; specific conditional yields suspension.
2
Trace the logical relationship
For a general conditional, al-Razi argues: 'the major is necessary for the middle, and the middle is constant for the minor, so the major is constant for the minor.' Understand that this is a valid modal syllogism in the first figure.
The modal terms 'necessary' (ضروري) and 'constant' (ثابت) are key.
3
Apply the common feature concept
The common feature (القدر المشترك) across both types is the absolute absolute (الإطلاق العام). This means the conclusion is an unrestricted absolute proposition, but only with the general conditional does the conclusion follow; with the specific conditional, the conclusion is suspended (توقف).
Al-Razi mentions that with the general conditional, the conclusion is like the minor in modality.
4
Distinguish the two cases
Contrast the outcome: with general conditional, the conclusion is absolute (following the minor's modality); with specific conditional, no definite conclusion can be drawn—judgment is suspended until further conditions are clarified.
Tawaqquf means the modal status remains undetermined.
5
Summarize the lesson
The key takeaway: the common modal feature is the absolute absolute, but the conclusiveness depends on the type of conditional. Always check whether the major premise is general or specific to determine if a conclusion can be drawn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشترك هو الإطلاق العام. وأما مع المشروطة العامة فالنتيجة كالصغرى، لأن الأكبر ضروري للأوسط، والأوسط ثابت للأصغر، فالأكبر ثابت للأصغر. لكن من المحتمل أن لا يكون ثبوت الأكبر ضرورياً لذات الأصغر ولا للوصف الذي به صار أصغر، ويحتمل أن يكون كذلك. فالقدر المشترك هو الإطلاق العام. وأما مع الخاصة فيه التوقف.
The common feature is the absolute absolute. As for with the general conditional, the conclusion is like the minor premise, because the major is necessary for the middle, and the middle is constant for the minor, so the major is constant for the minor. But it is possible that the major's constancy is not necessary for the essence of the minor nor for the description by which it became minor, and it is possible that it is so. Thus the common feature is the absolute absolute. As for with the specific, there is suspension.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage discusses the conclusion of a modal syllogism when the premises vary in modality. The author considers two types of conditional major premises: the 'general conditional' (المشروطة العامة) and the 'specific conditional' (المشروطة الخاصة). The common feature across different cases is the 'absolute absolute' (الإطلاق العام), meaning a proposition that is simply true without any temporal or modal restriction. When the major is a general conditional, the conclusion resembles the minor premise in modality: if the minor is absolute, the conclusion is absolute. However, the author notes a possibility: the major's constancy (ثبوت) for the middle may not be necessary (ضروري) for the minor's essence (ذات) nor for the description (وصف) by which the minor term is determined. Despite this, the common feature (القدر المشترك) remains the absolute absolute. In contrast, when the major is a specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة), there is suspension (توقف), meaning no definite conclusion can be drawn without further analysis. This illustrates the careful distinction in Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's logic regarding modal syllogisms: the conclusion's modality is not always straightforward and depends on the type of conditional premise.

Key Terms
الإطلاق العام (al-iṭlāq al-ʿāmm): An unrestricted absolute proposition, true without any temporal or modal qualification; the common denominator in modal syllogisms. [Note: In earlier logic (e.g., Ibn Sina), 'mutlaq' often means absolute in a temporal sense; al-Razi's use here is specifically for the common feature across conditional premises.]
المشروطة العامة (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma): A general conditional major premise that yields a conclusion similar to the minor premise in modality, but with underlying possibilities. [Note: Al-Razi distinguishes between 'general conditional' and 'specific conditional'; this distinction is refined from earlier discussions.]
المشروطة الخاصة (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa): A specific conditional major premise that causes suspension (tawaqquf) in the conclusion's modality. [Note: Al-Razi's 'specific conditional' is more restrictive than some predecessors; it often requires additional conditions for a conclusion.]
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension of judgment; the conclusion's modality remains undetermined without further investigation. [Note: In later logic (e.g., in reflective equilibrium), tawaqquf can be a methodological stance; here it is a technical term meaning no definite conclusion.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) It is always similar to the major premise.

Key Takeaway
The common modal feature in syllogisms with conditional major premises is the absolute absolute, but when the major premise is a specific conditional, the conclusion is suspended (tawaqquf) rather than determined.

Determining Conclusion Modality in Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author enumerates conclusions of mixed modal syllogisms with various premise combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion modality for any mixed modal syllogism by applying the principle of the weaker premise.

Core Idea
In mixed modal syllogisms, the conclusion inherits the weaker of the two premises.

When premises differ in modality (e.g., necessary vs. customary), the conclusion's modality is not arbitrary. Al-Razi's passage in Mulakhkhas systematically lists each combination and its result. By understanding these rules, you can evaluate arguments in Kalam and Fiqh where premises often have different modal strengths.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise modalities
Locate the minor premise (sughra) and major premise (kubra) in the passage. Note their modal adjectives: la-dururiya (non-necessary), 'urfiya 'amma (universal customary), mushrata 'amma (general conditioned), mushrata khassa (special conditioned), or waqtiya (temporal).
The Arabic terms are given in the source.
2
Apply the weaker premise principle
The conclusion's modality is constrained by the weaker premise – if one premise is non-necessary, the conclusion cannot be necessary. For example, a non-necessary minor yields a non-necessary conclusion even if the major is stronger.
3
Consult al-Razi's explicit rule
For a specific combination, find the corresponding rule. Example: when minor is existential non-necessary and major is universal customary, the conclusion is universal customary. Al-Razi states: 'Ma'a al-suqhara al-wujudiya al-la-dururiya ma'a al-'urfiya al-'amma fa al-natija mulaqa 'amma.'
The passage includes many such rules.
4
Reason why the rule holds
The major holds as long as the middle term exists; the minor affirms the middle for the subject but without perpetuity. Hence, the conclusion cannot be perpetual, matching the major's customary modality. The reasoning is that the major's universality is conditional on the middle's presence, and the middle is not guaranteed forever.
Understand the logic behind the rule, not just memorize.
5
Generalize to other combinations
Apply the same reasoning to other premise pairs: with conditioned or temporal premises. For instance, a minor 'urfiya 'amma with major mushrata 'amma yields a conclusion that is also 'urfiya 'amma due to similar constraints.
Practice with the various cases listed in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبتقدير أن لا يكون ضروريا له مع أن الأكبر لا يثبت للأصغر إلا عند حصول الأوسط له أمريكن الاكتفاء ودقا للأصغر ويحتمل أن لا تكون على الوجه المنهمرة يكون ضروريا له لكن ما التقديرين فلا شرط دوام الأكبر بنعام ذاق الاصغر وامامع الخاصتين فغير منعقد الأمه الصغرى الوجودية اللاضرورية مع النتح فالنتيجة كالكبرى ومع العربية العامة فالنتيجة مطلقة عامة مخالفة للمقدمين لأن الأكبر دائم بدوام وصف الأوسط مع احتمال أن يكون ضروريا وأن لا يكون والأوسط ثابت للأصغريح شرط اللضرورة وامع الأحبنات للاست حال ما ورود ومع الضمال التعلم واللادوام فالمعلوم أن لا و الاوسط اله ثم يحتمل أن يكون ضرور حالة إبتدل ضروريا له أبدله و إن كون دعا لما بتعام الذات او بد عام الوصف الذي جعل معه أصغر وإن لا يكون كذلك والمشترك في الأطلاق العامرة ومع المشروطة العامة فالنتيجة مطلقة عامة أيضا مخالفة للمقدمتين لأن الأكبر ضرورت الثوت في جميع زمان الأربطة للام طاقات للأصغر من جريان لله كيف تحت الا اللأضرورة نت الأجرة ذلك الوقت ويبقى سابو الاحتمالات والمشترك هو الأطلاق. العام قسم العرفية والمشروطة الخاصين ففيه التوقف الصغرى الوجودية اللاداعة مع الشح النتيجة كالكرى ومع العرفية والمشرقة العامين النتيجة مطلقة متاحة للعلة المذكورة في الوجودية اللاضرورية الحلقة الخاصة كالصغرى لأن معنى الكرى ان كل ما نت لالة الأوسط حمل له الأكبربة جميع زمان حصول الأوسط لاداعا لكن الاصفر مودلين بالأم سطح بعض الازمنة فهو موصوف بالأكبر بح ذلك الزمان ويستمع أن يكون موصوفا به دالما والا كان بعض ومن ين... ينج وجودية لا حالمة وبيان ظاهرة باللادوام فاختلاطها مع الخاصتي كانت السحر والصدى لوكانت المشروطة مقبلة باللأضرع ... الموصوفات بالأوسط موصوفا بالأكبر داعا وقد اشترطناف الكبرى انه ليس كذلك هذا خلف ولا يجب ان يدوم بدوام وصف الأصغر الاحتمال ان لا يدوم الاوسط بتعام وصف الاصحيح ان الأكبر لاحصل للأصغر الأعنديون الاوسطه وحنين الحب دوام الأكبر بدوام وصفا الأصغر فظهرات المستقر هنا ليس الأثرت الأكبر للصغر بشط اللا دوام فقطع اما مع المشروطة الخاصة فالنتيجة كالصغرى بعين هذه العلة وللصغرى الوقتية مع الفم التي كا لكرى ومع العرفية العامة المنحة مطلقة عامة مخالفة للمقدمت لأن المعلوم شوق الأكبر الأصفى حال حصول الاوسط له وفيها وتك ذلك فالاقتسام باسرها مجملة وكذلك مع المشروطة العامة وامامع العراقية الخاصة فالنتيجة وجودية لادامة مخالفة للمسن لان المعلوم شوق الاكبر للصف حال حصول الاوسط الة مع هذا الحمام من عريان انه يدوم بدوام قصف الاصغام لاوان لعبر فاثبوت الأكبر في مجمع زمان الأوسط الكنام نشرط ثبوت الا وسط جمع زبان وصيف الراء صغر وكذا القول مع المشروطة الخاصة ت الصغرى المنتشرة الكلام فيها كالكلام في العفنية من غير تفاوت الصغرى المكنة العامة هنا الأصفى عير داخل بالفعل عن الأوسط فلا تظهر كيفية النتحة الأنبيان منفصل فانفصل وضر وبها امامع المطلقة العامة فالنتيجة كالصغى لان الى كرى أن صدقت ضرورية كانت المنتجة صدرية والأمكنة خاصة والمشتركة إلى البيت تجتها مع الفتح كا لكم مع الوجودية اللاضرورية واللاد المة والرقية بالمية والتقنية برا تسلم من التنوير مع العربة العامة منتشرة مطلقة عامة إلى التي تعتبر فيها النسبة في وقت غير محتى من غير اعتبار للفرقة هو الامكان امامع الضرورية والدليلة فالنتيجة كا الكبرى علاما من وانا مع عبد اللدوام وجمع المشروطة في الكل ممكنة خاصة مخالفة للمقدمتين وامامع المجنة العامة و نے وقت غیر معین فرح لما ذكره في العنز الثالث من المقدمة الأولى الأولى وقع العلم الخاصة هذه المنية العامة منشية مطلقة التي يعبر فيها الضرورة المشروطة الخاصة هذه الدقنية مع قيد اللاضرورة ان قندت با للأضرحة ومع قيد الاد عام ان قيدته باللافها
Assuming that it is not necessary for him, while the major is not established for the minor except when the middle is obtained for him... [fragmented]. And with the two specials, it is not valid. The minor existential non-necessary with the universal customary, the conclusion is universal customary contrary to the premises because the major is perpetual with the perpetuality of the attribute of the middle with the possibility that it be necessary or not, and the middle is affirmed for the minor... And with the general conditioned, the conclusion is also universal customary contrary to the premises because the major is necessarily affirmed for the minor in its entire time of connection... And with the special conditioned, the conclusion is like the minor...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion of mixed modal syllogisms (qiyas mukhtalit), where the premises have different modalities. The author examines various combinations of minor and major premises—including existential non-necessary (wujudiya la-dururiya), universal customary ('urfiya 'amma), general conditioned (mushrata 'amma), and special conditioned (mushrata khassa)—and determines the modality of the conclusion. For example, when the minor is existential non-necessary and the major is universal customary, the conclusion is universal customary, which differs from the premises in modality. The reasoning is that the major holds as long as the middle term holds, but the middle may not be perpetual. The author also discusses cases where the minor is temporal (waqtiya) and the major is universal customary, leading to a conclusion that is universal customary as well. The passage systematically covers each combination, emphasizing that the conclusion's modality is constrained by the weaker premise, a standard principle in Aristotelian modal logic as adopted by Islamic logicians. This analysis is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh) and theology (kalam), where premises may have different modal strengths.

Key Terms
الصغرى الوجودية اللاضرورية
العرفية العامة
المشروطة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the minor premise is existential non-necessary (wujudiya la-dururiya) and the major is universal customary ('urfiya 'amma), what is the modality of the conclusion?
A) Necessary (dururiya)
B) Universal customary ('urfiya 'amma)
C) Existential non-necessary (wujudiya la-dururiya)
D) Particular (juz'iya)

Key Takeaway
In mixed modal syllogisms, the conclusion's modality is determined by the weaker premise, not the stronger one, as systematically demonstrated by al-Razi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the minor premise 'Every human is temporally originated (hadith)' which is universal customary, and the major premise 'Everything temporally originated has a creator' which is necessary. Determine the modality of the conclusion according to al-Razi's rules.
?
Classify each of the following premise pairs according to the types mentioned in the passage (wujudiya la-dururiya, 'urfiya 'amma, mushrata 'amma, mushrata khassa, waqtiya) and state the conclusion modality: (1) minor is existential non-necessary, major is general conditioned; (2) minor is special conditioned, major is universal customary.

The Syllogism
