8 min
The Problem of the Minor Not Actually Having the Middle
Discussion of the difficulty when minor term is not actually subsumed under middle in modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core difficulty in modal syllogisms when the minor term is not actually subsumed under the middle term.

Core Idea
In modal syllogisms, the minor must actually have the middle for the conclusion to follow.

This passage from Razi's Mulakkhkhas addresses a subtle but critical problem in modal logic. When the minor term is only potentially (not actually) described by the middle term, the standard syllogistic form fails to guarantee the conclusion. Let's unpack this problem and see why possibility alone is not enough.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism type
Recognize that the passage deals with a modal categorical syllogism, specifically a 'particular conditional' proposition (al-mashrutah al-khassah). The problem arises when terms are qualified by possibility (imkan).
2
Locate the given premises
The major premise states that the major term is possible for the middle. The minor premise states that the middle is possible for the minor. However, the minor is not actually subsumed under the middle (i.e., it does not actually possess the middle).
Focus on the phrase 'الأصغر غير مندرج بالفعل تحت الأوسط'.
3
Articulate the difficulty
If the minor does not actually have the middle, how can the judgment (the major term's possibility) transfer to it? The conclusion should be like the major premise, but the link is missing.
The text asks: 'فالحكم الثابت كيف يتعدى إليه' (how does the established judgment transfer to it?)
4
Understand the proposed solution
A preliminary solution suggests that if the major is possible for the middle, and the middle is possible for the minor, then the major is possible for the minor via 'possibility of possibility'. This is intuitive but will be challenged.
The phrase 'إمكان الإمكان قريب عند الذهن' indicates this is a natural but perhaps insufficient move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخاصة فلا شك في كون النتيجة كالكبرى لكن هنا هو أن الأصغر غير مندرج بالفعل تحت الأوسط فالحكم الثابت كيف يتعدى إليه فقيل في تقريره الأكبر لما كان ممكنا للأوسط الممكن للأصغر كان ممكنا للأصغر إمكان الإمكان قريب عند الذهن الحكم بكونه ممكنا ولقائل أن يقول لا نسلم أن الممكن الممكن لشيء يجب أن يكون ممكنا لذلك لأن حقيقة الأوسط مخالفة لحقيقة الأصغر فلا يلزم من كون الأكبر ممكنا للأوسط كونه ممكنا للأصغر ولأنه يبين أن ليس المراد من الكبرى الحكم بثبوت الأكبر للأوسط بل ثبوت الأكبر للموضوعات بالأوسط وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم إنتاج الأمين محب الكبرى لأن الأصغر لم يثبت له الأوسط فالواجب أن يقال في بيان هذه القرينة إن كان الأول كان الأصغر موصوفا بالفعل بالأوسط وحينئذ تزول الشبهة وإن كان الثاني فإما أن عنينا بالكبرى أن كل ما أمكن أن يثبت له الأوسط فله الأكبر على ما هو دأب الفائلين أو عنينا به أن كانت له الأوسط فله الأكبر بالفعل على ما هو رأي الشيخ فإن كانت الكبرى دلت على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط فله إمكان الأكبر لكن الصغرى دلت على أن الأصغر يمكن ثبوت الأوسط له فقد يقع هذا الممكن ويلزم منه ثبوت إمكان الأكبر للأصغر فهذا الفرض الممكن كشف عن كون الأصغر واجب الذات للأكبر بحيث لا ينقلب من القبول الإمكان الذاتي إلى القبول الاحتمال أن تكون حيث الاحتمال ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر مشروطا بثبوت الأوسط له فإن [؟] أن موما تكون ممكنا ولا تتوقف [؟] قبل ثبوت الأوسط له [خالًا؟] فهذا ما نعتقده هنا وامسامع العفية [؟] وامل زوال مانع العامة
And the particular conditional: there is no doubt that the conclusion is like the major premise. But here, the minor is not actually subsumed under the middle, so how does the established judgment transfer to it? It was said in its formulation: the major term, since it was possible for the middle which is possible for the minor, is possible for the minor. Possibility of possibility is near to the mind's judgment of its being possible. An objector may say: we do not grant that what is possible for a possible thing must be possible for that thing, because the reality of the middle differs from the reality of the minor, so it does not follow from the major being possible for the middle that it is possible for the minor. And also because it is clear that what is meant by the major premise is not the judgment of establishing the major for the middle, but rather the establishment of the major for the subjects by virtue of the middle. If that is so, the production of the two premises following the major does not follow, because the minor does not have the middle established for it. So it is necessary to say in explaining this conjunction: if it is the first, then the minor is actually described by the middle, and then the doubt disappears. If it is the second, then either we mean by the major: everything that it is possible that the middle is established for it has the major, as is the custom of the objectors, or we mean by it: if the middle is established for it, then it actually has the major, as is the opinion of the Teacher. If the major indicates that everything for which the middle is established has the possibility of the major, but the minor indicates that the minor can have the middle established for it, then this possible may occur, and from it follows the establishment of the possibility of the major for the minor. So this possible supposition reveals that the minor is necessary of essence for the major such that it does not turn from the acceptance of essential possibility to acceptance of possibility? It is possible that where possibility is the establishment of the major for the minor is conditional upon the establishment of the middle for it. If [??] that it may be possible and does not depend [??] before the establishment of the middle for it [?]. This is what we believe here, and with the general conditional [??] and hope for removal of the general obstacle.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific problem in modal syllogisms, specifically when the conclusion should be like the major premise but the minor term is not actually described by the middle term—it only potentially has it. The author first presents a proposed solution: because the major term is possible for the middle, and the middle is possible for the minor, the major is possible for the minor via 'possibility of possibility'—a concept the mind finds persuasive. An objector then rejects this, arguing that possibility is not transitive when the realities of the middle and minor differ; the major being possible for the middle does not guarantee it is possible for a different thing (the minor). Moreover, the objector clarifies that the major premise does not assert the major for the middle itself, but for subjects that have the middle. Therefore, without the minor actually having the middle, the conclusion does not follow. The author then refines the explanation by distinguishing two cases: either (1) the minor actually has the middle (in which case there is no problem), or (2) it does not. In the second case, two interpretations of the major premise are considered. The first interpretation (favored by objectors) is that the major applies to anything that could possibly have the middle; the second (attributed to 'the Teacher') is that the major applies only to things that actually have the middle. If the major is about possibility and the minor shows that the middle is possible for the minor, then a possible scenario arises where the major is possible for the minor—but this does not give actuality, only possibility. The author concludes that the establishment of the major for the minor may be conditional on the actual possession of the middle. The text ends with a statement of the author's own position, though some words remain unclear.

Key Terms
الأصغر (al-asghar)
الأوسط (al-awsat)
الأكبر (al-akbar)
الإمكان (al-imkan)
المشروطة الخاصة (al-mashrutah al-khassah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difficulty in producing the conclusion?
A) The minor term is not actually described by the middle term.
B) The major term is impossible for the middle.
C) The middle term is not a genuine universal.
D) The syllogism is in the second figure.

Key Takeaway
For a modal syllogism to yield a valid conclusion, the minor term must actually have the middle term, not merely potentially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following kalam argument: 'Everything that has a beginning is contingent. The world has a beginning. Therefore the world is contingent.' Does this syllogism suffer from the same difficulty? Why or why not? (Hint: does the minor 'actually' have the middle?)

The Objection: Possibility is Not Transitive Across Different Realities
Objection to transitivity of possibility in modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the objection that possibility is not transitive when the intermediate term differs in essence from the subject.

Core Idea
Possibility does not transfer across terms with different essences.

After the initial proposal of transitivity of possibility, an objector steps in. The objection is powerful: because the middle term and the minor term have different realities or essences, what is possible for the middle may not be possible for the minor. This critique strikes at the heart of the transitivity assumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says: 'We do not grant that what is possible for a possible thing must be possible for that thing.' This is a direct rejection of the transitivity of possibility.
The phrase 'لا نسلم أن الممكن الممكن لشيء يجب أن يكون ممكنا لذلك' is the key.
2
Understand the reasoning behind the objection
The reason is that 'the reality of the middle differs from the reality of the minor.' Even if B is possible for C, and A is possible for B, A may not be possible for C because the essences of B and C are different.
The text: 'لأن حقيقة الأوسط مخالفة لحقيقة الأصغر'.
3
Recognize the second part of the objection
The objector also clarifies that the major premise does not state that the major is established for the middle itself, but rather that it is established for subjects that have the middle. So without the minor actually having the middle, the conclusion does not follow.
The text: 'ليس المراد من الكبرى الحكم بثبوت الأكبر للأوسط بل ثبوت الأكبر للموضوعات بالأوسط'.
4
Evaluate the strength of the objection
This objection undermines the simple transitivity solution. It forces a deeper analysis of what the major premise means and whether the minor actually possesses the middle.
Think about how this applies to real arguments: e.g., 'Everything that is possible for a possible being is possible for that being' might fail due to differing essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخاصة فلا شك في كون النتيجة كالكبرى لكن هنا هو أن الأصغر غير مندرج بالفعل تحت الأوسط فالحكم الثابت كيف يتعدى إليه فقيل في تقريره الأكبر لما كان ممكنا للأوسط الممكن للأصغر كان ممكنا للأصغر إمكان الإمكان قريب عند الذهن الحكم بكونه ممكنا ولقائل أن يقول لا نسلم أن الممكن الممكن لشيء يجب أن يكون ممكنا لذلك لأن حقيقة الأوسط مخالفة لحقيقة الأصغر فلا يلزم من كون الأكبر ممكنا للأوسط كونه ممكنا للأصغر ولأنه يبين أن ليس المراد من الكبرى الحكم بثبوت الأكبر للأوسط بل ثبوت الأكبر للموضوعات بالأوسط وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم إنتاج الأمين محب الكبرى لأن الأصغر لم يثبت له الأوسط فالواجب أن يقال في بيان هذه القرينة إن كان الأول كان الأصغر موصوفا بالفعل بالأوسط وحينئذ تزول الشبهة وإن كان الثاني فإما أن عنينا بالكبرى أن كل ما أمكن أن يثبت له الأوسط فله الأكبر على ما هو دأب الفائلين أو عنينا به أن كانت له الأوسط فله الأكبر بالفعل على ما هو رأي الشيخ فإن كانت الكبرى دلت على أن كل ما ثبت له الأوسط فله إمكان الأكبر لكن الصغرى دلت على أن الأصغر يمكن ثبوت الأوسط له فقد يقع هذا الممكن ويلزم منه ثبوت إمكان الأكبر للأصغر فهذا الفرض الممكن كشف عن كون الأصغر واجب الذات للأكبر بحيث لا ينقلب من القبول الإمكان الذاتي إلى القبول الاحتمال أن تكون حيث الاحتمال ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر مشروطا بثبوت الأوسط له فإن [؟] أن موما تكون ممكنا ولا تتوقف [؟] قبل ثبوت الأوسط له [خالًا؟] فهذا ما نعتقده هنا وامسامع العفية [؟] وامل زوال مانع العامة

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main reason for rejecting the transitivity of possibility?
A) The major premise is false.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is not unconditionally transitive; the essences of the terms must be considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Author's Resolution: Two Interpretations of the Major Premise
Resolution distinguishing two interpretations of the major premise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish two interpretations of the major premise in modal syllogisms and explain how they affect the validity of the conclusion.

Core Idea
The validity hinges on whether the major premise is about possibility or actuality of having the middle.

After the objection, the author provides a refined solution. He distinguishes two cases: either the minor actually has the middle, or it does not. In the second case, two interpretations of the major premise are possible. Understanding these interpretations clarifies when the conclusion truly follows.

Guided Path
1
Consider the first case
If the minor actually has the middle (the first case), then there is no problem: the conclusion follows straightforwardly. The doubt disappears.
The text: 'إن كان الأول كان الأصغر موصوفا بالفعل بالأوسط وحينئذ تزول الشبهة'.
2
Consider the second case (minor does not actually have the middle)
Here, we must examine what the major premise means. Two interpretations are offered.
3
Interpretation 1: The major applies to anything that could possibly have the middle
Under this reading, if the major says 'everything that it is possible that the middle is established for it has the major', and the minor shows that the middle is possible for the minor, then a possible scenario arises where the major is possible for the minor. But this only yields possibility, not actuality.
This is the custom of 'the objectors' (دأب القائلين).
4
Interpretation 2: The major applies only to things that actually have the middle
This interpretation (attributed to 'the Teacher') requires the minor to actually have the middle. If it does not, the conclusion does not follow. This interpretation avoids the problem but at the cost of restricting the syllogism's applicability.
The text: 'إن كانت له الأوسط فله الأكبر بالفعل على ما هو رأي الشيخ'.
5
Assess the resolution
The author seems to lean toward a conditional resolution: the establishment of the major for the minor is conditional upon the actual possession of the middle. If the middle is only potential, only a potential conclusion can be drawn.
The final lines indicate that the possibility of the major for the minor depends on the middle being actually established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific problem in modal syllogisms, specifically when the conclusion should be like the major premise but the minor term is not actually described by the middle term—it only potentially has it. The author first presents a proposed solution: because the major term is possible for the middle, and the middle is possible for the minor, the major is possible for the minor via 'possibility of possibility'—a concept the mind finds persuasive. An objector then rejects this, arguing that possibility is not transitive when the realities of the middle and minor differ; the major being possible for the middle does not guarantee it is possible for a different thing (the minor). Moreover, the objector clarifies that the major premise does not assert the major for the middle itself, but for subjects that have the middle. Therefore, without the minor actually having the middle, the conclusion does not follow. The author then refines the explanation by distinguishing two cases: either (1) the minor actually has the middle (in which case there is no problem), or (2) it does not. In the second case, two interpretations of the major premise are considered. The first interpretation (favored by objectors) is that the major applies to anything that could possibly have the middle; the second (attributed to 'the Teacher') is that the major applies only to things that actually have the middle. If the major is about possibility and the minor shows that the middle is possible for the minor, then a possible scenario arises where the major is possible for the minor—but this does not give actuality, only possibility. The author concludes that the establishment of the major for the minor may be conditional on the actual possession of the middle. The text ends with a statement of the author's own position, though some words remain unclear.

الأوسط (al-awsat)
الأكبر (al-akbar)
الإمكان (al-imkan)
المشروطة الخاصة (al-mashrutah al-khassah)
دأب الفائلين (the custom of the objectors)
رأي الشيخ (the opinion of the Teacher, i.e., Ibn Sina)

A) The major applies to anything that could possibly have the middle.
B) The major applies only to things that actually have the middle.
C) The major applies to things that are necessarily existent.
D) The major is not relevant to the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a modal syllogism depends on whether the major premise is interpreted as requiring actual or only possible possession of the middle by the subject.

Modality Transfer in Syllogisms: Customary and Conditional Premises
Author explains the conclusion when combining general customary and general conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of a syllogistic conclusion when combining general customary and general conditional premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion's modality follows the weaker premise: a general customary conclusion arises from customary and conditional premises.

In categorical syllogisms, the modality of the premises affects the modality of the conclusion. Al-Razi here examines a specific combination: a general customary minor premise and a general conditional major premise. He explains why the result is a general customary proposition, and why using a particular conditional major leaves the conclusion uncertain. Understanding this helps you evaluate arguments in Islamic theology where modalities matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify premises and their modalities
Locate the minor and major premises. Note that the minor is 'urfiyya 'amma (general customary) and the major is mashruta 'amma (general conditional). They are not simple categoricals but modal propositions.
General customary: predicate holds as long as subject exists in a certain state. General conditional: predicate holds whenever a condition is met, for all individuals.
2
Trace the dependency chain
The major term (P) is perpetual as long as the middle term (M) is perpetual. The middle term is perpetual as long as the description of the minor term (S) is perpetual. Thus P depends on the description of S.
This chain determines the modality propagation.
3
Check necessity transfer
Is the middle term's perpetuity necessary for the subject? No, it depends on the subject's description, not its essence. So the major's perpetuity is also not strictly necessary, only as long as the description holds.
Hence the conclusion cannot be necessary (daruriyya) but must be general customary.
4
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is: 'Every S is P as long as the description of S holds' – that is a general customary proposition.
The conclusion's modality matches the minor premise's modality, which is weaker than the major's.
5
Contrast with particular conditional major
If the major is particular conditional (mashruta khassa), meaning the condition applies only to some individuals, then the dependency chain is broken for all cases, leading to uncertainty (tawaqquf).
The conclusion cannot be drawn with any modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصغرى العرفية العامة مع الكبرى المشروطة العامة تنتج عرفية عامة، لأن الأكبر دائم بدوام الأوسط اللازم بدوام وصف الأصغر، فيلزم أن يكون الأكبر دائماً بدوام وصف الأصغر. وهذا إنما يكون في دوامه بدوام ذاته، فالنتيجة إذن عرفية عامة. وأما مع المشروطة الخاصة ففيه توقف.
The minor premise is a general customary proposition and the major is a general conditional proposition; they produce a general customary conclusion, because the major is perpetual with the perpetuity of the middle term which is necessary with the perpetuity of the description of the minor, so it follows that the major is perpetual with the perpetuity of the description of the minor. This is only in its perpetuity with the perpetuity of its essence, so the conclusion is therefore general customary. As for with the particular conditional, there is hesitation.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses the result of combining a general customary minor premise with a general conditional major premise in a syllogism. The author argues that the conclusion is a general customary proposition. The reasoning is that the major term is perpetual as long as the middle term is perpetual, and the middle term is necessary as long as the description of the minor term is perpetual (i.e., the middle term applies to the subject whenever the subject's description holds). Therefore, whenever the subject has that description, the major term holds perpetually. However, this perpetuity is based on the essence of the subject, not on a necessary connection, so the conclusion is general customary (i.e., it holds as long as the subject's description holds, but not necessarily). In contrast, if the major is a particular conditional (restricted to some individuals), the result is uncertain (tawaqquf). This analysis follows the modal logic of categorical syllogisms where the modalities of premises affect the modality of the conclusion.

Key Terms
العرفية العامة
المشروطة العامة
اللازم بدوام وصف الأصغر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) No conclusion (tawaqquf)

Key Takeaway
Combining a general customary minor and a general conditional major yields a general customary conclusion, reflecting the principle that the conclusion's modality follows the weaker premise.

Mixed Modal Syllogisms: The Common Conclusion in the First Figure
Rules for mixed modal syllogisms in the first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modal combinations in a first-figure categorical syllogism yield a conclusion and determine the modality of that conclusion.

Core Idea
Mixing modal premises requires careful transfer of necessity and persistence.

In Islamic logic, premises can carry different modal strengths: necessary (daruri), customary ('urfi), and conditional (mashruta). When we combine them in a first-figure syllogism, the conclusion's modality depends on the specific combination. This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas explains the rules for such mixed modal syllogisms, focusing on what he calls the 'common' (al-mushtarak) conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal types
Familiarise yourself with the three main modalities mentioned: necessary (daruri), customary ('urfi), and conditional (mashruta). Each has a general and particular form. The general customary ('urfi al-'amm) is true in most cases; the general conditional (mashruta al-'amma) is true as long as a condition holds for the middle term.
The 'common' (al-mushtarak) refers to the modality that results from mixing any two premises.
2
Recall the first figure structure
In the first figure, the middle term (al-awsat) is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. The major term (al-akbar) is predicate of the conclusion, and the minor term (al-asghar) is subject of the conclusion.
This structure is essential for understanding how modal properties transfer.
3
Apply the mixing rules
For each combination of minor and major premise modalities, use the author's rules: e.g., general customary minor with general conditional major yields no conclusion; particular customary minor with a necessary major yields a conclusion like the major; etc. The text provides these rules for the first figure.
The conclusion's modality is either necessary, customary, conditional, or existential (wujudiyya).
4
Understand why necessity may fail
The passage explains that when the minor is conditional (mashruta), the middle term holds only temporarily. Even if the major is necessary for the middle, that necessity may not transfer to the minor because the middle is not necessary for the minor. Hence the conclusion may be merely existential.
This is a key insight: modality does not simply transfer from premises to conclusion.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage discusses mixed modal syllogisms in the first figure, specifically the conclusions that arise when combining different types of premises. The author begins by stating that the 'common' (al-mushtarak) in such mixtures is the general customary proposition (al-'urfiya al-'amma). When mixed with a general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma), no conclusion is produced — this is because the major term must be necessary for the middle term due to the description of the minor, but the conditional premise does not guarantee that necessity in all cases. The passage then considers other combinations: particular customary and conditional, general customary, and particular conditional. For example, with a particular conditional minor and any major that is not necessary, the conclusion is existential (wujudiyya) rather than necessary, because the necessity of the major for the middle does not transfer to the minor due to the middle's contingency. The argument preserves the logical details of modality transfer, emphasizing that the conclusion's modality depends on the premises' modalities. The author uses technical terms like 'persistence' (dawam) and 'description' (wasf) to articulate the conditions for necessity.

Key Takeaway
In mixed modal syllogisms of the first figure, the conclusion's modality is determined by the weakest link: the minor premise's modality often restricts the transfer of necessity from the major.

Mixed Modal Syllogisms in the Second Figure: When the Conclusion is Necessary
Author states conditions for necessary conclusions in second figure mixed modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a necessary conclusion in second figure mixed modal syllogisms and apply them to a kalam argument.

Core Idea
In the second figure, necessity transfers to the conclusion under three conditions: one premise necessary, different quality, universal major.

Modal syllogisms mix premises of different modalities. When one premise is necessary and the other may be necessary, assertoric, or possible, the second figure can still yield a necessary conclusion if the premises differ in quality and the major premise is universal. This passage from al-Razi explains why. Let's walk through the three cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and conditions
The second figure has the middle term as predicate of both premises. The author adds two conditions: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal.
These conditions correspond to valid moods like Cesare and Camestres.
2
Consider the three possibilities for the other premise
If one premise is necessary, the other can be necessary, assertoric (not necessary), or possible. The author examines each case.
3
Trace necessity preservation in case 1 (both necessary)
When both premises are necessary, the predicate is necessarily affirmed of one term and necessarily denied of the other, creating a necessary opposition. The conclusion is necessary.
4
Trace case 2 (one necessary, one assertoric)
The necessity from the necessary premise overrides the assertoric premise, forcing a necessary conclusion. The assertoric premise cannot block necessity.
5
Trace case 3 (one necessary, one possible)
The possible premise's contingency does not affect the necessity arising from the necessary premise. The conclusion remains necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And by the success of God, the mixed [syllogisms] of the second figure, before the [?] place, there must be a premise: that this figure, whenever one of its premises is necessary, and then the other two conditions are present, meaning the difference in quality and the universality of the major, then the conclusion is necessary, inevitably, because one of the two premises if it is necessary, then the other may be necessary or not, or may be possible for it. If the first, then the predicate is affirmed for one of the two terms by [?] and acquired from the other by [?] and there is between the two terms a necessary opposition. If the second, then the necessity of the [?] for the [?] pure and safe from the necessary is necessary. And if it is so, then one of the two terms necessarily has the necessity of this predicate, and the other term necessarily does not have the necessity of this predicate, so this type returns in reality to the first, and the conclusion is necessary. And if the third, it is known that the first [?] which carries the necessity and [?] itself from being necessary or not, and if the conclusion is necessary in both cases, then it is also [?] the claim, or both premises are necessary.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for producing a necessary conclusion in mixed modal syllogisms of the second figure. The author begins by stating that when one premise is necessary and two other conditions are met—specifically, the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise is universal—then the conclusion is necessarily true. The reasoning is given: if one premise is necessary, the other premise could be necessary, not necessary, or merely possible relative to the first. In each case, the author shows that the conclusion ends up being necessary. For the first case (both necessary), the predicate is affirmed for one term and denied for the other, resulting in a necessary opposition. For the second case (one necessary, one not), the necessity from the necessary premise forces a necessary conclusion because the non-necessary premise cannot overcome the necessity. For the third case (one necessary, one possible), the possible premise's possibility does not affect the necessity arising from the necessary premise. Thus, in all scenarios, the conclusion is necessary. This demonstrates a key property of the second figure with modal premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second figure, what condition ensures a necessary conclusion when one premise is necessary?
A) Both premises are particular
B) The premises have the same quality
C) The premises differ in quality and the major is universal
D) The minor premise is universal

Key Takeaway
A necessary conclusion in second figure mixed modal syllogisms requires one necessary premise, difference in quality, and a universal major premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a second figure modal syllogism from kalam with one necessary premise and an assertoric premise, ensuring the conditions (different quality, universal major) are met. Then verify that the conclusion is necessary.
?
Analyze the following argument: 'Every act that causes harm is necessarily prohibited. Some contracts are not harmful. Therefore, some contracts are not prohibited.' Is this a valid second figure modal syllogism? Does it satisfy the conditions for a necessary conclusion? If not, why?
